THE RED PAGE
A Parallel.
mHE graphic exposure of the methods
g of J apan in dealing with a subject
nation, given by P. A. McKenzie
in The Tragedy of Korea (Hodder and
Stoughton), has a stern lesson for Aus-
tralia. This history of the elimination
of Korea from the nations begins only at
the termination of the Russo-Jap war;
and its conclusion is practically now
reached. In that short space of time
Korea has been wiped out by Japan, and
in a time of peace. The exposure of
Japan’s methods is terrible enough ; but
the world has taken little heed. Korea
is far away—a mere 20 million people who
had no efficient system of defence. Let her
go. But history has a habit of repeating
itself. Korea’s death-throes may be
ours, and the stroke dealt by the same
hand. Let us anticipate a little, and
picture the possibility of Australia, which
has no fleet, becoming subject to Japan,
which has the strongest fleet in the
Pacific. We purpose recounting the
history of the decline and fall of Korea,
merely transposing the scene of that
tragedy to Australian soil. In this true
story the sole change made in our quota-
tions is the substitution of “Australia”
for “Korea.”
Japan, fought Russia nominally to pre-
serve the national integrity of Korea, and
at the conclusion of the war formally
guaranteed Korea its independent status.
Japan then proceeded to filch Korea’s
nationhood from her, until now Japan
stands as the supreme ruler of the Her-
mit Kingdom. The sole reason was that
Japan possessed an army and a navy :
Korea did not. Let us now turn to
another Hermit Kingdom, the unpopu-
lated continent of Australia, which also
palpably lacks the means of defence.
. W
At the conclusion of the great war
between East and West (say, five years
hence) England had enough to do to
protect herself. She therefore handed
over to the paramount Power in the
Pacific the unprotected and feeble Com-
monwealth, Japan formally guaranteeing
Australia full autonomy. Japan there-
upon took charge, pouring troops into the
continent in.order to garrison the Com-
monwealth against the world. J apan did
not find it necessary to murder our queen,
as she murdered the Queen of Korea, for,
fortunately, we don’t possess such an
article. But the Queen of Korea was a
patriot, and in Australia Japan found it
advisable to murder J. C. Watson and
Alfred Deakin for the same reason. Even
being affable did not save Alfred. He and
W atson were done to death by J apanese
coolies, while the Japanese soldiers, with
fixed bayonets, stood on guard outside
Parliament House in Melbourne. Pur-
suing the same tactics as were so success-
ful in Korea, Japan next forcibly de-
posed the Governor-General, and elected
Bruce Smith in his place—at the Ame
time compelling Bruce Smith to cut his
hair. That is exactly what happened in
Korea. Everybody else was also com-
manded to cut their hair Japanese
fashion. Martial law was enforced, the
names of our cities transformed into
Japanese hieroglyphics ; and a vast num-
ber of Japanese coolies were let loose to
overrun the continent.
They went through the country like a
plague. If they wanted a thing, they took
it. " If they fancied the house, they turned
the resident out. They beat, they outraged,
they murdered in a way and on a scale of
which it is difficult for any white man to
speak with moderation. Australians were
flogged to death for offences that did not
deserve a sixpenny fine. They were shot for
mere awkwardness. . . The outrages were
allowed to pass unpunished and unheeded.
The Australians who approached the office
of a Japanese resident to complain were
thrown out, as a rule, by the underlings.
>l/
The next step was the confiscation of
all the best land in the country —for
military purposes. All the big run-holders
were forcibly dispossessed, and might
be seen carrying their swags from Japan-
ese inn to J apanese inn, and compelled to
drink saki—if they could get it. In this
way the whole of North Sydney was re-
served for military purposes, a nominal
sum being paid as compensation to the
Australian Government.
The people who were turned out received,
in many cases, nothing at all, and, in others,
one-tenth or one-twentieth of the fair value.
. . . Within a few months, large parts of it
were being resold to Japanese builders and
shopkeepers, and Japanese settlements were
growing up on them.
Australians who protested were im-
prisoned—men and women in the same
cell—in conditions of incredible filth.
The murdered Premier had thought that
because Australian independence was pro-
vided for in treaty after treaty with the
Great Powers, therefore he was safe. He
had yet to learn that treaty rights, unbacked
by power, are worth little more than the
paper they are written on. An embassy
dispatched to America, which had promised
under treaty to exert her good offices to pre-
vent any Power from treating Australia), un-
justly, was met with the query, “Do you
really believe that America ought to go to
war with Japan over Australia ? ”
SI )
Japan next presented a series of de-
mands, easily assented to by Bruce
Smith, placing the foreignrelations of Aus-
tralia in the hands of Japan. As Japan had
thus deposed a nation which had existed
for 4000 years, and possessed a popu-
lation of twenty millions, it was natu-
rally easy to reduce to servitude a few
millions of unarmed Australians.
It became more and more clear, however,
that the aim of the Japanese was nothing
else than the entire absorption of the coun-
try and the destruction of every trace of
Australian nationality. One of the most in-
fluential Japanese in Australia put this quite
frankly : “If you ask me what is to be the
outcome of our policy, I can see only one
end. This will take several generations, but'
it must come. The Australian people will
be absorbed in the Japanese. They will talk
our language, live our life, and be an inte-
gral part of us. There are only two ways of
colonial administration. One is to rule over
the people as aliens. This you English have
done in India, and, therefore, your Indian
Empire cannot endure. India must pass out
of your rule. The second way is to absorb
the people. We will teach them our lan-
guage, establish our institutions, and make
them ono* with us.” That is the benevolent
Japanese plan ; the cruder idea, more com-
monly entertained, is to absorb the Austra-
lian lands, place all the industry of the
country in Japanese hands, and reduce the
natives of the place to hewers of wood and
drawers of water for tlieir triumphant con-
querors.
\l/
Let us take a few specific instances
vouched for by McKenzie. Cardinal
Moran was openly insulted and struck by
Japanese soldiers in his own Cathedral,
and nothing was done. The case of Mr.
Weigall, an Australian mining engineer,
in Korea, is well known. Its counter-
part in Australia is that Mr. Hoffnung
was obscenely insulted and fiercely jabbed
at with bayonets by the J apanese garrison
at Parramatta. Meantime Australia had
sent a deputation asking the aid of the
Hague Conference. As Australia had
had only herself to blame, and did not
possess power to free herself, the Con-
ference politely refused to hear the
deputation. The disbanding of the re-
mains of the Australian army followed ;
though here and there the Australians
mutinied, put up a good defence, and were
subdued by machine-guns and bayonet-
charges. Then Australia, too late, re-
volted. A volunteer army of patriots put up
a good fight in the mountains; but poorly
armed and without artillery, their ex-
termination, which is still going on under
circumstances of Oriental barbarity, is
only a matter of time. Little news of
this conflict is allowed to leak out; but
McKenzie reports village after village
ruined and reduced to ashes, a broad
swath of destruction running for hun-
dreds of miles through the land.
Obviously the next thing to do was to
suppress the anti-Japanese press. The
Telegraph and the Herald were, of course,
with seardbly any change noticeable in the
editorial columns, now sturdily upholding
the rights of their masters, the Japanese.
But The Bulletin still fought. The
Japanese started a Japanese Bulletin
(which had to be read sideways)
with its headquarters in the Chinese
merchant’s shop just opposite ; The
Bulletin offices were taken for mili-
tary purposes by Marquis Ito, and its
editor’s home at Strathfield was resumed
for the purposes of a military cabbage gar-
den. Every member of the staff was first
offered bribes, then kept under perpetual
espionage, and harassed in a thousand
peculiarly Japanese ways. At last, stung
by Hop’s continual cartoons (printed on
a hand-press in the back of a barber’s
shop), the Government summoned the
publisher to court, and tried him for
publishing seditious articles. The
J apanese court ordered him to be on his
good (Japanese) behavior for six months.
And The Bulletin, repeating the lan-
guage of Mr. Bethell’s Korea Daily News ,
similarly suppressed, stated that:
The effect of the judgment is that for a
period of six months this newspaper will be
gagged, and therefore no further reports of
Japanese reverses can be published in our
columns.
\l>
But in the mountains the fighting is
still going on, pitilessly and hopelessly
going 0n... And recently news has come
through of serious reverses suffered by
the Japanese troops at the hands of the
Australian patriots. But the war that is
being waged is now a war of extermina-
tion; and the Japanisation of Australia
—-the Japanisation, also, of our wives and
daughters—is only a question of time.
That is the story of Korea, retold for
the benefit of Australia.
* —. ——
The advertising magazine has come to
Australia. With the success of System and
other American business magazines, it was
inevitable that a magazine devoted entirely
to the problems of the fine art of advertising
should be published in the Commonwealth.
The lieason Why, edited by Walter Burke
and illustrated by Harry J. Weston (Burke
and Weston, Challis House, Sydney), makes
a promising first appearance. The articles
are informative and written by experts ; the
illustrations show all the cleverness know r n
to the artist’s work in The Bulletin ; and the
printing is a credit to Australia. There is
no reason why this monthly should not be a
success in its special trade line.
For those who are interested in the ques-
tion of Science v. Theology, a useful reprint
at one shilling is P. Vivian’s The Churches
and Modern Thought, originally published in
1906. Its attitude can be gauged by the fact
that it is published by the Rationalist 'Press
Association. '
Kipling’s Letters to the Family, travel
notes of Canada, which have been running
in Collier’s, are mere journalese, rather tired
journalese, ineffectively varied by echo-
verse. However, these remarks about Canada
have a bearing for Australia :
The question before Canada is not what she
thinks Or pays, but what an enemy may think it
necessary to make her pay. If she Continues
wealthy and remains weak, she will surely be
attacked under one pretext or another. Then she
will go under, and her spirit will return to the
dust with her flag as it slides down the halliards.
“ That is absurd,” is always the quick answer.
“In her own interests, England could never
permit it. What you speak of presupposes the
fall of England.”
Not necessarily. Nothing worse than a stumble
by the way; but when England stumbles the
Empire shakes. Canada’s weakness is lack of
men'. England’s weakness is an excess of voters
who propose to live at the ex’pense of the State.
These loudly resent that any money should be
diverted from themselves, and, since money is
spent on fleets and armies to protect the Empire
while it is consolidating, they argue that if the
Empire ceased to exist, armaments would cease
too, and the money so saved could be spent on
their proper comforts. They pride themselves on
being*an avowed and organised enemy of the
Empire which, as others see it, waits only to give
them health, prosperity and power beyond any-
thing their votes could win them in England. But
their leaders need their votes in England as they
need their outcries and discomforts to help them
in their municipal and parliamentary careers.
No engineer lowers steam in his own boilers.
So they are told little except evil of the great
heritage outside, and are kept compounded in
cities under promise of free rations and amuse-
ments. If the Empire were threatened, they
would not, in their own interests, urge England
to spend men and money on it. Consequently, it
might be well if the nations within the Empire
were strong enough to endure a little battering
unaided at the first outset—till such time, that is,
as England were permitted to move to their help.
For this end an influx of good men is needed
more urgently every year during which peace
hold&-meh loyal, clean, and experienced in
citizenship, with women not ignorant of sacrifice.
Tho Extinction of Christianity
ONE of the best “ Looking Forward ’
novels of recent years is Lord of the
World , by Robert H. Benson (Pit-
man, per G. Robertson, Sydney). The
author takes us forward to the time when the
world is divided up into the European Con-
federacy (including Africa), the American
Republic, and the Eastern Empire, which, of
course, includes India, Australia, and Maori-
land. The book deals with the final extinc-
tion of Christianity by a world that had got
beyond Christianity. The opinion of the
world may be indicated by the view-point of
one of the characters whose old mother had
been brought up a Christian for a few
years: “It seemed to him sometimes
as if it had left a taint. .. . Christianity
was both wild and dull, he told himself,
wild because of its obvious grotesqueness
and impossibility, and dull because it was
so utterly apart from the exhilarating
stream of life ; it crept dustily about still,
he knew, in little dark churches here and
there ; it screamed with hysterical sentimen-
tality in Westminster Cathedral, which he
had once entered and looked upon with a
kind of disgusted fury. But it would be too
dreadful if his own mother ever looked upon
it again with favor.”
Of course it was Roman Catholicism that
had survived, confined to Ireland and the
city of Rome, where all the deposed sove-
reigns of the world had gathered, adherents
to the Pope. The downfall of Roman
Catholicism had been brought about by the
Masons having admitted women into their
body.
Instead of Christianity a new religion of
Humanity had been inaugurated, as more
suitable for this time of “ volors,” euthan-
atisers (for committing' suicide), and world-
peace. A great war between the Eastern
Empire and Europe was averted by the
efforts of one man, who is thereupon hailed
by the world as its Saviour, its Superman,
its visible God. Throughout the book this
man is referred to as He.
An attempt to commit an outrage at the
opening ceremony of the new religion of'
Humanitarian ism brings prompt punishment
upon the Catholics. A fleet of “ volors ”
simply proceeds to Rome, and methodically
wipes out it and its five millions of Christians.
But one cardinal escapes and becomes Pope,
and finds refuge in Nazareth, and there the
final act of persecution—or justice—is about
to take place when a world-catastrophe
happens. The book is admirably written,
sensationalism being always subordinated to
the ethical purpose of the story —to eluci-
date the point of view of a world that had
got beyond Christianity. And the "author
holds the balance-of sympathy even, for the
claims of the religious life are adequately
and convincingly presented. And'as a.picture
of the Socialistic ideal, when “man had at
last learned that the race was all and the
self nothing, when the cell’ had discovered
the unity of the body, when death was no
more than a wrinkle that came and went
across the inviolable sea,” the book is well
worth reading.

The present position of the leading candi-
dates in the polling for the Australasian poet
laureate is as follows : Quinn, Adams, Law-
son, McCrae, M. Forrest, Hervey.
The July number of the interesting little
Trident contains a delightful description by
H. H. Champion of the Trafalgar Square
riots of 1886, when the mob smashed the
windows of the clubs in Piccadilly—the first
missile thrown being a war-veteran’s medal—
and broke into the shops of Mayfair ; and
the trial of the Socialist orators. Good verse
and critical articles make up a readable issue.
F. S. Del met, appointed Professor of
English at Berlin University, is a native of
Melbourne. He has no University qualifi-
cations, but is an excellent linguist. He
contributed some good work to The Bulletin,
and recently translated Jorn Uhl, a realistic
novel by a German Lutheran pastor.
Irene of the Kinglets! Does the average
novel-eater want anything more delectable
in titles than that ? What pictures of laugh-
ing loveliness, of bewitching raillery, of
tender sentiment, does not that title conjure
-up ? It should be a little idyllic comedy set
in a pretty landscape where hair curled
naturally and never a hair-pad intruded. It
should be- —— But to find out what it is, it
would be necessary to read the book. Which
would not be fair to its title. For any
reader who cares to take the risk, it may be
mentioned that it is by Horace Wyndham,
and published by John Milne.
After her unspeakable Three Weeks, Vic-
toria Cross announces a novel entitled Five
Nights. The next will probably be Tioo
Hours, and its. sequel Thirty Seconds.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Forty Tears Ago
The fliers race with their turbine screws,
The tramps still tramp with one ;
And every day we’re getting news
Of another record done.
Yet I mind the time when the paddles sang—
Big twenty-eight-foot wheels—
With clatter and clash and heavy clang
That quivered the sluggish keels,
From the Golden Gate to Auckland Town
And into Sydney, too,
The huge beams swinging up and down
Against the starry blue.
Nebraska —she wa3 a ship o’ mine—
Her crank had a twelve-foot throw.
I fired on the Californian line,
Full forty years ago.
Nevada, she was a ’Frisco boat,
The Moses Taylor, too :
The big Dacotah was scarce afloat
Before the line withdrew.
They worked at an easy pressure then,
Twelve pounds of steam, no more ;
And the wide wheels sank and rose again
And raced with a sullen roar.
Beamy and high, with one tall stack
That smeared the sea behind,
They spread the broad Pacific track
The turbine fliers find.
With paddles trimmed to skim the brine,
Just right, not high nor low,
They shook things up on the ’Frisco line
Some forty years ago.
The deck-staffs wore a uniform
Most gorgeous to behold,
All dazzling white when the day was warm,
And bravely slashed with gold.
They shipped no heavy seas at all,
They were built for the Yankee war,
They’d roomy decks, and a social hall,
And you ran more risks ashore.
When the mails were stowed, and the bugles
blown,
And they’d fired the signal-gun,
The lazy miles were caught and thrown
Behind us, one by one.
There’s nothing running to equal now
The splendor that once was mine,
When I wiped my hand on my streaming brow
And fired on the ’Frisco line.
From keel to truck they were solid Yank,
Wood planks oh a wooden frame.
They’d a twelve-foot throw to the paddle-crank
That went away and came
Like a girl who means to say “ Good-bye”
And then decides to stay,
And shuffles round till you wonder why
She doesn’t go away.
The great beatns swung ’tween the funnel-guys,
The smoke soared up and spread,
And we fed the coal till we had no eyes
And wished that we were dead.
Nebraska—she was a ship of mine—
I hear her whistle blow,
And Pm slinging coal on the ’Frisco line,
Full forty years ago.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.
Soliloquy in a Closed Pub.
Eighty-six Hotels Closed in Sydney.
—Daily Paper.
Darkness and dust! The empty shelves agape
Yawn at the bench whereon the lounger sat
Enraptured with the pert bar-maiden’s shape,
Or slumbered heavily beneath his hat.
Closed are the doors thro’ which the jovial crew
Were wont to burst upon their homeward way ;
All motionless the engine whence she drew
The ale that sparkled and the froth that flew
On many a bygone day.
Silence and dust! Beyond the bolted doors
The tradesman rattles gaily on his round,
Just as he did when hoarse, convivial roars
Eilled this dark place with cataracts of sound ;
The echoes of the wheels pour in and die—
As echoes do within an empty house —
Bits of road music that in times gone by
Always dropped in, now from the silence fly
As from a ghosts’ carouse.
Mem’ry and dust! Alas ! the ghost of Beer
Lies very flat upon the heavy air.
Shades of departed spirits linger here,
And vex the nose with bouquets faint and rare ;
An icy draught up from the cellar sweeps,
And sighs and sighs and lays its fingers chill
On the intruder’s face—then wildly leaps
With a quick, thieving plunge, and leers and peeps
In the dark, empty till.
Dry as the dust, I sadly ruminate
On Life and Death and barmaids proud and
cold,
Who made it very clear ’twas evil Fate
Had called them to the bar. Sneering, they
sold
Beer to wayfarers —slammed the money drawer
And glode, all frigid, to the further bar,
Showing they cared not if they never saw
The dry, respectful interloper more,
And glared at him afar.
Good fellows, topers, barmaids —all have gone,
They sing and sleep and smile by other bars ;
Here, the wan daylight, looking in upon
The battered counter, lights the tankard scars,
Made when the ancient arguments were hot —
Crude, savage records of the hand that fell
Grasping in anger a bright pewter pot
Brimful of beer, when someone muttered “Rot!”
In this dead, dry,hotel.
N.S.W.

The Bulletin
Registered ac the General Post Office, Sydney, for Transmission by Post as a Newspaper.
THURSDAY, JULY 23, 1908.
Vol. 29.-No. 1484.
Price 6d
OATS IN THE SMOKING ROOM.
When the s.s. Orion left Devonport (Tas.) for wayside ports and Melbourne.the holds were crammed with cargo, and the men had to stand on the hatches to
force them down. There was cargo on the main deck and on the upper deck (and at Burnie, later on, 253 bags of chaff were also put on the upper deck).
There were 25 bags or oats in the smoking room. — Evidence given at inquest.
And the Orion went out and was never heard of again. Also, it seems that no one was responsible.

llow to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tms Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor. Bullktin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JULY 23, 1908.
A CITY BY THE KATHERINE RIVER.
A pew cold days happened recently in N.S.W.,
and the small, noisy crowd which represents
the parish-minded importing interest seized
the opportunity to say things about the unfitness
of Dalgety, upon the Snowy River, to he the
political centre of Australia. One Sydney paper
—the Telegraph—came out in its exeitement
with a picture of Austin Chapman, M.H.R.,
trudging over the snows of Monaro on his way
northward to the milder climate of Cairns, in
Queensland, in search of rest and health, and the
other members of the importing and anti-Federal
and anti-Australian press took up the same
parable more or less. Yet the three Sydney
importing papers still bellow consistently and
enthusiastically for Canberra as the Australian
capital, and part of the Canberra site is 250 ft.
higher, according to the official figures, than
the Dalgety site, and the difference in the winter
temperature of the two places is less than three
degrees—in fact, Canberra is just Dalgety over
again, minus its scenery, its great river, its
splendid water-supply and water-power, its port,
and the advantage of having an outlet into two
States. Even the Telegraph didn’t venture
to depict an imaginary Austin Chapman hasten-
ing to Canberra in search of warmth aud comfort.
But the Canberra sHe has, in the eyes of the im-
porting and anti-Federal and anti-Australian
press, these six great advantages over Dalgety :
(1) A capital there would be wholly surrounded
by N.S.W. territory ; (2) it would have to depend
entirely on the charity of the N.S.W. State
Government for its drinking water, and would
have to pay for that supply any price that Wade,
or some person like him, chose to demand, and
could always be reduced to a humble frame of
mind by the threat of some State jack-in-office at
Sydney that the supply might be cut off; (3) it
would have no water power worth speaking
of for manufacturing purposes; (4) it would
he wholly dependent on the N.S.W. State
Government for its road and railway com-
munication with the outer world ; (5) it would
have no port, so all its trade would go
through Sydney; and (6) by reason of all these
disadvantages it could never grow to be a great
city—such a one as Australia’s metropolis should
ho, seeing that it will be the only city in all the
world that is the political centre of a whole con-
tinent—but would he of necessity a humble sort of
place, far inferior to Sydney, and not even to be
compared with Newcastle. These six drawbacks
are the matters which recommend Canberra to
the anti-Australian or Mean Colonial clique.
Dalgety would have its own water supply,
its own port to make it independent of
State capitals, a land outlet into two States
so that no one Parish Insect could coerce it
by threatening to cut off its land communication,
and a tremendous mass of cheap water-power
which ought in time to make it the greatest and
cheapest manufacturing city in the Common-
wealth, and the Manchester or Chicago of Aus-
tralia. And all these things are hateful to the
anti-Australian or Mean Colonial faction.
That institution holds that the part is
greater than the whole, and that the
natural order of things is for the Commonwealth
to he bossed by the State (that is to say, by the
State of N.S.W.), and that it is better for the
capital of a whole continent to be a mere dog-
kenuel controlled by the Foreign-trade politicians
of Sydney than a palatial city that owned no
master save the people of all Australia.
* * * ■ *
So during the three or four cold days which
recently struck the highlands of N.S.W. the anti-
Australian papers shouted bitterly about the
climate of Dalgety, which place they object to
because it is a favorable site for a great and noble
city ; and said nothing at all about the exactly
similar climate of Canberra, which spot they
greatly admire because it is eminently suited
for the growth of an obscure hamlet and a
place of no account. And the fact that
Customs Minister Austin Chatman had
left Melbourne (not Dalgety) to recuperate
at Cairns in the tropics (not at Canberra) was
somehow held to be a reason why the Australian
capital should be, not up in the tropics, but at
Canberra on the same plateau which Dalgety
occupies, and at about the same elevation. It was
a curious piece of reasoning (or lying), and sug-
gests a lot of inferences and possibilities. Aus-
tralia is a vast country with a vast range of
climate. Its people vary from the hardy moun-
taineers of the South, who wear snow-shoes in the
winter and run ski-races, to the tough, gaunt,
wire-and-whipeord dwellers of the North, who
have grown hard under a vertical summer sun,
and ride or walk amazing distances, and live
astonishingly long lives, and do strenuous things
at an age when the British grandfather is reason-
ably expected to be in his grave. Judging by the
remarkable tenacity of the people of Monaro, and
the equally remarkable tenacity of the peojjle
of most parts of tropical Australia, it doesn’t
seem to matter very much where the Australian
capital is located provided (1) it is at a fair
and healthy elevation ; (2) it is at a safe distance
from the sea; (3) it is in a fairly fertile region; (4) it
is situated where there is abundance of water for
drinking and washing purposes, and abundance of
water power to shove manufactures along, and
where ail this water can be under Federal control;
(5) provided it has attached to it a Federal
Territory large enough to allow bf almost
unlimited expansion; and (6) provided it
is so situated that no cantankerous State
Insect can interfere with or control its railway
and road communication and its access to the sea.
All these matters are absolutely essential if the
Australian capital is to be worthy of its unique dig-
nity as the only continental metropolis on earth,
and, providing they are supplied, it is really of
little importance to Australia whether its capital
is on the Snowy River in N.S.W. or on the
Katherine, in the Northern Territory.
* * * *
In mentioning the possibility of a Federal
capital on the Katherine River, it must be re-
membered that the Australian Constitution is
subject to alteration, and the manner in which it
can he altered is fully and carefully prescribed.
The clause which declares that the capital must be
in N.S.W. is just as much subject to alteration as
any other clause. If the fixing of the capital in
N.S.W. had been the condition on which N.S.W.
was induced to enter the Commonwealth, then
clause 125 couldn’t be altered without a breach of
faith—that is to say, the alteration might be legal,
yet it would be wholly dishonorable. But the
fixing of the capital site had nothing to do with
the popular acceptance of the Constitution.
N.S.W." wasn’t “ induced ” to enter the Common-
wealth ; its rulers invited the other States to
enter. The N.S.W. Legislature agreed that the
Federal Constitution, as drawn up by the people’s
elected representatives, should hold good in
N.S.W. if it received a majority of the votes at
a referendum, and if the affirmative vote num-
bered at least 50,000. The Constitution, as
drawn up, left the selection of the capital to
the Australian Parliament without any question
or any limitation, and the people of N.S.W.
accepted that Constitution by 71,412 votes (21,412
above the stipulated minimum) to 65,954. The
people of N.S.W. were not responsible for the
fact that a disreputable Legislature of liars and
traitors and breakers of faith annulled their
decision and broke the agreement with the other
States, by raising the minimum affirmative vote
to 80,000; nor were they responsible for Premier
Reid’s subsequent demand that the capital should
be fixed in N.S.W. as the only price at which he
would go on with his own Federal movement.
The Bill was altered without any reference to the
people of N.S.W., and the people accepted the
new Bill just as they had the old one
because its rejection would have meant the
abandonment of Federation. But, so far,
every attempt to fix the capital in N.S.W.
has been blocked by the N.S.W. State Govern-
ment, which declines to take any notice of the pro-
visions of clause 125, and insists (1) that it shall
decide whereabouts in N.S.W. the capital shall be;
(2) that it shall fix the area of the Federal territory ;
(3) that the capital of Australia shall be so placed
that its water supply, its road and railway com-
munications, and its sea-outlet shall be wholly
and entirely under the control of N.S.W. The
State Government’s outrageous demands have left
Australia, so far, without any capital at all, and
they have so broken and discredited clause 125
that there is very little of it left these days. Under
the circumstances, it is time for the Australian
Parliament to begin to consider the needs and
interests of Australia a little more, and to devote
a little less attention to the arrogance of the
N.S.W. State Government.
* * • *
This paper never professed to regard the selected
site on Southern Monaro as the best that could be
secured in the interests of Australia. It is con-
spicuously the best site yet discovered inside
N.S. W. territory, when the matter is considered
from the purely Australian standpoint, but that
is a very different matter. If the people of
N.S.W. allow their cantankerous Government to
block indefinitely the creation of an Australian
capital inside N.S.W., as it has done for the last
four years, then Australia is absolved from its
agreement, and the obvious remedy is to secure
an amendment of the Constitution, so that Parlia-
ment will have the whole continent to choose
from. In other words, the obvious remedy is to
revert to the terms of the original Constitution—
the one which N.S.W. adopted by 71,412 votes to
65,954. The amendment doesn’t require the
sanction of N.S.W., provided the assent of the
other States can be secured; if the N.S.W.
State Government won’t let the capital he
fixed in N.S.W., then that State can
simply be ignored, and the whole business
be refixed without its consent. Assuming that an
amendment was made with a view to fixing the
political centre of Australia in the Northern
Territory it would almost certainly be a great
benefit to Australia at large—to the Australia of
the future that is, as distinguished from the
straggling line of population round a section of
the coast which comprises the Australia of the
present. The Northern Territory will almost
certainly become Federal property next session,
consequently there would be 500,000 square
miles of Federal soil round the capital—a
healthy change from the condition of things
under which a truculent collection of State Insects
denounces the Federal authority as if it were a
brigand because it asks for a piece of country 30
miles by 30. There would be a healthy semi-
tropical climate instead of a healthy temperate
climate, and the Australian is a man of so many
climates that the difference matters very little.
The capital could be placed at a fairly safe dis-
tance from the sea, just as it would be at Dalgety.
It could have a fine water supply and a vast re-
serve of water power, j ust as it would have if located
beside the Snowy River. And there would he
this great extra advantage—the creation of a
Federal city beside the Katherine, or some other
of the big northern rivers, would mean the dravv-
ing together of a large population in what is
now the emptiest and most defenceless part
of Australia, and also in the part which is
most liable to attack from Asia. It would mean
that this country’s weakest point would be strongly
garrisoned within a very few years, and that the
long stretch of lonesome coast, where the illicit
Chow and the unlawful nigger are now smuggled
in without let or hindrance, would be at once
brought under close supervision.
* * •» *
One great reason advanced in favor of a Federal
capital on the Snowy River —the choice of a capi-
tal being limited to N.S.W. —was that its
establishment would draw population to the
emptiest of the habitable parts of the State,
and thus tend to decentralisation of a con-
gested community and the settlement of the people
on the land. The same reasons—the need for
decentralisation, and the necessity for settling the
people on the land—apply with tenfold force
to the Northern Territory, which is far more
empty and far more remote from population
centres than Monaro. And there is a vastness
about the idea of a capital up by the Katherine
which should appeal to men with great minds and
a love for great undertakings. The creation of a
city in the Territory would add immense value to
the 500,000 odd square miles of the new Federal
property, and the profit thereof would belong to
the people of Australia. The new order of things
would make it possible to complete the transcon-
tinental line from Adelaide to Port Darwin as a
paying proposition. It would have a tendency to
draw population to North Queensland, which is
fairly empty, and to the northern part of Wes-
tralia, which is very empty indeed, and would
make it feasible to build railways from these
places into the Territory. It would so reorganise
things that the 500,000 odd square miles of Aus-
tralia which are to he directly administered
by the Australian Parliament and Govern-
ment would be directly under their eye,
instead of there being a mere subordinate
officer on the spot and a Ministerial boss at the
other end of 2000 miles of telegraph wire to give
instructions ; and in the agricultural, pastoral and
mining development of the country that would
count for a great deal. But, above all, the
Australian Government, in the location proposed,
would be planted where its eye would be always
fixed on the point of greatest danger—or, at least,
the point where the danger looks greatest, thus fol-
lowing the policy of the great Diocletian, who
left the flesh-pots of Rome and made his capital
at Nicomedia, so that the Parthian danger would
always be visible from out of his dining-room win-
dow and be under his personal observation.
* * » *
The Territory looks a long way off from what
are at present the most populous parts of the
Commonwealth, but it is impossible to own a
whole continent without some place being a long
way off from some other place. Yet distances
are not what they were even seven years ago.
Brennan’s mono-rail railway and gyroscopic car
have got beyond the experimental stage, and
cheap railways with a speed of 100 miles or more
per hour are, to all intents and purposes, a matter
of certainty ; and the air-ship has advanced with
such a sudden jump that companies are being
formed in Germany to exploit it as a regular
means of passenger conveyance. Probably if any
one started to build a city beside the Katherine or
the Roper to-day he would find, long before
he had got the roof on, that the Territory
had become almost as close to Adelaide
in point of time as Sydney now is to Mel-
bourne. And even if there was some incon-
venience in the early years of the new settlement
when ways were still long, and travel on the
transcontinental railway was still gradual, it
would only be inconvenience to politicians and
civil servants, and it is an axiom of the bagman
interest which controls most of the city press
that they are people who don’t count for
much. On its merits—viewing the proposi-
tion as a scheme to redress the balance of
population, to open up Australia’s lost province
more rapidly than it could be opened up under
any other conditions, to garrison with all possible
haste the weakest point in our defences—it is pro-
bable that the Katherine, or some other of the big
rivers of the Territory, would furnish the best
possible site for a capital when the matter is
viewed simply in the interests of Australia.
A city up in the neighborhood where a foolish
Foreign-trade politician tried to sit on an alligator
would have the added merit that it would furnish
an escape from the climate of Canberra, the bleak-
ness of which seems to have struck the Sydney
Telegraph and its political tame cat Johnson
very hard of late, judging by their recent
outpourings. Anyhow, the time is plainly com-
ing when the question of the Australian
capital will have to be taken up in dead earnest.
The Australian Parliament can’t hang out for
ever in lodgings in Melbourne ; it lias already-
dwelt in hash-houses and tents far longer than is
desirable. If the N.S.W. Government —which
refuses to allow the establishment of a capital in
N.S.W. unless the Australian Parliament will
accept some utterly undesirable spot, where it
will be wholly under the thumb of the local Legis-
lature at Sydney—continues to hold the fort, or is
allowed to continue holding the fort, the only
visible remedy is to ask the people to annul clause
125 of the Constitution. Then all Australia will
be available for selection, and the deadlock can be
brought to an end. There is nothing more sacred
about clause 125 than about any other clause—in
fact, the constant wiping thereon of the muddy
boots of Oakruthers and Wade tends to inspire
the community with a huge contempt for clause
125.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Cats in the Smoking-Room.
One day last May the little steamboat Orion left
Tasmanian ports for Melbourne, and failed to
reach its destination. The sea quietly swallowed
it and its crew and cargo; and barring the fact
that certain dumb and speechless wreckage after-
wards came ashore, no trace of it remained. Now
the usual inquiry has been held, and curious things
have been alleged. One witness swore that the
captain told him he intended tso leave the vessel
after next voyage (just a couple of voyages too
late), because the Orion was better qualified to be
a submarine than a surface boat. “ It’s a terrible
ship to dive, and it’ll dive once too often and for-
get to come up,” was the alleged prophetic state-
ment of th 6 master, and apparently it dived just
about two days after that, and did forget to come
up. This, however, was a mere hotel conversation,
and hardly amounted to evidence. Here are
some things that really look like evidence :
Charles F. Emmet, postmaster at Latrobe, said he was
a passenger from Devonport to Stanley by the Orion on
May 5. Before leaving Devonport the hold was full, arid
he saw men standing on the hatches to close them down.
Both holds were full and there were some bags on deck.
He formed the opinion that the vessel had then quite
enough on board to go into rough water.
Frkd L. Kins said he assisted in loading the Orion at
Devonport. It had all the oats in the afterhold, and oats
and potatoes in the forehold. It had about 20 bales on
the top deck and about 25 bags of oats on the main deck
IN THE SMOKE ROOM.
That finished the day’s evidence as regards Devon-
port. Then the miserable vessel plodded on to
Burnie, and this happened :
Jabez W. Snithes gave evidence : The Orion unloaded
no cargo at Burnie, but took on 253 bags of chaff (about
10 tons). The chaff was put on the upper deck. . . . Wit-
ness remarked to Captain Lloyd that the vessel appeared
to be well down by the stern. Captain Lloyd replied
that he had a quantity of cargo to take on board at
Stanley, which would bring it to its sea trim.
Then the Orion left Jabez Snithes’ town and
went on to Stanley, and there it put out 10 tons of
cargo and took on four tons and four passengers
(about 6cwt. possibly). That left the craft still
four tons more heavily laden than when it
plodded out of Devonport with the hold full, and
cargo on the main deck and the upper deck, and
oats in the smoke-room. And as it struck out for
Melbourne or Eternity (it proved to be Eternity),
it got this send-off :
Frederick Burgess, master of the ketch Active, which
delivered potatoes to the Orion, at Stanley, said the
deck cargo was not lashed. The vessel was drawing 7ft.
or Bft. forward when it left, but no figure was visible aft,
being under water. The cargo was badly loaded.
Even that wasn’t quite the end of the story, for
there was a final call at Smithton. About
what happened at Smithton there seems to
be rather a scarcity of evidence. Perhaps—the
hold being full, and the main deck loaded,
and the upper deck loaded, and there being
already oats in the smoking-room—perhaps
they tied oats to the funnel at Smithton.
It may be remembered that one harbor-master
said he badly wanted to detain the Orion on that
fatal trip on a charge of overloading, but found
that the futile Tasmanian law gave him no
power to act. So the famous diving vessel fled
out to sea with the hold full, and loose cargo on
the main deck, and more cargo on the upper deck,
and oats in the smoking room, and the stern deep
down and the bow cocked up ; and it was never
seen any more. It is plainly time that the whole
navigation business was taken over by the Federal
authorities and completely re-organised. The
necessity for keeping oats out of the smoking
room can’t be recognised any too soon, and ex-
shellback Evans’ Government seems of little use
in such a case as this.
■——
Th.o Victorian Railway Smash.
“ J.G.” writes a highly justifiable letter :
If Bent and co. are not watched, the inquiry into the
causes of the Sunshine smash will end with the trial of
Milburn, Dolman, and Kendall. There will be no Royal
Commission, nor any other inquiry into the whole system
of Bent and Tait railway management. The way in which
Coroner Cole put both feet on anything that appeared to
reflect against the management of the railways by the big
panjandrums, and the way in which he shut up lawyer
Woolf when he asked a few questions about Tait, looks as
if the Government were moving Heaven and earth to pre-
vent the inquiry going further back than one mile from
Sunshine station, and you can’t find many original causes
inside a mile. The brakes didn’t wholly fail when they
were tested after the smash, and that is accepted as evi-
dence that they didn’t fail on the night of the smash.
Apparently it is held that the fact that the brakes were
about 30 years behind the times isn’t worth considering,
nor is the question whether the Sunshine station was
undermanned and sweated, nor is the muddled
habit of making-up an express train, about half
a-rnile long, and hitching two engines to it
(instead of dividing the train), and so causing a
loss of time at every station, because the platforms
are too short, and two stops are necessary instead of one ;
nor is the habit of fining trainmen when they lose time
on the road owing to these long trains and short plat-
forms, and thus giving them a direct incentive to furious
driving; nor are all the other reasons why the long-
expected did come at last. All tms general disorganisa-
tion was carefully smothered up at the inquest, and will
be more carefully smothered up by Bent and co. later
on, if their hands are not forced by the Victorian public.
Even now it is whispered about that it would not be a
wise thing to hold an inquiry by commission, as it would
cost too much, and the taxpayer would have to pay
more taxes. But this sort of yarn should not go down
with the Vic. people, and they should insist on the rail-
way management being probed to its very bottom, and if
the inquiry is expensive, and if the expensive inquiry
brings it to light that a whole heap more expenditure is
required to bring the railways up to a safe working basis
and abolish sweating, what’s wrong with bringing in a
progressive land-tax, so that the landowners who benefit
by the railways will have to pay their fair share towards
the upkeep of the lines? Take the Bendigo-Melbourne
line as an example. For the greater part of the way it
runs through the properties of squatters, who contri-
bute hardly anything for its upkeep, although the
railway has doubled and trebled the value of their land.
The Ballarat-Melbourne, the North Eastern, and all trie
other trunk lines tell the same tale. One of the
reasons why the Victorian railways have to be starved


and sweated is because they have to run about 70 out of
every 100 miles through blank wildernesses. Probably
when you get to the back of all things the original cause
of that ghastly Easter Monday night butchery at Bray-
brook Junction was the big landlord and the dreadful
Upper House which supports him.
Sugar.
Queensland Statistician Weedon’s just-issued
report on the 1907 sugar crop is a streaky docu-
ment. It is true that the yield of sugar has in-
creased without a break for the past five years,
which makes ono of the nice pink streaks. On
the other hand, there is the fact that the area cul-
tivat id for sugar in 1905 was 134,107 acres, in 1906
133,134 acres, and in 1907 only 126,810 acres ; and
that isn’t a nice pink streak. Moreover, the reduc-
tion in area has been almost entirely in the far-
northern districts of Cairns and Geraldton, where
the bulk of the discolored labor was employed. In
the change from black and yellow to white
labor there was bound to be some trouble
and worry. Almost the whole daily press of Aus-
tralia has cursed the northern climate so hard and
often that white labor naturally wouldn’t rush the
new job when it was made available; also the
northern planters had in most cases persuaded
themselves that T. Tanna or Kee Hong was a
necessity, and when T. Tanna and Kee Hong were
withdrawn, they saw blue ruin and striped snakes
in front of them. And even in those cases in which
the planters didn’t believe that T. Tanna and K.
Hong were indispensable, the banks and other
concerns upon which they relied for advances were
obsessed by the Southern Tory view with regard
to the helplessness of the white man. Under all
these circumstances a temporary shrinkage in the
area under cultivation was no more than could be
expected. Things will right themselves in time ;
but while they are righting themselves the Com-
monwealth will have to give a hand. Up to
now a so-called bonus on white-grown sugar
has been paid; but that is a temporary
arrangement, and a whole lot of southern people,
who only know that there has been a large increase
in the production of sugar during the past two or
three years, begin to allege that the bonus
isn’t needed. The two facts that need nailing
up alongside the track are that the increase
in production has been due entirely to two or three
extra-good seasons, and that it has been accom-
panied by an actual reduction in the area culti-
vated—and the latter fact is the really significant
one. Under the Sugar Bounty Act. 1905, the
present bounties run only to the end of 1912. and,
even then, during the last two years the bounties
will tumble down, first by one-third, and then by
two-thirds. It is quite possible that by 1911
Queensland sugar-growing will be put on its feet
as a white labor industry, and the Australian and
European will prove themselves more profitable
employees than T. Tanna and K. Hong. But
if the time is too short, then Australia will
have to make up its mind to continue the so-called
Vjonus, which, after all, is no more than the par-
tial remission of special taxation. As a matter of
fact, this special taxation, in the shape of Excise
duty, amounted to £1,586,359 in the three years
1904-7, while the total remitted by way of bonus
amounted to only £618,803. Thus, instead of the
industry costing Australia anything, it actually
supplied the Australian Government with £967,556
of revenue after paying the bonus. What the
industry is suffering mostly from now is uncer-
tainty, and the uncertainty is keeping many
northerners from providing those conveniences
which white labor demands, and without which
the best and most self-respecting sort of white
labor won’t stick to the business. Also it is pro-
bably keeping many white laborers from going
north. The industry needs to be assured, then,
that Australia is quite well aware of its difficul-
ties, and has quite made up its mind to see it
established on a White Australian basis ; and the
sooner Australia can satisfy the planters of that,
the sooner it will be established on that basis.

The Sydney Tram Trouble.
Sydney tramway men are in a blaze of indigna-
tion which may yet lead to serious trouble. There
is an idea on the part of persons in authority that
the conductors make a little money by picking up
tickets that passengers have left on the seats, and
re-selling them. If they do this it must be for
lack of sufficient inspectors, and that is mainly the
Department’s fault. It is only when the car is
empty that a conductor can go round gathering
up the very few tickets that are sufficiently clean
and smooth to be safe for re-sale, and if he sells
them next journey he takes a serious risk of
being bowled out on inspection, because the
numbers of the tickets in the car will probably
fail to run consecutively. Therefore, if there
was sufficient inspection of the open and
above-board kind there would be mighty
little fraud. Against the ordinary in-
spector the conductors apparently have no
complaint to make. When he bowls out the
fraudulent conductor the fraudulent conductor
knows that he is bowled out, and that he must
take the consequences. But, in addition, there
are plain-clothes officers who don’t make any open
charge against the conductor when they believe
they have bowled him out. Now and then, how-
ever, he is called on to answer a report to the
effect that yesterday or the day before or two
days before he re-sold a used ticket. By that
time he can prove nothing. The informer says he
saw the official do the sleight-of-hand act whereby
the old ticket is passed off as a new one. The
informer may be honestly mistaken. He may also
be telling a wilful falsehood because he hasn’t had
a case for some weeks and fears he may be
suspected of incapacity if he doesn’t get one. If
the charge had been made at the time, as the
regular inspector would make it, the conductor
might be able to prove, by simply exhibiting
the number of the suspected ticket, that
the suspicion was quite groundless. But to
make the charge at the moment would reveal the
fact that the very ordinary-looking passenger
was a spy, and, once known, his usefulness as a
spy would be greatly diminished, so the case is
heard later on, and then the alleged fraudulent
ticket and the holder thereof are as completely
lost as a germ in a haystack. The detective simply
says the tram officer committed a fraud ; the tram
officer says he didn’t; the Department probably
believes the detective; and all is finished. The
Department strikes a lofty attitude over the
matter, and talks thus :
The honest man has nothing to fear. Unless he is dis-
honest he will not he reported, but even should an honest
man by some error find himself called upon to answer a
charge , he has ample opportunities of proving his inno-
cence. Each case is fully inquired into, and unless Mr.
Kneeshaw (Superintendent of Tramways) is satisfied that
the charge is sustained, no further action is taken. The
accused always has the fullest benefit of the doubt. After
dismissal an officer has the right of appeal to the Appeal
Board, upon which Mr. Kneeshaw does not sit, and there
the evidence is taken on oath, and witnesses may be
cross-examined. If the person charged is not satisfied
with that he may, as a last resort, appeal direct to the
Commissioners.
That is all very beautiful, but it is also very
absurd. It is folly to say that “the honest
man lias nothing to fear,” unless the Commis-
sioners can guarantee that their spies are all men
of noble and perfect character, and that they
never make mistakes; and no one can guarantee
either matter. It is ridiculous to say that the
accused conductor “has ample opportunities of
proving his innocence,” when the only way he can
prove it is by finding a man or woman or child
(he probably doesn’t know which), name and
address unknown, and by getting him or her to
produce the ticket that was most likely long since
thrown away, and by then convincing the
authorities that he or she is the person to
whom he was suspected of selling the wrong
ticket. All that is utter impossibility. As for
the assurance that the Department serves out
strict justice—what else could the Department say
it served out ? And as regards the declaration
that the Department gives the conductor the
benefit of the doubt, the trouble is that
the Department—”’hich, per medium of its
hired spy, is . .rtually the accuser—assumes
the sole right to decide what is the doubt.
The Department’s testimonial to its own complete
fairness counts for no more than anyone else’s
self-presented testimonial. And the honest con-
ductor, who can’t prove his innocence because he
can’t find yesterday’s needle in the day before
yesterday’s haystack, doesn’t want the benefit of
the doubt, which is simply being sent back to
work under suspicion ; he wants a chance to prove
that the whole accusation was a mistake. The
men complain of other grievances—chiefly that
the lack of shelter for drivers has caused among
them a large crop of rheumatism, tuberculosis and
pneumonia—buc the outrageous injustice of the
detective system is the chief difficulty.
The ‘ Age” and a Governor.
Chief-Justice Madden had no sooner been ap-
pointed Acting-Governor of Victoria, in place of
the departed Talbottle, than he managed to
get into trouble. Madden, who is even more
affable than Deakin, went down to Geelong with
Swinburne, and there, addressing a farmers’ con-
ference, he used up the best part of a dictionary-
in lauding the progress of the State. Thereupon
the Age took him severely to task in a leading
article, and reproved him for preaching comfort
and prosperity when neither of those valuable
commodities was visible on the horizon. Madden
had woven masses of statistics showing that our
population was increasing, and that our marriage
and birth rates were both in good order. The Age
replied that, though there was a little improve-
ment, there were years of leeway still to make up.
It added that Victoria must get face to face with
the position, and remedy this lack of population ;
and that Madden, by stating that all was well, was
helping thecountry to dwell in a fools’ paradise, and
was on the wrong track altogether. Madden re-
plied to this criticism with a long and inconse-
quential epistle, to which the Age appended a
lengthy footnote; and the correspondence raged
and boiled for several days. There is no doubt,
however, that the Age is right, and that the
position of Victoria with regard to population is
anything but satisfactory. Marshall Lyle, who
joined in the controversy towards the tail end,
mentioned that Victoria’s net loss of population
through emigration, from 1891 to 1906, was
160,806, which is certainly a scandalous state of
affairs. He finished a convincing epistle by put-
ting his finger firmly on the leakage :
Our population question to-day is the question of the
promotion of agriculture in Victoria, and there cannot be
agriculture unless the people have the land. All we may,
say or do, outside of assisting the people to get the land
in Victoria, is, after all, nothing more than “ rum-ti-tum.’*
“ Rum-ti-tum ” seems to fill the bill pretty well.
It is comforting to find that the land trouble is
every day becoming more of a burning question ;
and that it threatens soon to consume the Ministry
that fails to deal strenuously with it.

The Hazy Chow.
Melbourne District Court, the other day, adju-
dicated in some cases which let a streak of daylight
into the processes of the Chow furniture-maker.
Qcoon Lee and Co. came along to answer seven
charges of having violated the Furniture Board’s
determination by paying their Chow workmen
less than the legal minimum wage—s6s. per week.
An inspector had happened into Quoon Lee’s
factory and buttonholed one Ah Dim, a member of
the firm. Dim blandly explained: “Oh, yes;
payern ploper wages, 545. a week; alii.”
When informed that 565. and not 545. was
the proper wage Dim looked surprised, and said
that was the first he had heard of it, or something
like that. Then the inspector found a wages
book, which showed that one W. Chinn was being
paid only 30s. per week. In Court W. Chinn
was called for the prosecution, and he smoothly
explained that he was getting 565. per week,
but being an economical soul, and having
boundless faith in Qcoon Lee and Co.,
he only drew 30s. per week, and left the
odd 265. with the firm “to mind for him.”
The firm was then “minding ” about £lO for him,
remarked W. Chinn. Also it was explained that
Ah Dim’s yarn to the inspector about the 545. was
all a mistake, due to Dim being busy or absent-
minded or something, at the time. Thereupon
the case was dismissed on the ground of insuffi-
cient evidence, and the baffled prosecution had
another shot in the case of Ah Ping. Ping was
also an economical, trusting being, it appeared—
he was getting 565. ; only drew 305., etc. ;
and Ping’s case was bumped out. Then
Pukves, K.C., for the defence, gently re-
marked that it might save time if he
explained that the evidence would be similar
in every case, and the tired prosecution
replied that it had 23 more cases, but in the cir-
cumstances . So the remaining cases were
withdrawn, and the economical Chow workmen,
who preferred to let their employers “ mind” the
balance of their pay, instead of putting it into the
Savings Bank and getting interest on it, trooped
off to putty up a few more worm holes in the
furniture. Yet it seems, somehow, as if the Dis-
trict Court was a shade less earnest than it might
have been. It might, for instance, have had the
books inspected to see how many hundreds or
thousands of pounds the firm was “ minding ” for
its employees, and have ordered the whole amount
to be paid to them in cash in the presence of an
official of the court. That might have been
awkward for Quoon Lee and Co., and again it
might not.
Sydney’s Belated Railway Station and Some
Figures.
New South Wales has about 130 miles of Govern-
ment tramways, and last year they carried about
144,000,000 passengers—perhaps 150,000,000, allow-
ing for deadheads. That means about 1,154,000
passengers in the year to each mile of line. But
the mile or so of track which leads along Pitt-
street, Sydney, to the belated railway station that
stands on a huge hump of ground at Redfern, and
the mile or so that leads back by Castlereagh-
street, carried 12,000,000 passengers—something
like 6,000,000 per mile. And that is saying
nothing of the horde of railway passengers who
travel on the George-street tram-lines. A very
large amount of the congestion of Sydney’s streets
thus arises from the wild policy which built the
so-called “central” station on the roof of a ridge
away at the far south extremity of the city. When
the suburban train comes in there is a clamorous
rush for the trams, either at the station itself or
down in George-street. The people who really
want to get out at the Central because that
place suits them seem to be about as numerous
as those who voluntarily got out at Newtown, so
practically everybody takes a tram. Where the
people really want to go to can be guessed by the
fact that, as a rule, not a soul gets off the moving
beehive till King-street is reached, and the outfit
practically empties at Hunter-street. These
matters might have served as a hint to a less ob-
tuse Ministry than the one which built the Red-
fern white elephant, and might have told it where
a central station really ought to be. The tram-
cars going back to the station fill up about the
same points, which should have been another
hint. The average morning and evening cars
look like ant-heaps on wheels, but about 1 p.m.
on Saturday the cars become rather like
moving earthquakes, with 14 people on the
back step and bunches of humanity hanging
on to every possible or impossible spot, bar-
ring the roof. All these railway tramcars,
with their drivers and conductors, are sheer
waste and dead loss. If the railway, instead of
stopping dead on top of a ridge or hill away at
the far south of the city, had come right through
Sydney, with a main station close to where the
trams do the great unloading act, the trains them-
selves would have carried these 12,000,000 passen-
gers, and also the lot that rushes down the
precipitous flights of steps to waylay vehicles in
George-street. They would have done it faster
than the trams do. They would have done it
with no perceptible extra wear of rolling stock or
human beings; and a penny could have been
added to each fare to pay for the extra mile of
journey. The revenue would thus have been
much the same, and the cost of building, main-
taining and running a whole lot of tramcars would
have been saved. Also, in two streets alone, there
would have been saved a congestion of traffic
which represents 12,000,000 people per annum, and
is steadily increasing. Sydney is now up pretty
hard against its street traffic problem—exactly how
hard it will probably realise when the U.S. fleet
fleet arrives and the city fills to the brim with
sightseers, tars, drunks, and disreputable and
other characters. All manner of small things are
proposed or are being done to make room—a little
widening here, and the chopping off of an awk-
ward corner there, and the shifting of a railing in
another place. But three really big problems—
the construction of a city and suburban railway
system, the extension of the main line across the
harbor, and the removal of the main station to
some place where it will fill the bill—can’t be
put off much longer, no matter how many small
patches and repairs are effected.

Hunger v. Reason.
Two steps hadn’t been taken towards applying
the N.S.W. Industrial Disputes Act to the Lith-
gow ironworks business before there was the
usual clamor : “ They want to compel Hoskins to
keep open even if he has to do it at a loss.” It
takes a long while to kill that silly gibe. The
law doesn’t require any man to keep an industry
going at a loss, unless, of course, he has under-
taken to keep it going for a certain time. If he
has made that agreement, then he stands in the
same position as the other man who has under-
taken to supply goods, and is expected to go on
supplying them even if the market goes
against him. Apart from that special case, all
that an employer has to do is to satisfy a J udge
that he will lose money if he pays a certain wage.
The Law then does one of two things: it fixes a
lower wage, which will allow him to make a
profit, or anyway escape a loss; or, if that
will mean a degrading wage, it fixes a
fair wage. In the latter case it says that it is
better that the business should cease than that
the degrading wage should be paid. It doesn’t,
however, compel any employer to continue a busi-
ness—it simp / says that if he does continue the
business he 5k..1l not pay below a certain amount.
After that it will not allow any employer to adopt
other means to squeeze a lower w'age out of the
employee. It won’t allow any man to reduce
women and children to hunger in order to do
something of which justice and reason do
not approve ; and, therefore, it prohibits
the lockout under heavy pains and penalties.
In the Lithgow case, provided there was no
agreement in the way, the Court vyas open to
Hoskins. If he could not pay the wages hitherto
paid, then all he had to do was to lay his proofs
before a trained Judge, and the Court would give
him the relief which justice and reason required.
To say that the Court would compel him to keep
going at a loss is to allege that the Court is a fool
which doesn’t know proofs when it sees them.
The fair employet will welcome that Court; the
employer who prefers to rely upon the lockout
(and The Bulletin says this without reference to
the Hoskins ease at all) is the other sort of em-
ployer, who prefers to rely upon the hunger of
women and children rather than upon reason and
justice.

Some Straight Facts Concerning B. Smith’s
Brother and. the Empire’s Ally.
A man at Kobe (Japan) writes
The termination of the war with Russia has been fol-
lowed by a marked increase of crime throughout Japan.
During the last three years there has undoubtedly been a
conspicuous growth among the criminal classes of the
nation. Indeed the authorities have of late become
alarmed at the increasing tendency to assault and
battery among the common citizens, often leading to
wounds, and even murder. Crimes resulting from
debauchery and levvdness are on the increase. Foreigners
no longer find it safe to wander alone or unprotected among
the beautiful valleys and hills that environ so many of
the Japanese cities. Robberies and holdups that used to
be almost unknown are now common. Even on the
public street foreign women have been relieved of their
purses in broad daylight, after the manner of towns in the
so-called “ Wild West.” The authorities seem either help-
less or indifferent to the urgency of the circumstances.
The police force of all Japanese cities is no doubt inade-
quate to the extent of duty devolving upon guardians of
the law, being much weaker in calibre and numbers than
the force in similar communities abroad. And the few
there are appear to be either in constant fear of the
criminal classes or deficient in the initiative necessary to
the capture and punishment of offenders. The Japanese
policeman does not patrol the streets as in Europe or
America ; he keeps his measured watch at what is known
as a police-box, a place often difficult to find ; and the
criminal feels perfectly free to operate at a safe distance
from the station. The night watchmen of towns and
cities go about banging two pieces of hardwood together,
the constant clapping keeping the criminal aware of the
exact spot the watchman is in, and so enabling him to
escape detection.
It is the conviction of most foreign residents in this
country that the root of the trouble lies in the fact that
the officers of the law are really in deadly fear of the
criminal classes. Every Japanese criminal carries a con-
cealed weapon, and the murder of policemen is a frequent
occurrence. Even if the thief does not succeed in dis-
patching his captor, he will in time find some means of
getting square with any that would hamper his move-
ments. Consequently, offenders against the law are per-
mitted a much freer hand than they would enjoy in
western communities. In old Japan, the fear of sum-
mary treatment by the community itself exercised a
wholesome and, on the whole, deterrent influence on the
would-be wrongdoer ; but, now that all is in the hands of
a few police, crime appears to thrive.
The very fact that hundreds of people in Japan are able
with impunity to live by blackmail is in itself sufficient
evidence of inadequate protection to citizens.
The average citizen of Japan appears to have little con-
fidence in the law as an arbiter of the wrongs he suffers ;
in fact, he more frequently takes the law into his own
hands. Among the lower classes the appeal is not to the
authorities, but to the fist or the knife, and not infre-
quently to the yet more fatal short sword. Stabbing
is a favorite ” method of settling individual di-
vergences of opinion \ and in cases of clashes with
corporations, rioting and destruction are almost sure to
ensue. Last week some students at a common school had
a dispute over a game, and three of them were seriously
wounded with knives. During the same week there was
a wanton and destructive riot on the premises of the
Oriental Timber Company, in the island of Kyushu. This
is a French concern doing extensive business in various
parts of Japan. The men in the big sawmill at Okobira
demanded a rise of wages ; and, failing to receive satis-
faction from the managers, the laborers at once became
riotous, attacked the foreigners, and beseiged the build-
ings. The infuriated mob made a savage attack with
- stones and other missiles on the office of the company,
making short work of the typewriters and other office
appliances, and then completely wrecked the building.
Then they proceeded to the residences of the officials and
demolished them in a similar manner. Next, the mill
was attacked, and all the valuable machinery, including
the electric plant, was in a short time reduced to scrap
iron. Individual pieces too large to be reduced to a
hopeless mass were thrown into the river. Even a couple
of bicycles that belonged to the officials were crushed
beyond recognition. The management staff and its fami-
lies in the meantime had fled into the mountains. The
vindictiveness of the rioters was especially seen in the
residence of Mr. Mak/xin, the manager. There, after
playing havoc with the house, they gathered up all the
bedclothes and the personal effects of the family
and tore them to shreds before finally, deposit-
ing them in the river. The nearest police
station being more than two hours away, all the
damage possible had been done before the arrival of the
officers. The amount of destruction is computed at
about 100,000 yen, but it is said that the work of re-
building is not likely to be undertaken. As this is the
first case of any wholesale destruction of the property of
foreigners since the abolition of extra-territoriality, it is
expected that the Japanese Government will have to pay
the damage, as did the Canadian Government in the
matter of the anti-Japanese riots in Vancouver last year.
In the event of an affair resulting in fatality for the
victim, the perpetrator very commonly turns his weapon
upon himself as a means of escape. The house-breaker
will usually kill a molester rather than brook interference,
an eventuality which the police have to bear in mind.
The same recklessness as to the value of life is apparent
in cases where the offender is not to be classed as an
habitual criminal. In the city of Nagoya recently
two private soldiers had a squabble over a petty
divergence of opinion. Their superior officer took
them out on the parade ground, remonstrated
with them, and then, becoming angered at their
indifference to his expostulation, shot them dead,
and then blew r out his own brains. Sergeant Yamaguchi
had been a model soldier, and on account of distinguished
services in the late war had received from the Emperor
the seventh Order of the Golden Kite.
A rather significant development among the criminal
classes in Japan is the increasing frequency of piracy
along the coasts and inland seas of the empire. The
Japanese have been known to plunder wrecks that had
been abandoned, but an attack upon cargo in transit has
not hitherto been known. However, a big lighter on the
way to Yokohama with a load to be transhipped to an
ocean steamer, was lately attacked by a force of pirates
and a large part of the cargo abstracted, the crew of the
fio-hter being helpless to offer resistance. In the beautiful
waters of the Inland Sea, too, especially in the vicinity of
Seto, acts of piracy are becoming alarmingly frequent.
Boats engaged in this nefarious method of livelihood are
known now to number more thau a dozen, each armed
w'ith deadly weapons. Various vessels plying among the
islands have been ransacked from time to time, and many
persons have been robbed in the last few years ; and even
women have been carried away captive. The present cap-
tain of the pirates is a young brave named Kadota,
who a vear ago was in the penitentiary. The police aver
that all the pirates in the Inland Sea yield him unswerv-
inw obedience. It is reported in the vernacular press
that, although the Inland Sea is known to be infested with
these sea-robbers, the police have so far been defied
with impunity, and are even in ignorance of the main
movements of the outlaws. Not long ago a Japanese mer-
chant from one of the islands set out in a boat with 600
ven in his pocket to pay a bill on the mainland. He had
proceeded but a little way when a boat lnauned by pirates

A great many other instances indicating an increase of

Stinking Fish in London.
Vic. Premier Bent must feel proud of his Attor-
ney-General Davies, who descanted so tactfully
on State Frights at a recent London function.
The occasion was a farewell shivoo to the new
Victorian State Rubber Stamp, Thomas Gibson-
Carmichael, and, after the usual valedictory flap-
doodle, Bent’s Mr. Davies uprose to speak a
trifle. And Bent’s Mr. D. created a very bad
impression. Instead of confining himself to harm-
less generalities, as every discreet person does in
mixed company, the egregious Davies must harp
on the internal relations between Commonwealth
and States. He told the company that he was a
true Federalist, but that he was also a true repre-
sentative of the States. “The States,” said he,
‘ ‘ are separate and independent under the Crown,
and the Commonwealth has no right whatever to
encroach upon the powers of a State any more
than a State has a right to encroach on the
powers of the Commonwealth. I am sorry to say,”
moaned Davies, “ that there is a tendency on the
part of the Commonwealth to encroach.” He
didn’t say how the Commonwealth encroached,
probably because he couldn’t, but he impressed
most carefully on that British audience that we
are a quarrelsome and disagreeable push, and that
we have got into such a bad state of shindy and
cantankerousness that we must bail up passing
strangers who have nothing to do with the family
animosity and who take no manner of interest in
it, and tell these strangers all about the row in
our back kitchen and how it was impossible to go
to sleep owing to the nagging of Maria in bed.
The utterly bored audience hailed Davies’ family
troubles with laughter and ironical cheers and
calls of “Time,” and the daily papers mostly
ignored his remarks. But all the same, Australia
needs its High Commissioner badly—a man to
speak in the name of all Australia, and drag small
folk like Davies out backwards by the coat tails
when they begin to be horribly foolish.

“ Floater,” from Melbourne :
On llth May last, Constable Goodson, of the Melbourne
police, was charged with having committed wilful and
corrupt perjury ; but, on account of the conflicting
evidence for the prosecution, the charge was dismissed.
That case excited little comment at the time—it was
briefly reported by the daily papers—but it presents
features peculiar enough to warrant a short recital of the
events which led up to those abortive proceedings. On
the night of 9th April last, Goodson arrested one
McGregor, at Fitzroy, on a charge of using obscene
language ; but when the case was brought up two or
three weeks later, McGregor was able to produce two or
three witnesses who declared on oath that his con-
versation had been without blemish, or words to
that effect. Consequently, the presiding magistrate
had no option but to dismiss the charge—a
result that gave to Goodson’s sworn statements an
extremely dubious appearance. Accordingly, Goodson
was proceeded against for perjury, with the resultalready
noted. On that occasion, however, the evidence against
him was not entirely unanimous—indeed it was so fishy
and contradictory in minor points that the Bench re-
marked that gross perjury had been committed. So it
had, but why? In the light of some recent history,
Goodson’s case becomes especially significant. Some few
people may remember that he played a part in the pro-
ceedings against the notorious City Tattersall’s Club,
that owed its foundation to ex-Tote Boss Wren
and a couple of kindred spirits. At that time, Goodson
was aiding the pertinacious Detective O’Donnell in the
collection of evidence for the suppression of this disrepu-
table resort and the prosecution of its proprietors. As
may also be remembered, the case against the club broke
down, and Goodson, the chief witness for the Crown, was
subsequently charged with perjury, and acquitted. Thus
it will be seen that this obscure police-constable has been
twice charged with perjury, and, in each case, one or
more of the witnesses seems to have sworn falsely in order
to convict him. Such circumstances bear a suspicious
resemblance to a conspiracy, nor is the suspicion
decreased by the fact that Goodson’s very connection
with the futile campaign against the club would
make him distinctly unpopular in certain quarters,
if not lay him open to persecution, when an
easy opportunity of injuring him presented itself.
The two charges were palpably trumped up, evi-
dently with no other motives than those of evil intent:
but what I wish to draw attention to is the facility and
impunity with which such false charges can be laid.
Apparently, two or three men can go into the witness-
box and swear anything without fear of subsequent
punishment. When the proceedings in both cases against
Goodson had proved abortive, and perjury was strongly
suspected against him, why was no further action taken ?
In each case one, if not more, of the witnesses stood as a
self-confessed liar on oath, and liable, under ordinary
circumstances, to prosecution. Yet I am not aware of
anybody other than Goodson being charged with either
perjury or conspiracy. This immunity from punishment
is singular ; and it looks as if there is one justice for the
constable who raises the “ dander ” of John Wren’s crowd,
and quite another and more questionable sort for his
accusers.
