In 8. Aus. the farmer has given up being hostile
to the Labor party ; and of the members of the
Commission which has been moving around getting
information to help him in marketing h’s grain,
two-thirds belong to the party that suppo.." T.
Price, You don’t frighten aS. Australian farmer
now even by telling him that a Socialist is coming ;
■ instead he mostly asks Mrs. Farmer to get “the
front room ” ready, and they start wondering
what the Socialist is going to do for them now.
According to one of the farmer-members of the
' Commission that has been taking evidence lately
in Sydney, about the finest thing that has been
done for “ the backbone of the country ” up to now
is the appointment of inspectors to see that
“the backbone” isn’t robbed in the matter
of fertilisers, and the appointment of other
experts to find out and advise what
special treatment the soils of various parts
of the country call for. There was a time when
the imported fertiliser consisted mostly of some
indefinite dirt in a bag with a bad smell attached ;
but nowadays the stuff has to pass a rigorous
Socialistic examination, and the result has been so
remarkable that, whereas only 60,000 acres were
artificially fertilised in 1897, this year the total got
up to 1,456,000 acres. On the information gathered
during this present inquiry and previously, mem-
bers of the Commission put the improvement in
the average yield at 100 per cent, in the case of the
poor areas of. S. Australia—and S. Australia is
mostly poor areas—and anything from 20 per cent,
upwards in the case of the better country. That
is the outcome of " interfering” with the “ great
natural resources ”; so in these days when the
Foreign-trade Ass brays about the folly of “ arti-
ficial aids to industry,” the S. Australian farmer
says “Rats!” and goes out to vote for Labor, or
Protection, or both.
«. ;
The wealthiest corporation in Melbourne is the
Metropolitan Gas Company, which always pays a
first-class dividend. This is one interesting fact.
Another is that, at the Kew court, on July 8, a
married man, with a sick wife and a sick child,
was charged with stealing a bicycle, house-linen,
etc. He was employed by the Gas Co. as a lamp-
lighter, and his wage was 255. a week. His wife
appeared and gave evidence as follows :
Accused only repeived 255. per week, and had to pay
out of it 3s. for a bicycle to do his work, and 10s. for rent.
Witness was unable to work herself, having been under
medical treatment for years, and her child had been
attending the Eye and Ear Hospital. She asserted, amidst
floods of tears, that her husband had committed the
crimes through absolute poverty, in order to support her
and the child. Then the witness broke down.
The Chairman of the Bench referred to the case as
“ pitiable,” and admitted that it was due to “in-
sufficient wages.” But, “as there were six
charges,” the Bench awarded six months’ gaol.
And probably it would have been simple
justice if those of the Gas Co. responsible for
the crime of paying a man 225. a week
net for his services (the whole dashed lot
of them) had, at the same time, been given 32
months’ Hard Jug. Still, of course, the Tory
orator will declare that Wages Boards are quite
unnecessary.

The Private Enterprise slaughter-house, as a
rule, shouts to Heaven; and, when it doesn’t
shout, it screams. Heaven can’t hear itself harp-
ing for the uproar made by private slaughter-
houses, and saints go about with their halos over
one eye asking bothered archangels why the nuis-
ance isn’t stopped. The average private slaughter-
house proprietor gets so used to the disastrous
smells of his profession that ordinary, fresh
breeze seems flavorless and uninteresting. If he
is a conscientious person he cleans up his pre-
mises in accordance with the law, and regretfully
suppresses the bright blue smell of dead pig, the
purple odor of cow, and the green and red whiff
of the Sheep That Was. But, if he is one of the
bad old school, and belongs to the large gang which
howls about interference with Private Enterprise,
he just lets the offal lie round until the accumu-
lated horror brays in the sun like a brass band
gone mad. Quite lately, a doctor from Melbourne
Board of Health inspected a Private Enterprise
establishment from which Ballarat draws some of
its meat, and this is a brief newspaper description
of the tragedy he discovered :
In referring- to one place situated in Buninyong Shire,
and just over the Ballarat East boundary, it was stated
raw offal is simply thrown into the yards and pigsties,
where a large number of pigs are kept and fattened for
the market. The yards and some of the pigsties pre-
sented a filthy and disgusting appearance. Besides raw
offal being scattered, and some of it trampled and half-
buried in slime and mud, some hundreds of carcases and
portions of carcases of sheep were found lying all over
the place, in all stages of decomposition, and evidently
intended as food for the pigs.
The fact that he managed to stagger back to
civilisation and make a report is probably owing
to the fact that he timed his visit for the middle
of winter. That example of Private Enter-
prise, under a November sun, would be fatal to
any ordinary human being unprotected by a
diving-suit. It is gratifying to read that the
Board of Health has resolved to request the
Buninyong Shire Council to “take steps.” It
wouldn’t have been unreasonable, under the cir-
cumstances, if it had asked the council to take
a cannon. Almost anything might reasonably be
taken to an affair of that kind.

A better from a gentleman whose pen-name is
none too legible :
The Methodists, the Sydney Telegraph, and other
religious bodies are greatly indignant over the proposed
labor ordinance in Papua. Emergency meetings are
called, audit is yelled that the ordinance will lead to the
enslaving of the native population. Now, why all this
stir ? Is it because the Australian Government has
control over Papua ? If the Imperial Government had
control, and, according to usage, simply forced the
natives to work, would anything be said ?
In British ‘ ‘ Protectorates ” in the Central Pacific, forced
labor has been in vogue for several years. Take the
Gilbert Group, for instance. The natives have been com-
pelled to do “Government work” since 1900—not one
month in the year, but three days of every week, except
about a month before Christmas and a week or so before
the King’s birthday. This includes women as well as mjen,
and the alternative there is imprisonment with hard
labor. Fairly heavy land-taxes are imposed. There are
three mission societies in the Group—barely on drinking,
and certainly not on speaking terms with each other ; but
not one of them ever said a word against forced labor.
The proposal does not sound very nice—interfering
with the liberty of the subject and all that, don’t you
know; but when you become acquainted with the
natives the thing takes on another complexion. A set of
houses (living, cook, firewood, bath) for each family must
be built by the working community, roads must be kept
in repair, prisons built, hospitals for skin diseases erected
and maintained in good order, leading marks erected on
the reef showing the boat entrance, etc. This “ Govern-
ment work ” done, the nigger still works compulsorily,
but h<- does it more directly for himself. He must gather
his fallen cocoanuts ready to be cut out for copra making,
work on his babai-plantation, clear away the under-
growth in the bush —things he rarely tackled before the
“slavery” days. The work for the coming month is
planned and discussed in open council in which all
natives take part. Native magistrate, councilmen and
police supervise the work. This still leaves the natives
enough time to play their games—dancing, kite-flying,
or racing miniature canoes on the surf —and to seduce
each others’ wives.
Sometimes labor is misapplied, as when the natives
were compelled to pull down their large fine houses and
erect houses of uniform size and pattern (12ft. long by
10ft. wide), about the only ordinance in connection with
enforced labor that has caused any real discontent.

The N.S.W. Forestry Commission, after a solid
year’s tripping, is still proceeding merrily along
the primrose way, with many crusts and empties
littered in its wake. And, according to the baro-
meter, it looks as if the report will not be ready
for next session of Parliament. Ninety-nine men
out of every hundred were fully aware, before the
Commission started to wander voluminously over
the landscape (1) that the forests of N.S.W.
should be placed under better control; (2) that
the export of hardwood should be stopped until
improved methods of administration are adopted ;
(3) that a system of reafforestation should be prac-
tised especially on those areas where the land is
unsuitable for settlement ; (4) that nurseries
should be established in central districts; (5)
that a Commissioner should be appointed to
administer the timber industry ; (6) that in future
leases provision should be made for the planting
of trees and the protection of young timbers; (7)
that on the Western plains steps should be taken
to plant belts of trees for shade and timber pur-
poses. Yet the Commission has been travelling
about Eastern Australia for a solid 12 months
while one witness after another, and single wit-
nesses, and casual heaps and stacks of witnesses
told it these facts over again, and it threatens to
continue this journeying and good living inde-
finitely at the expense of Wade’s surplus.
A Vic. Art Show.
“ 0.K.” writes in bitter mood :
The pictures in the latest Exhibition of the Vie. Artists’
Society number 350, including the black-and-white draw-
ings, among which Will Dyson has something interesting
to say, and Tom Carter attracts attention with a crayon
portrait study of a gracious woman. To say that 200 of
the exhibits are unworthy of place in a pretentious
show is to understate the fact. The average quality of
the V. A.S. paintings was higher ten years ago, when they
were fewer, and when more care was taken in the disposal
of them. Indiscretions in the hanging are apparent
everywhere. Some of the indiscretions amount to sins,
and seem intended to excite protest.
Instance the portrait of Norman Lindsay (No. 5) by
Bernard Hall—the strongest thing in the gallery. It
shouts defiance at its nearest neighbor—the picture of
Sydney Harbor by Streeton. Streeton’s decorative work
is for the most space a presentment of bright blue paint
—a solid cerulean sea dotted with white-sailed boats
that are on the mimic water, but not in it. This
expanse of blaring blue emphasises the bilious “ greenery-
yallery” background to Norman Lindsay, and repels the
admiration due to the portrait as a work of art and a
likeness. With a different environment it would make a
better impression. Almost equally convincing and less
objectionable is the same artist’s portrait of Archibald
Strong (24). Bernard Hall can present a young man in
his habit as he lives, but his failures to portray a pretty
girl are unpardonable. There are two attempts, and the
victim in each case is Miss Ruby Lindsay. Heavens 1
How cruelly these artists advertise one another ! An
avowed portrait (23) of the glowing Ruby misrepresents
her as a troubled matron of uncertain age, propping up
her chin with her thumb as she wavers, perhaps, between
the question of beef or mutton for dinner. She is backed
by a parlor wall (presumably). The wall tint is bright
yellow, aggravated by a blue and green dado. No. 30 intro-
duces the same young lady disguised as an ’Arriet
in a bright blue bank-’oliday ’at, leaning against
the.same hideous parlor wall as before. The cult of ugli-
ness can go no further than in these libellous paintings.
But Bernard Hall is a finished craftsman, who can stand
upon his professional dignity to defend his arrogant
mannerisms of color.
The trouble is that many of his pupils have adopted the
cult, without having anything to stand upon. A few
years ago Leslie Wilkie was accepted as a most promising
student, and newspaper commentators were advised to
encourage him. What can be honestly said of Wilkie to-
day ? liis work is utterly devoid of charm even where it
shows technical ability. His No. 19 gives a back view of a
brown, unwholesome female figure in the nude, ungrace-
fully posed on a faded mauve cloth, so hard in its folds
that one would pity the poor bare sitter if she looked real.
But the touch of human nature is not there. The hand
wherewith the figure scratches her head is as arti-
ficial as the arm that directs it. No. 27, by Leslie
Wilkie, is “ The Debutante,” price £lO5. The damsel hasa
swollen face and neck, mouse-colored hair, and a frock
and gloves that are insults to her complexion. There
are misguided jargonists, maybe, who will affect to see a
harmony of color in this large picture—as though there
could be true harmony in a concord of sour tones. The
thing is Ugly. A sensitive person, condemned to live in
one room with it, would die of it. The same remark
applies to other figure paintings in the present collection,
not excepting the Bft. x sft. portrait of Panton, P.M., by
Mrs. Muntz Adams. This gigantic affair may be Art to
Panton. It would be death to some people if they had to
take it for a term of punishment.
Yet the painters, in many cases, have a good knowledge
of the tricks of the trade. They are artists in the same
degree that a painstaking mummer of long experience
and no ability is an actor. They do their best, but they
ought not to be doing it at all.
No. 45, “The Sawmill,” by Hal Waugh, was lately ex-
hibited elsewhere, with many other Australian bush sub-
jects by the same improving striver. The false and
depressing coloring is the matter with it. .In 299, “ The
Forgotten Dead,” the color is equally false and exceed-
ingly festive. All the tints of Joseph’s coat are introduced
into an alleged bit of West Melbourne. The blue, red,
pink and yellow houses in the background seem calcu-
lated to wake the forgotten deceased. Here is the very
“jim-jams” of art. Yet the painter, Alf Fischer, may
bope for general recognition of his ability when he' has
sobered his style and ceased to paint the town red. In
one of the water-color pictures, where his gaiety of spirit
finds a more fitting outlet, the result is very pleasing.
Of the water-colors, by the way, none are more simply
effective than two by Amy Vale—weak in drawing, but
tasteful in composition—and other two or three by Jane
Sutherland. Several contributions by McOubbin tell of his
recent visit to England. He has broken out into the*
Turneresque method, and there is a less decided imitation
of Corot in one instance. The original McOubbin has
effaced himself for the time being, and the atmosphere
of Australia is absent from his work. Walter Withers
is another “ old hand ” unworthily represented, nor has
J. Ford Paterson anything to boast of, although “The
Lagoon ”is a characteristic canvas. Talking of John
Ford P., the baneful influence of his National Gallery
picture, “ A Bush Symphony,” can be detected in
sundry places on the V.A.S.’s walls just now.
The “Symphony” was sickbed o’er with the pale
cast of a decadent impressionism. New in design,
when the Gallery trustees paid £2OO for it, the
friends of the artist thought they saw the element of
greatness in it. Now there is a cult of mystic art, and
what the high priest tried to evolve from his own inner
consciousness is being weakly imitated by faithful
disciples.
The most successful of the young painters is C.
Wheeler, who has received more than enough praise for
“ Grandmother’s Gown ” (No. 34). The picture, though
cold in tone, is not unlovely in character. Clever and
faulty. A young girl, seated before a dressing-table
against an indefinite background, hasturned round in her
chair to look in a hand-mirror and get sight of her reflec-
tion in the table-mirror behind her. The effect is not
vivid, yet there is an air of truth about the picture.
Something much stronger may be expected from
Wheeler next year, if he closes his ears to the jargon of
them who deprecate rude health and sanity in art pro-
ductions, and cures himself of a tendency to insipidity in
his coloring. The picture might have been made more
forcible.
Of the others “ too numerous to mention,” some are
meritorious, but none are striking. The most strikingly
inferior ones can be left to condemn themselves.

A very much larger Mark Hambourg than
of yore—much more robust in frame, and much
more robust in art —appeared at Sydney Town
Hall on Saturday last. Also he showed much
larger geniality. His reception of a very warm
welcome was quite touching. He threw a not-
aggressively-long crop of hair at the audi-
ence in a whirl or Catherine wheel, and he
infused a certain heartiness into his repeated
bows, and a geniality into his smile.
Some years ago Hambourg came here as a very
moderate band. He has come back twice, and
each time he has added to the number of the
instruments. Also each time he has come back
with a better conductor. He is now a complete
orchestra, and one may take it that the conductor
is settled in his place for life, and has not
much left to learn. Mark presents many of
the features of a first-rate band. He can
fill the Town Hall with the sound of his piano
in a manner that satisfies without making one’s
head ache. That is more than can be said for the
Town Hall’s own fixed instrument, as those know
who remember the largest organist in the world
when he threw himself like one possessed upon the
world’s (almost) largest organ. And Mark can, as
well, produce those ppp up to fff effects
which suggest moonlight scenes, or the drop-
ping of eiderdown feathers, or of raindrops, or
of hailstones or thunderstorms or earthquakes;
and he can send not only the critics, but people
who do really know something about music, into
raptures what time they discourse of all those
technical excellencies which are bound up in the
Musical Dictionary. Hambourg is a great man
in his own particular way. As a pianist, he is
quite in the front rank, and nothing but the dire
necessity to save up for the American fleet can
make his season other than a success.
** * *
“The Red Mill” revolves joyously at
Sydney Royal these nights, and brings good grist
to the box-office. It is a merry show, with no
particular pretensions to be classed except just as a
fun-maker; and Sydney evidently has a vacancy
for that sort of show just now.
*- * it-
As a wind-up to its Sydney season, the
Flemming-Day co. revived “Doctor Wake’s
Patient ” at the Criterion. The sentiment is
treacly ; the fun, for the most part, aggressively
obvious. It is, however, on those very qualities
that “ Dr. Wakes Patient” grips the great heart
of the people—the sort of people, that is, who
don’t stop between laughter and tears to ponder
on the unworthy methods by which their emotions
are being got at. Given the situation of a frOnt-
rank Harley-street doctor, loving and loved by a
maiden of title, the audience is asked to credit
that the latter’s haughty parents, who didn’t
object to their child’s suitor as a doctor, are going
to forbid the banns and go fanti about the
man generally as a farmer’s son. It is
an archaic idea dating back to the epoch
when ladies wore crinolines and talked in
all seriousness of “the better classes.” A suc-
cessful grocer or ironmaster (a Lipton or a Krupp)
is a more desirable match in London or Berlin
society to-day (no matter who his progenitors
were) than one with more birth and much less cash.
Palpably, the hypothesis that the Earl and Coun-
tess of St. Olbyn would become anguished wrecks
on discovering that Dr. Forrester Wake’s people
owned a farm is advanced so that Andrew Wake—
that splendid old humbug—may dampen every
handkerchief present by offering to “renounce
the boy for ever,” and wander abroad in an atmo-
sphere of desolate uprightness with “ the wife” —
an outcast for that, though honest, he is poor.
But it isn’t good enough. One mentally indicts
the aged bucolic (even in the very act of
listening to his protestations and observing
his “business” with the pathetically-immense
Family Bible) of obtaining the sobs of the female
immediately behind one under false pretences.
And so with the humor. The Devonshire agri-
culturist who is prosperous enough to send his
son to a University doesn’t talk like a Tivoli
comedian. Such British countesses as are outside
Colney Hatch comport themselves with more re-
pose than she of St. Olbyn—if only for their
liberty’s sake. And one of the reasons why the
Church of England continues to impress the
masses is to be found in the fact that such digni-
taries as the Criterion Bishop of Selby are im-
possibilities in it.
* * * *
The most interesting feature of the very
brief “Dr. Wake” season was the acting of A.
Mallalieu. This writer has visited the Criterion
about a dozen times since the Flemming-Day
co. came there, and yet failed to appreciate to the
full, till last Saturday night, this mime’s finished
artistry. Mallalieu played Duff Wynterdon, a
rather fatuous youth about town. He never once
over-stressed a word or gesture (a wondrous feat
in such a part), and yet he made his every point
so that the house rose at him, at short intervals,
with cries of joyous affection. His gift is that of
the skilled draughtsman; he gets his effects in
the fewest lines possible. Beatrice Day was an
admirable Lady Gerania. She brought an air of
reality even into the unconvincing scene where
the doctor persuades her that “ duty ’’ compels
them both to bow to the incredible attitude of her
parents. In lighter vein she was charming. Her-
bert Flemming played Andrew Wake as well as
it is possible to conceive of anyone playing that
mass of bogus sentiment, and A. S. Homewood
was good as his Byronic doctor-son, who de-
servedly, towards the end of the play, endures
the stigma of knighthood—Britain’s now firmly-
established method of ear-marking utter J.P.-like
obtuseness. Harriet Bronson was a bewitching
little person in the hands of Elise Clarens. Mr.
and Mrs. Murdoch, the Bishop and the Countess
aforementioned, this paper declines to take
seriously.

“ Miss Hook of Holland” makes her bow
to Ma State for the first time on Saturday night
at Sydney Criterion. Miss Hook will be sup-
ported in her trying feat by a number of tuneful
songsters and dancers of both sexes, so that her
legal guardians confidently anticipate the exorcis-
ing, by her agency, of the chronic depression that
sits upon the Leah (not to say the Gummidge)
among the States.

This week sees the last of Miss Anglin as
the very gradually penitent Thief at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney); and on Saturday the charmingly-natural
lady will make a second start in “ Zira,” described
as “ a drama in four acts, by J. H. Manners and
Henry Miller, founded upon the same story as
Wilkie Collins’ ‘New Magdalen.’” But, as the
first act of “ Zira” is pitched in S’Africa, at the
close of the Boer war, this “ New Magdalen ” is
served up in a dress that W. Collins might not
recognise. G-. S. Titheradge is, on this occasion,
Sir Frederick Knowles, F.R.C.S. ; and if W.
Collins suggested the main story, Sir Frederick
Treves, F.R.C.S., probably suggested Knowles.
Titheradge ought to do splendidly in that part; you
can rarely watch him without thinking what a
charming bedside manner he would have if he were
in the doctoring business.

The “Kilties,” a Scottish band of two
score players, who can play as softly as the
buzzing of a bee and as loudly as the trumpetings
of twelve “must” elephants, open in Sydney Town
Hall on August 15. It is on a world tour under
engagement by Fred Shipman. There is a vocal
choir, two seven-foot drum majors, and a clan of
dancers to add novelty to the band’s attractiveness.
* * *-
At Sydney Lyceum, the übiquitous Spencer
has anticipated the arrival of the American fleet
by reeling off views of the armada entering San
Francisco Harbor, the parade at San Pedro, the
flower fete at Santa Barbara, the official reception,
and the long line of warships swinging at anchor.
The “Merry Widow” waltz craze is in its last
nights, and all who have not seen and heard are
warned to do so quickly. It is good. The story
of Samson begins a new epoch in moving pictures,
and writer has great hopes that he will yet see
Revelation and Genesis flitting across Spencer’s
screen. Samson is a picturesque figure, but
Delilah is not the writer’s ideal of that Philistine
charmer. Writer always pictured her with a
Madonna-like face—that type, of woman always
suggests the Madonna face and long tapering
fingers and small plump limbs. The Bible in
moving pictures is a good idea, as the gentlemen
who composed the Old Testament had a splendid
knowledge of human nature and a breezy decisive
way of telling things.
* * *
Shakspeare is becoming more famous. He
has got into the Fiiekergraph. At Sydney
Palace on Saturday, the large, hot, kilted tragedy
of “ Macbeth ” was shown in a series of West’s
Pictures, and the joy of the audience was
unbounded. It felt it had learned something of
a classic that in unpictorial print seems to lack
juice. Victoria’s Premier, the beknighted Thomas
Adolphus Bent, was in the auditorium, and the
Great Living Statesman was immensely interested
in the scenes without words reminiscent of the
Somewhat Dead Great Writer. “The Pirate’s
Honor ”is the latest star attraction. It is quite
possible for a pirate to possess such a commodity
as honor without stealing it. “ Scenes in Surrey ”
comes as balm after the bloodshed of “ Macbeth ”
and the pirates’ fight in mid-ocean.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli has been brightened con-
siderably by the arrival of the Miles Stavordale
Quintet, the clever banjo players who can make
their instruments preach like a country clergyman
on a drowsy summer morning. As the sermon
drones along you can almost hear the occasional
reverent and subdued cough, the old man in the
back row shuffling his feet, and the indignant
verger kicking the inoffensive and religiously-
inclined dog from the porch. Miss Mignonette
Kokin, a bright young person from Chicago, who
character-sketches and dances, lets the audience
see how she does her lightning changes with the
help of two dressers. The revelation is made
per medium of the biograph screen. This
keeps the audience interested during the wait, and
the instant the picture is switched off, the lady
herself steps on to the stage just as if she had
floated down off the screen. Galetti’s dogs and
baboons constitute a good animal turn. The dogs
look affable, and are distinctly clever, but the
baboons—though talented—suffer from their near
resemblance to inferior human beings. This paper
would rather see baboons keeping to Nature than
observe them keeping close to Man, and imitating
his accomplishments. The rest of the bill leans
mostly on the agile John Higgins, Tod Lowen,
Regan and Ryan, Fred Bluett, and clever little
Baby Watson.
*m * *
“Brewster’s Millions” is well into its
second week at Adelaide Royal, and has been ad-
vertised by a money-scattering act in the public
streets. Practical Adelaide took the usual com-
petition seriously, and sent in all manner of de-
tailed suggestions for spending a spare quarter-
million on good works ; the prize went to someone
who suggested presenting the Commonwealth with
a warship. Brewster himself is what our ances-
tors described as “an agreeable rattle,” and
Kingston plays him with just the requisite light-
ness of touch. Mrs. Brough and Miss Temple,
Roland Stavely, and many other good players flit
airily through parts that are quite unworthy of
them. The yacht scene is rather a tight fit on the
Royal stage, but it does all that was expected of
it. The co. promises the almost-tragedy of “John
Glayde’s Honor ”to finish its season. The Tivoli
has gone back to its own brand of Variety.
* * ->ic *
The same old “ Fatal Wedding ” —which
is not a wedding at all, but a deading—was re-
vived at Melbourne Royal on Saturday. Instead
of a different plot, or an entirely new set of dia-
logues, the management offered a change of cast
as a suitable novelty in this connection. The
public craving for novelty will not be fully satisfied
by the change. Aunt Cora appears this time as a
stoutish lady, with a benevolent chin and a
serenity of countenance quite unbecoming to a
villainess of the deepest dye. Also the weeping
young mother (Miss Beatrice Holloway) pre-
serves a pleasing embonpoint through all her
extraordinary misfortunes, and in no particular
is the foolishness of this audacious narrative
less apparent than before as regards the
grown-up characters. The improvement is
in the juvenile section. Little Queenie Wil-
Hams, the boss kiddie, is very little and
bright, and almost natural in her precocity. When
“ The Fatal Wedding ” first came to Melbourne
the presiding youngster was surprisingly clever,
but since then the supply of Australian stage
infants has increased in volume, and the
phenomenon of yesteryear is outdone by the prize
nipper of to-day. Under the careful management
of 0. JR,. Stanford, the Tin Can Band variety show
has been even too much developed. There are
now 40 kiddies on the job, and although the
scene is always strong in little details, it outstays
its welcome. The sparkle goes out of it. Which
remark has already lost its application if the per-
formance has been shortened. Anyhow, the
rowdy kiddies from the Alley will march to the
banging of the tin can for a fortnight only.
* * *
Last week this paper feared that the gods
of Bent’s capital would not save Anderson’s some-
what dull drama. The doubt was ill-grounded.
The gods are doing their best, and “Man for
Man ” will hold the big stage of the Russell
Theatre for another two or three weeks. (The
“ Russell ” is not the official name of the house,
but the name which ought to have been chosen in
preference to the “King’s.”) Every, night last
week 1100 bodies—-some of which contained souls,
perhaps—were packed into the spacious amphi-
theatre, and 2200 eyes glared at Dunbar when he
remarked that the game was going nicely into his
hands. The less expensive seats are less crowded,
owingto the fact that Anderson’s wealthier patrons
are mainly addicted to the giddv-girl brand of
bellowdrama, and want a lot of gilded sin for
their money. There is no gilded sin to speak of
in “ Man for Man.” Nor any blud. Bert Bailey .
committed a murder, he says, but the crime is a
bygone incident, and it contributes nothing to the
gaiety of the story, save that it accounts for the
gentleman’s presence at Portland, where he gets
introduced to George Cross. Bailey, the blood-
thirsty. is not intended to send a shudder through
the audience’s frame. He comes under the head-
ing of comic relief—a species of intermittent
humor which is comic when it arrives, and a relief
when it goes away.
RUTH LINCOLN as Sally Hook in “ Miss
Hook of Holland,” to be staged by Meynell
and Gunn at Sydney Criterion on Saturday,
25th July.
Photo, Burlington Studio.
Maskelyne and Devant’s Mysteries, now
at the Bijou, have received all The Bulletin
approval they ever deserved. In fact, a little
extra indulgence has been granted to the show on
account of its moral purity and tendency to go
slow. “Will, the Witch and the Watchman” is
a very ancient illusion, loaded with talk and
“business” calculated to set a Sunday-school
picnic in a roar. For the rest, the juggler is
neat, the conjurer is an expert, and Barclay
Gammon, with his quick, thick tongue and lively
treatment of the piano, has no difficulty in shaking
things up.
* * *
The tenth week of “The Merry Widow”
brings an announcement that her nights are
numbered; “Peter Pan” is coming back to Her
Majesty’s presently, therefore the Widow must
waltz elsewhere. Before she goes the Melbourne
public has an opportunity to note the effect of a
new conductor on the music of the opera. Harold
Carr was to take the chair last Saturday, and the
difference between massive Slap, and gentle
Harold should be more or less perceptible to the
ravished ear of the Widow’s frequent attendants.
** * *
Mrs. Wiggs moralises with continued
success at the Princess’s. The breath of popular
applause has not tarnished the shining surface of
Mrs. Wiggs’s artistic triumph. She makes her
points as quietly as at first, nothing exaggerating
nor setting down aught in malice. Her example,
which is even better than her precepts, should
deter Hiram Stubbins from exceeding the legiti-
mate limits of a stage drunk when he rolls upon
the ground of the Cabbage Patch in his greed for
the guffaws of a weak-minded minority. The
Grseco-Roman style'of intoxication is out of place
in such company. Otherwise the performance has
been slightly strengthened by a change in the
personality of Miss Lucy’s young man. All the
same, Miss Lucy must be regarded as unlucky in
her voung men.
** * *
Kubelik is to give three more recitals at
Melbourne Town Hall, for the delight of the local
musical community and the entertainment of those
people who missed hearing him last month hut
are bent on getting there this time. The World’s
Greatest Violinist is a celebrity not to be missed
by those self-respecting persons. The three
Kubelik nights are Saturday (25th), Monday
(27th), and Thursday next.
** * *
West’s star attraction at Melbourne
Olympia last Saturday night was Don Juan, some
of whose erotic exploits were thrown upon the
cambric with thrilling effect. He is attired in the
vivid, loquacious togs of the First Charles epoch,
and, when not prancing about masked to the
teeth and slaughtering elegant beings as harm-
less-looking as himself, the Don is making
passionate proposals to buxom wenches in
satin raiment. Sometimes, the p.ps. cause the
mercury to almost ignite in its upward impetus.
The most noticeable facts about the film are the
splendid oratorical powers of Juan’s clothes, his
furniture, and his apartments; and his timid
pugnacity when facing a spook which unaccount-
ably wanders about in ill-fitting hardware.
Altogether, it is a nice, imaginative piece of fic-
tion from Paris, and is the sort of fattening food
for the masses which can be extolled. Other
incentives to optimism are “The Good Candi-
date,” “ The Cat’s Revenge,” and “Jones Has a
Tile Loose.” But “ A Breach of Promise Case”
—a rather inane picture of a gentleman dog and a
lady dog—-makes one despondent.
* * * sK
Abdullah’s Arabs, who made their first
appearance at Melbourne Opera House last
Saturday, are equally bright and exciting in their
famous “act.” There are a dozen of them, includ-
ing the bearded Abdullah himself. In the build-
ing of human pyramids these cheerful sons of the
desert take turns at bearing the heat and
burden of the superstructure. Any of them,
seemingly, can support several of his colleagues
on his shoulders and twined around him.
Finally ten Arabs dispose themselves neatly about
the body of the principal Samson of the troupe,
his legs being the only pair of understandings in
touch with the stage. After which an individual
arid collective whirl of Arabs—a wild competition
in agility—rouses the Rickards public to the
giddiest height of enthusiasm it has attained for
many a long month. The “ turn ”is better than
merely wonderful. It is exhilarating. There are
other “turns” by other people, but the'Arabs
leave no room for any rival importance in the bill.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Parvenu”: Let me correct some of “Ami’s” mis-
apprehensions concerning- Dan Barry, deceased. He says
(9/7/’08) that he “ can never imagine Dan Barry being
stage-struck. Whatever had to be filled up he’d take it
respectably.” Now, if Barry had a fault, it was his high
opinion of Barry as an actor. As for filling up vacancies—
well, rabbits! plain and illuminated rabbits! Oftdid Daniel
brave the scathing sarcasm of his “bunch” by picking
out the “fat” parts—many of which were entirely un-
suited to him—solely on account of their corpulence.
“ Ami ” opines that Barry had “a gopd education,
gleaned partly from the Melbourne University.” As a
fact, Barry had to shift for himself at the age of eight, or
thereabouts ; selling papers was one of his early enter-
prises. Dan’s only Alma Mater was the kerbstone. He
was given to romancing some, to put it mildly, which
accounts probably for “Ami’s” quaint views as to the
place where this mime absorbed his learning. “Ami”
notes that Barry’s health had “ for many years been
delicate.” Rabbits and small deities! His homely old
dial glowed like a summer sunset! Once, on seeing in a
patent-medicine book an account of his mortal illness,
and the swift and complete cure that was effected by the
swallowing of Bashed Pills, I asked him whether the
nostrums had really done him good. Dan answered with
the frankness that'made him the lovable character he was
that he’d never had a day’s scarlet illness in hiscrimson life,
but had got five bright-red pounds for the testimonial.
“ Ami ” is right as to Bob Holtyford being the descendant
of Bob Whitworth, but when naming Barry’s stock artists
he omits Lou Levy' and Gladys Grant. Barry, Baker and
Norman were all supering together in Arthur and Helen
Vivian’s Dramatic Co.
“ Harrison Owen ”: Re the evil influence which the rise
of the shickergraph had on the fortunes of the late
Dan Barry (B. 9/7/’08), the old busker was enabled
through this instrument to demonstrate for the manyth
time his wonderful knack of adapting himself to circum-
stances. About a fortnight after the San Francisco earth-
quake, Dan arrived in our hamlet and bespread its walls
with the following ad. : —“ Pictures of the Fearful Holo-
caust. Realistic Reproduction of the Devastating
Horror. Gigantic Upheavals of Mother Earth. Giant
Buildings Swaying and Crashing,” etc., and much
more written (capitals and all) as only one man in
Australia could write it. The public, pausing not
to calculate how long it would take an airship to bring
a load of flickergraph films over from Am-murika, hurried
like mad to see the glorious and bloody wreck. For a
couple of hours they gazed blankly at pictures of the
Eight Hours Day procession and other peaceful events,
but never a spadeful of earth quaked anywhere. At long
length the deceived rustics became boisterous, and for a
time it looked as if somebody would have to go in search
of the Law to read the Riot Act; but Dan came before the
screen and, in reply to rounds of hisses, catcalls,
Kentish fire, etc., he gave the soft lie which turneth away
stoush. And because Australians (most of ’em) have a
sense of humor, law-an’-order was restored.
Hawthorn has raised a choral society in its midst, and
the first concert of the new organisation is to make the
welkin ring next Wednesday (29th). Fred Mewton, a
clever young organist, will wave a baton at the choir, the
assisting soloists being Miss Lilian Reid, Leslie Banfield
and Hattenbach with his ’cello.
“Dora G. W.’s” trope: Beethoven’s “Moonlight”
Sonata, as interpreted by Hambourg, is a wild and
stormy thing. Others have played it, and have
suggested naught but a golden moon gleaming on the
placid waters of the harbor, with lovers filled to over-
flowing with “Love’s Young Dream” and a determina-
tion to catch the last boat home, gliding down to Manly.
Mark’s rendering whirls us outside the Heads in the
teeth of a roaring gale, while dark clouds obscure the
.moon, and white-haired combers hurl themselves against
the South Head, and sea-mews complain, and gulls argue
the point, and all is terror and commotion.
The sight of the three Chows now pervading
Sydney in their national costumes brought back memories
of Li Hung Chang, who looked as quaintly out of place
when he visited London, a decade ago, as these aliens do
here to-day. While I live I shall not forget Li’s arrival in
the Empire Lounge, London. It was a Saturday night.
The promenade that encircles the back of this huge
music-hall was packed with .youthful Britishers, male and
female. Every light lady in the metropolis who
could raise the wherewithal to get her frock out
of pawn and pay her ss. entrance-fee seemed to
be there. The great arc with its inhabitants suggested
a drunken rainbow after a downpour of patchouli. Far
away on the stage below an unimportant balladist made
faint melody. Suddenly strange noises were heard with-
out, and the whole place grew still with amazement as
Li Hung Chang, followed by five or six of his countrymen,
entered. The points that struck one hardest about Li
were—(l) That he was about 6ft. Bin. high ; (2) that a
foot at least of the said stature was forehead. He seemed
to like the Empire Lounge. Though his party had a
couple of boxes Li kept obtruding himself on Lutetia’s
chosen haunt. He paced gloomily' round the circle, glaring
deadly over the ladies’ heads, and then paced gloomily
back again. Strenuous efforts were made to anchor the
potentate, whose extraordinary night-gown arrangement
in eighteen different colors went far to confirm previous
rumors as to its owner’s immense wealth. (The London
female regards a Chow or nigger as “adorable” and
“ simply sweet.”) To his credit be it said, he remained
adamant—in public. When he left at about 11 p.m. a shrill
cheer went up ; the aged Pagan’s “turn’’had been the
most successful of the evening.
“Snell”: Some managements are almost too “fresh.”
For instance, at the opening night of the King’s Theatre,
Melbourne, a man was dragged out of the gallery for
crying “ No ! No ! ” when Tommy Bent called for cheers
for the King. It’s a question if a man who pays to see a
play is not justified in crying “No !” when other people’s
politics are pushed at him, and managers are rash in
needlessly turning on the chucker-out. Some day they
may badly want a chucker-in.
“ Vulcan ” : Manager Harry Rickards tells London Era
that, being 60 and a grandfather, he pines to quit the
show business and settle down in the sphere of life for
which he feels Nature intended him—viz., that of a
British country gentleman. However, Henry doesn’t see
much prospect yet awhile. He had the misfortune to
make more money last year than in any one year before,
but his bookings run right up to 1910, and unless he can
find somebody to buy his theatres he’ll have to grind at
the heartbreaking job of piling up coin till further notice.
Florence Baines, the buxom proprietress of “Miss
Lancashire Limited,” has resolved to make Australia her
home and abiding place, and has possessed herself of
15,000 Westralian acres, with the intention of becoming a
squatteress. This lump of land and the cattle and
sheep on its surface represent only portion of the Aus-
tralian profits of “ Miss Lancashire,” so there is evidently
money in running a cheap sort of play with a loud dish-
wrecking hoyden in it. Miss Baines intends devoting
herself to cattle breeding and theatrical management,
which somehow reminds of the genius who divided his
time between prize-fighting and the collection of rare
editions of Shakspeare.
The City Dads of Christchurch (M.L.) have promul-
gated a by-law ordering theatre managers to number
admission tickets to correspond with the seats in the
house, and making it a punishable offence to issue
more tickets than there are seats. So good old “ Standing
Room Only ” is dead and buried in Christchurch, and the
grass is already growing over his grave.
“ D.” : London is just now torn by conflicting emotions
over the dancing of Maud Allan. Maud cavorts about a
public stage in a more undressed state than any other
female has dared to affect since the gay old times when
two coats of woad and a stone battle-axe were considered
the height of propriety. The special correspondent of
the Paris Nf.w York Herald hurried over to see her show,
and afterwards wrote this to hispaper(l/6/’oB)from London:
—“lt is a mooted question here whether her dance is
thoroughly improper or thoroughly artistic. There
is no middle opinion. Many go to see her be-
cause they think it is the most improper perform-
ance ever put on the English stage. As many
others go to see her because they consider it absolutely
pure and chaste art. Her dance struck me as being in-
decent and at the same time perfectly modest. She does
not wear tights or fleshings. She is barefooted and bare-
limbed. The draperies she wears are of the thinnest
gauze. In the Salome dance she is nude to the waist
except for some strings of beads and jewelled ornaments.
Below the waist she is also practically nude, as the single
gauze skirt is transparent and is generally floating high in
the air.” Local loyalists, who hold that the views of British
royalties and their associates are, in such matters, even
as the views of Ebenezer of the Abernethy Tabernacle,
and that the tastes of these exalted ones jump with
the tastes of Ebenezer’s godly spouse Martha, will be
amazed somewhat at the Herald’s further statement:
—“The King and Queen of England have seen Maud
Allan dance a number of times, including a special per-
formance at the Earl of Dudley’s house. Lady Richardson
has seen her about twenty times, and Prince Francis of
Teck about eight times.”
Pansy Montague Milo was last month touring Ireland,
and the truthful Cruikshank announced “she has covered
herself with fresh glory.” There are people who consider
there is room for covering of some description.
“Jean Sibi ” : There is one point about “ The Belle of
Mayfair ” that I should like to grow diffuse upon—but
won’t. I allude to the Gibson girl septet who exhibit
themselves in Act 11. According to the canons of Gibson
art, they ought to be thrilling examples of grace, deport-
ment and shapeliness ; but I doubt if any of the ladies
under notice fulfils all those requirements. The leader
of the team, robed in black velvet, appears to me as a
trifle too short and buxom for an ideal Gibsonienne,
while the bulk of the others, strikingly garbed in ruby, by
the way, seem unnecessarily slender. I could say a word
or two more, but, as that would be unchivalrous, I heap
much silence on the subject and pass on. And that is not
all I have against “ The Belle ”as a show. I object to the
painful freedom of Sir John Chaldicott’s malapropisms.
They hurt me so much that I would cheerfully assist in
his arrest for furious inanity.
“R.M.”: Was the detective in “The Thief’’really on
the wrong scent when he accused young Henri of stealing
the money? I doubt it. What if the boy and not Marie
was the thief after all ? They both confessed their guilt,
and in neither case was the confession withdrawn. In-
criminating bank notes were found in the possession of
both. So far their claims are equal. But now there is
the detective’s evidence to turn the scale in favor of the
boy’s guilt. There are several items—his heavy losses on
the turf, his entertainment of an actress in town, notable
an expensive form of distraction—which were never
properly explained, and could not be accounted for by a
paternal allowance of less than a “pony” a month.
What if the authors themselves have been deceived ?
Will somebody take the matter up ?
“ Melb ”: Everyone is charmed with Fitz Gerald’s article
in The Lone Hand on Rignold. Allow me to weave a
little woof into the web. The mother of William and
George was a splendid actress, even in such parts as
Romeo and Hamlet. Fitz Gerald under-estimates Irving’s
position just before “The Bells.” Irving had made a
name by his performances at the Queen’s in “ Dearer
Than Life,” “ Uncle Dick’s Darling,” and “ Oliver Twist ”
(he was praised by Dickens as Sikes), and, above all, in
“ The Two Roses,” at the Vaudeville. He had been a
leading man in London five years, ever since a successful
performance in “ The Belle’s Stratagem.” Yet, as Rig-
nold says, his appearance in “Fanchette,” at theL3 r ceum,
was a dismal failure. No wonder that Manager
Bateman tried to supersede him with Rignold. Jack
Ryder said, “ Don’t go ; the house will be in the hands of
the bailiffs 1 ” Well, maybe Rignold would not have
saved it from that. When Irving played Charles 1., Rig-
nold played Cromwell in opposition, in a play by Col.
Richards. Belmore’s Cromwell was a despicable foil for
Irving-. Rignold’s was the optimistic kind, adapted from
Victor Hugo. By the way, Belmore’s daughter, Miss
Daisy Belmore, teaches stage elocution in this city. She
was the original Dacia, named after her, in Barrett’s
“ Sign of the Cross.” Rignold’s Henry V. obliterates all
others in English stage history, from Kemble, Macready,
Phelps and C. Kean to Coleman, Calvert, Benson and
Waller.
Australia was well represented in Cork, Ireland, on a

Mozar, and Frank Lawton were showing in Musgrove's
“ Belle of New York ” co. at one theatre, and Milo and
Cruickshank Lang held the fort at a rival establishment.
NO FOLLOWERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One Australian management, it is said, has been com-
pelled to take strong measures to prevent the female
adorers of his leading man invading those parts of the
theatre where there is supposed to be no admittance
except on business.
Ye little ladies in the stalls,
And pert young persons in the pit,
Whom his entrancing smile enthralls,
Contented be from where you sit
To view the actor’s lovely face,
And glory in his youthful grace.
The manager is very wroth
At maids who wish to throng behind,
And so the unromantic Goth
Has set this notice, duly signed,
High up before the tearful crowd
Of girls : “ No followers allowed ! ”

DESPERATE.
A kitten was found to be suffering from plague. It
bee me infected through contact with rats. —Daily paper.
Thomas: “I can't stand this. There is nothing
for it hut to go and he inoculated."
The latest outrage on the feelings of help-
less babes was perpetrated the other day in Mel-
bourne on the departure of retiring Governor-
General Northcote. In the words of the morning
paper —
On and about* Princes Bridge a throng of about 3000
school children had gathered. At the approach of Lord
and Lady Northcote they were formed into an open
square. In the centre a flag was raised, and the children
saluted bareheaded. A band which attended them played
the National Anthem, which the children sang with vigor.
The carriage was stopped. Mr. Sachse, Minister for Edu-
cation, was among the children, and Lady Northcote
requested as a favor tliat he would grant a holiday on a
suitable date for the State schools throughout Victoria.
Mr. Sachse promised favorable consideration of the sug-
gestion. The little ones requested the Minister to inform
Lord Northcote that the children of Victoria feared God
and honored the King, and in the presence of the flag
they wished him to take that message to the King. This
Lord Northcote undertook to do.
Never in the whole course of its life has The
Bulletin met a child that would, of its own free
will and without prompting, send a message of that
description to a middle-aged gentleman that it had
never even seen. If this paper ever did meet
such an infant, it would raise its fatherly gamp on
high and, taking careful aim, smite the degenerate
furiously on the half way down. And it wouldn’t
feel any remorse about it e.ther —unless it
.happened to break the gamp.
A Sydney property-owner found himself
the unwilling landlord of one Rock recently, and
had to use legal dynamite to shift the obstruction.
“ Propputy’s” agonised tale (as told to Justice
Cohen) was that, his house being vacant, Rock got
hold of the keys, elected himself non-paying
tenant, and subsequently “refused to do any-
thing ”in the way of moving. Seemingly, being
Rock, he just deposited himself, and awaited the
legal shifting-powder with ponderous muteness.
* * * *
The jealousy between Sydney and Mel-
bourne is brotherly love compared to that which
exists between Wellington and Auckland in Maori-
land. The trouble dates back to the days when
Wellington collared the capital site from Auck-
land. Wellington calls itself “ The Empire City ”
—whatever that may mean—and Auckland re-
torts by dubbing itself the “ Queen City ” —which
is more understandable. Kipling came along and
threw dynamite on the troubled flames by address-
ing the northern city as “last, loneliest, loveliest,
exquisite, apart,” and so forth, and Wellington
never quite got over that. Then Wellington com-
menced to boom with a mighty roar, and to
soar upwards in population, commercial import-
ance. and swelled head. But Auckland,
although it didn’t soar quite so fast, still went
upwards sufficientlv fast to keep ahead of
Wellington. When" the Yankee Fleet was first
announced, Wellington clamored that the capital
was the place it ought to visit, but the White
Armada announced that time was limited, and
therefore Auckland would be the door at which
it would hand in its card. Wellington lost the
power of speech for nearly a fortnight, and when
it recovered, it arose with dignity and said it was
real glad that the squadron was going to Auckland.
It was just the sort of squadron Auckland
deserved. The men were badly disciplined and
mad and drunk and possessed by a devil, and
every place the armada had called at on the
voyage was mighty glad to see the tail of it disap-
pearing over the horizon. Then Wellington sat
down on its tail to think of some more nasty things
to say.
J • * * *
St. Kilda (Melb.) Council, the other day,
got the answer it deserved when it asked Minister
Cameron to repair, ornament and improve the
local pier, and then hand it over, holus-bolus, so
that the Council might charge Id. or 2d. a head
for promenading thereon. Cameron, of course,
refused ; but the blessed Council, having heard of
the Hinglish practice of charging, still hankers
after the trifle of revenue —at State expense —and
will presently come cadging again. St. Kilda
Council is about the most incompetent adminis-
trative body in the Commonwealth. Years ago it
traded away its tramway rights for a trifle ; it did
the same with its gas rights ; it has no market;
its town hall is in such an unfinished, dilapidated
state that it earns hardly a penny of revenue; it
was too big an ass to build the tramline that Bent
afterwards built, and so lost considerable re-
venue; it has chivvied the public off its fore-
shore to make room for merry-go-rounds, wheels,
and other follies; it has handed over its bath
sites to private speculators ; and now it wants the
State to put the pier in order so that it can
skirmish up a shilling or two for the dog-man’s
wages. If St. Kilda Council had made the most
of its natural advantages and position, it might
be the model municipality of the State, with
hardly any direct taxation ; but apparently the
ratepayers like to be taxed, or they wouldn’t have
put the men they have done into the Council.

* « * *
Some men have no luck. A train runs
to Oodnadatta, in Central Australia, but once a
fortnight, and then it only runs in a moderate
way of speaking. Also, if you managed to escape
beingkilled by the train that runs (in the gradual
way of speaking) once a fortnight, you would have
to wander on 1200 miles or so before you got in the
way of another one. And yet, with all that time
and space for dodging, a man found himself in
trouble with a train at Oodnadatta, the other day,
and all the rest of his life he will carry round a
maimed hand as a memento of his shocking luck.
THE RAILWAY TRAM.
An intelligent Government having placed Sydney's “ Central ” Railway Station on the top
of a huge knob of earth at the far south end of the city, this is the way the Ashfield and
Burwood resident starts his journey home.
The curse of drink again. That well-
known Sydney man who stands charged with
lifting £2OOO, and who, it is alleged, fitted
out a schooner for Guam via the Islands,
took 10 gallons of whisky along with the
“trade.” Now the “trade” schooner isn’t
allowed to carry spirits to the simple Islander;
also there is a limit to the ammunition, and the
amateur trader stocked up with an excess of car-
tridges. So there was trouble with the Customsover
a clearance, and, while the matter was being fixed
up, three days slipped by —and then the arrest
happened.
A war of some dimensions is raging in
North Queensland. The Rifle Association is the
cause ; the clubs are the belligerents. Charters
Towers influence, it is declared, has been domi-
nating affairs in the past, and Townsville has
waded in to burst up the alleged monopoly.
Occasionally the clubs radiating about the two
centres meet, and resolve and generally declare
that what the other set has been doing is hereby
rescinded and demolished. The commotion
reached its top at a special meeting called at the
Towers, the other day, when two chaiimen took
two chairs, and each said his chair was the only
true and cons’tooshnal piece of furniture. The
secretary didn’t know where he was, and until he
could find out he refused to read the minutes.
Legal opinions were produced which were, of
course, at variance ; and the gathering ended in
blank cartridge. Now the Minister for Defence
is being implored to settle the trouble; and, if
these rifle-club bodies are to be taken seri-
ously, the trouble can’t be settled too suddenly.
Nor can the circumstances which make such
trouble possible.
If Sydney is going to have a tramway
strike, it may as well have it during the visit of
the U.S. fleet as at any other time. There is a
dreadful fear that the system will break down
during that period of excitement, and make Sydney
a laughing stock. More electric power is wanted,
and the officials had the importing disease so
badly that they couldn’t think of having the
new machinery made in Australia ; and now the
British contractors have failed badly, as per usual,
and the machinery, that was to be on hand at
latest two months before the fleet came, isn’t
likely to arrive till three months after it is gone.
If the cars are running at the time of the great
spree they will have to stand a huge extra strain,
and it is quite probable that for want of power
the system will snap like a carrot, and Sydney
will be a by-word and a reproach. But if the cars
are not running they can’t break down. And it
would be more respectable to show our visitors a
strike in good working order than a tram system
that wouldn’t work at all. In fact, it might be
worth while to pay the men to strike that week,
and allow them 6d. a day extra for rioting. Un-
less there was some rioting the Yankees might
refuse to believe that the strike was real.
THE DIGNITY OF LABOR.
The white population in the Transvaal, says the corres-
pondent of the London Dailt Telegraph, seems to have
already been stirred up, and navvying by whites is no
longer looked upon as an impossible spectacle. Indeed,
extensive drainage operations have lately been carried
out by European labor in the streets of Johannesburg.
The track laying in the neighboring colony in connec-
tion with the linking up of the Bloemfontein-Kimberley
railway line was done by about sixty poor whites, and
they maintained the good average of lj miles of rails laid
per' day, while their record was If miles. One hammer-
man who came especially under notice was, until very
recently, in a good position as an accountant. There
were three accountants in the gang, a blacksmith, a
watchmaker, and a number of Dutch farmers. — Mel-
bourne Herald.
THE ROMISH EDITOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rome was making its power felt even in the newspaper
world. It had gripped the London Turns. Its columns
were open to all Papal questions under a Roman Gatholic
editor. And the doings of the archbishop and the priests
had a prominent place among the daily and weekly news-
papers.—Extract from Yelloiv Pup oration, S. M. Herald,

The Romish editor is clad
In silken vestments red,
A censer wreathes an odor had
About his mitred head,
And devils scurry in like mad
To earn their daily bread.
His scissors bear the sign of Rome,
His ink is Popisli ink,
And from his nib drips sable foam
Whene’er he stops to think.
The mitre on his thoughtful dome
Vibrates with rage and drink.
With Popish plots his brain is packed.
He writes to Rome : “ We will,
Ere very long, begin to act.
We have our eye on Dill.
He’ll be a bishop—or be racked !
Ha ! ha ! a bitter pill ! ”
The priests play dominoes in pairs,
The cardinals play cards
,(“ Thrippence-a-dozen ”) ’neath the stairs,
While brigand Papal Guards
>Go up and down with dreadful swears,
Disguised as drinky bards.
Year in, year out, the Plotter sits
Wittiin his Romish den,
While with his incense-sodden wits,
And Pope-blesssd, ruffian pen,
He makes, his sly and wicked hits
At good, staunch Orangemen.

ALCOHOL IN MEDICINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Of forty-two samples examined not one was totally free
from it, and several ran up to over 40 per cent., which
means that every patient who took a dose was getting-
more alcohol than if he drank a nip of the average
whisky. ... A tonic “ recommended for the treatment
of the alcohol habit ” panned out 26J per cent., which is
more than would be expected in a liquid beef prepara-
tion. —Sydney Telegraph.

You can pacify the “ missus,”
Likewise keep

With an inward, coward quaking,
And much grief,
Through a Heaven-born specific,
That will quench your thirst terrific—
“ Liquid Beef.”

Come with me ;

For your spree.
If you know a righteous yearning
For repentance, and for turning
A new’ leaf;
Never hesitate to turn it,
Then just tear it out and burn it —
Mine’s a “ Beef.”

Australia is suffering from a rush of pig--
tail. Every time The Bulletin looks out the-
window it sees something shuffling by dressed in
faded pyjamas, and with a tiling that looks like a
thin, dead serpent dangling from the back of its
head. These are understood to be ambassadors
or something from the North, and when the Sydney
daily paper young man goes meekly along to sit
at their feet and interview them, they make
remarks of this description :
In all places where the British flag- flies the Chinese
always appear contented. What wonderful colonisers the
British are 1 My advice to my fellow-countrymen is this :
There is a saying, “ When you are in Rome you must do
as the Romans do,” therefore I say to my fellow-country-
men, “You must at all times remain good and law-
abiding citizens ; you need not then fear any injustice-
under British rule.”
If, after this, The Bulletin’s vegetable John
dares to recite to it: “ Imperial Csesar, dead and
turned to clay, might stop a hole to keep the wind
away,” this paper will immediately take steps to
get its lettuce' somewhere else.

PEAPES (EL CO. LTD.,
“MEN’S OUTFITTERS,”
309-311 George Street, Sydney.
Providence last week gave the Victorian
police a leg-up by delivering into their hands a
gang of untidy housebreakers. The affair began
on a Saturday at midnight, when a South Yarra
woman hurried into the local police station with a
parcel of jewellery, and told the man in charge
that it had fallen from a cab which had driven
away at top speed from a small house in a back
street. The constable, after comparing the valu-
ables with descriptions in the stolen-property list,
made noises for a brother John, and the two set
off in a stealthy manner for the back street. As
they drew near, they heard shouts of “Murder” and
“Police,” so, getting up off their waistcoats (they
had crawled all the way from the station on their
official stummicks to avert suspicion), they rushed
in and found a man on the floor and another
kicking him. The Johns leaped at the pair and
bore them off to the watchhouse, where the kickee
laid a charge of assault against the kicker. The
kickist, who had been shifted without his coat or
vest, sulkily asked the police to send back
for the garments, as he was cold. They did
so, and when the clothes arrived they felt
in one of the vest pockets and discovered a
skeleton key, which key gave rise to suspicions.
The police prospected round some more, and
found bits of burnt jewellery in the untidy fire-
place of the house in the back street, and frag-
ments of broken, jewellery in the untidy yard.
They hadn’t sufficient time just then to crawl up
on the untidy roof, or they would most likely have
found that littered with jewellery also. By the
capture of this gang, the virtue of tidiness has got
another advertisement, for if the thieves hadn’t
got into the habit of dropping the spoil on the
public highway and casting it about the house and
backyard, they would, in all probability, be still
burgling peacefully for their crust. Then the fact
that one person thought the other had stolen the
package of jewellery (which in reality had been
untidily dropped from the cab), and knocked him
down and kicked him under that erroneous im-
pression, and caused the kickee to call out “ Mur-
der” and “ Bigamy,” and things like that—these
matters also contributed to the downfall. Even
the Victorian police will walcemp and do some-
thing if you shriek “Murder 1 ” long enough.

“ D.M.W.” his reply :
Rats to the theory of “Mabel Holmes" (B. 11/6/’08)
that the colored male possesses “ an irresistible attrac-
tion ” for the white female! The white man consorts
with the colored woman the world over, if you like ; but
the reverse is about as common as white hair in a girl of
15, or any other unnatural phenomenon of the sort.
The greaser races of South and Central America
are the product of a blending of European and savage.
How many Spanish women mated with Indians? There
are and always have been plenty of half-caste niggers in the
United States. How many of them have white mothers?
Maoris and whites have for long lived nearly on an equality
in Maoriland. How many Maoris have white wives?
I’m all with “ Mabel Holmes ” in her wish to see niggers
banned from white men’s countries, and that for a thou-
sand good and sufficient reasons. But it isn’t a good or
any other sort of sane reason to barrack for exclusion on
the ground that women of our color cherish a mad,
dangerous tendency to marry yellow and dusky men.
They don’t (except in the rare cases where they are hope-
less perverts), and to hurl such a generalisation at them
amounts to what ought to be an indictable libel.
A while ago the Australian press pub-
lished a cablegram from London which was to the
effect that some authorities (unnamed) considered
the submarine “not adapted” for Aus-
tralian defence. The impression con-
veyed was that the submarine was a de-
licate little thing only fit for pottering
about harbors. Now comes the news
that 17 of the craft have steamed from
Dover to the Firth of Forth, a distance
of 415 miles—under water, except for the
conning towers—at a speed of 10 knots,
and this paper feels moved to inquire why
the submarine is “not adapted” for Aus-
tralian waters. Those 17 dots rippling
along the surface of the sea would make a
hostile admiral a lot more nervous than
the sight of seventeen ships of the class
Bull keeps in this section of the ocean,
and if they can navigate the North Sea,
it is up to the authorities we heard about
to explain why they couldn’t struggle
along in the Tasman Sea also. A dark
suspicion begins to float around that some
“authorities ” in England, in their desire
to discourage the creation of any marine
force that won’t be directly under British
orders, are prepared to declare indis-
criminately that battleships, cruisers, tor-
pedo boats, and sub-marines are all “ not
adapted ” for Australian waters —unless
Bull owns them, that is. Some day we
may even hear that big guns won’t stand
the climate on the Australian coast.
The Bulletin’s Oldest Inhabitant grows
garrulous :
Another lover-poisoning episode occurred last week in
aristocratic Toorak (Melbourne). Charles Groves, a farm
laborer, aged 22 years, had been “keeping company’
with Mary WalkingtOn, aged 23, a domestic employed at
Mrs. Murchison’s private hospital for the insane, “Clover-
dale,” near the old Government House grounds, Toorak.
The man gave the girl (of whom he was jealous) strychnine
in chocolates, and then took some himself. Both are
dead. “Cloverdale” in the early ’fifties was built by
Edward Arnold, a bookseller of 56 Elizabcth-street, Mel-
bourne. The quaint little shop still stands, buttressed by
Altson’s newly-erected tobacco shop and chambers. That
particular corner (half an acre in extent) was known in
early days as “ No. l,” and, when this scribe was employed
in Arnold’s book store next door, half a century ago, was
also known as “ Cusbmore’s Corner.” At the first land
sale the half-acre was bought by Mr. James Ross for £32,
the purchaser entering into a bond to erect a “ substantial
building valued at £2O within two years.” Mr. Ross, to
oblige certain friends, consented to cut up the half-acre,
and in May, 1839, the lots brought at auction £650. In
1888 the bit of ground was valued at £493.500. In the
late ’fifties Mr. Arnold sold “ Cloverdale ” to John William
Bogg, a partner in the wool firm of J. H. Clough and Co.
Mr. Bogg lived in the house for some years until Clough
and Co. dissolved. Mrs. Arnold was a daughter of Mr.
Perry, the first to establish a plants nursery in Victoria,
on the road near Heidelberg. William Perry, of the old
auction firm of Symons and Perry, was a son of the
nurseryman. Arnold sold off his book business in JB6l
and went with his wife to Dunedin (M.L.). He was an
artist by profession and a good draughtsman. In Dune-
din he established a photographic business and did well.
Picking up a picture of “ Old Otagoites”in the’sixties,
I find Arnold and many other old Melbournites—Vincent
Pvke, Brodie, etc.—amongst the number. And mean-
while Charles Groves and Mary Walkington are still dead.
For downright flat perjury a maritime
collision case is supposed to hold the record. If
one side produces 12 witnesses to solemnly declare
that a vessel’s lights were showing with a dazzling
brilliance, the other easily musters 14 to prove
that they were all dead out. But the cases under the
N.S.W. Liquor Act dealing with sly-grog selling
are going strongly to beat the sailor men’s record.
Thus Smithers, S.M., was called upon last week
to decide a sly-grog case. A constable swore that
he called with a “ friend ” at a house in Redfern
(Sydney), at 1 o’clock on Sunday, June 21. He
asked for drinks, was served with beer, and paid
9d. His “friend” went into the box, swore that
he shouted for the bobby, and paid 9d. The police
swore they visited the house, and found a keg of
beer. As a side issue, the friend of the police
further swore that the beer-seller called on him
and tried to square it up. The occupier of the
house then went into the box. He swore that he
had never seen the previous witnesses in his life,
had never sold them beer, and hadn’t sold anybody
beer. He was a kind person, and at the request of
the local football cl übused to mind their beer. After
him about seven witnesses swore that they were
members of alocal football club. On Saturday nights
they would buy a keg of beer. The defendant
would receive it, and on the Sabbath they would
go to his place for a quiet drink. They all
severally swore—(l) Defendant did not sell them
beer; (2) the beer was their own beer ; (3) the
police witnesses were not on the premises at 1 p.m.
on June 21, but they (the footballers) were.
In face of this galaxy of talent, Smithers, S.M.,
was speechless for a bit. Then, remarking that
there was artistic lying by one side or the other,
he fined the defendant £5O for luck.

The Prahran Chronicle (Melbourne) re-
cords a complaint made by Malvern Council
against Tait’s railway economies. Councillor
Edgar remarked that he had to wait in the dark
at Kooyong station because Tait didn’t run to a
light, and when the train came along he was
staggered to find it in charge of one man—appa-
rently Tait had economised by sacking the fireman
and making the driver fire himself. Of course, if
anything happened to the driver, the train would
run wild till it struck something, or died out for
want of steam ; and Tait ought to know better
than that in a district where rich men reside.
Knox of the Hat dwells somewhere about
Malvern.
**.*•*
It is really time this crime of starving
infants and other valuable Victorian citizens was
taken in hand seriously. So far the Courts have
been regarding it as a playful little trick of the
trade, to be met with a fine of a few shillings.
But, as Chairman Dr. Norris told the Board
of Health the other day, of 212 samples of alleged
milk captured during the past six months in Mel-
bourne and suburbs, no less than 61 were found on
analysis to be adulterated. The community
which is content to have that proportion of swindle
in relation to its most important article of food
supply hardly deserves to be alive.
Queensland Supreme Court Justice Real
has decided that Thomas Keane is defunct; but
for all that Thomas may not be nestling in
Abraham’s bosom. The story that the Judge
listened to was a bewildering mixture. In 1893
Keane, a laborer, of Maryborough (Q,), left for
Sydney. Before going he gave his Government
Savings Bank pass book to a friend, William
Clancy, of Goomeri (Q.), with a request to send it
to his father in Ireland should anything happen
to him. There was a credit of £156 in the book.
Since then neither Clancy nor Keane’s relatives
in Ireland have heard from him. It is alleged,
however, that upon reaching Sydney he went into
partnership as a builder with one Neville, but
neither Keane nor Neville has been found.
In 1899 a son of Clancy read in a paper
that a Thomas Keane had been admitted
to the Plague Hospital in Sydney, and had
died there. This caused Clancy the elder to leap
to the conclusion, without any apparent reason,
that the cadaver was Keane of the pass-book. In
1905, Clancy went for a trip to Ireland, and
handed the book over to Keane’s father, and toldl
him of Keane’s disappearance and alleged passing-
out. The old man had fresh inquiries made im
Sydney, and it was discovered that the plague-
stricken Keane had not died, but had been dis-
charged cured. This Keane wrote, alleging that
he was the real Keane of the pass-book. The*
solicitors sent him a list of questions to answer to>
prove his identity, but he didn’t reply to them.
The final act came, the other day, when the
Queensland Deputy Curator of Intestate Estates
applied for letters of administration for the £156
and £B5 9s. Id. interest which has since accrued,
and Justice Real decided that a man who left that
amount of money lying around unclaimed was
dead enough, anyway, and granted the appli-
cation.

By “Green Tape, Junior” :
Belmore, a small Sydney suburb with a large voice,
sent its inhabitants to interview Railway Commissioner
Johnson last week. They wanted (1) more trains and (2)
bigger trains. They could not go to Belmore often
enough, and when they did go they had to travel in
heaps, the trains were so crowded. Johnson, in reply,
was dubious, to put it mildly, and he gave this extraordi-
nary reason : “ Notwithstanding the facilities at the new
Sydney station, it is almost impossible to get an additional
train in or out of the station at certain hours of the day.”
Shades of Owe’Sullivan 1 The colossal white elephant,
the cynosure of the Southern Hemisphere, hasn’t
been open more than a couple of years, and
already it can’t grapple with the ordinary business of
the railway service ! What on earth will it do in ten years”
time? Perhaps Owe’Sullivan will be Premier by then !'
If he is, he had better pass a law ordering the populace
to start to go home at 3 p.m., and to go to work before'
daylight. Or he may have to declare that the people are'
to go in every other day, or that those who go in to work:
to-day can’t go home until to-morrow. Ye gods ! The'
Central Railway Station, which is to carry the name of;
Owe’Sullivan down the ages, is already too small to carry
the traffic to poor little insignificant Belmore 1
THE PLAIN GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bishop Green, of Ballarat, earnestly appeals to the
local young man to give less regard to girls who are
pretty, and marry those who are good.
Listen to the proposition
That a bishop puts in prose :
Care not for her mere condition,
Or the color of her nose ;
Do not mind the size and brightness
Of the eyes beneath her hood ;
Disregard her buoyant lightness—
Wed a maiden who is good !
There is hope in solid ankles,
And in hair of sunset tint;
Even though the cross-eye rankles,
There is virtue in a squint.
Scorn the deep, deceptive dimple,
Heed the goodly bishop’s strain.
Dames and maids are always simple—
Always artless when they’re plain !
Dicken ! Mr. Bishop, Dicken !
No safe rule is to be had.
She who’s freckled and no chicken
May be more than pretty bad.
If she’s lovely, true, some flat may
Carry oil your wife from you ;
But, who knoweth ? Even that may
Be a great advantage too.
VIC.
S.S.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N.S.W. State Bank.—Officially this is
known as the Government Savings Bank ot N.S.W.
Originally it was worked in connection with the Post
Office ; but it was taken out of the eye of the Postmaster-
• General on March 1, 1901, when the postal and tele-
graphic services were transferred to the Commonwealth.
For six years the business was administered from the
Treasury, which paid a commission to the Post Office for
that part of the work which it continued to transact.
This period was, on the whole, not one of much pros-
perity ; and the reserves were pretty nearly swamped.
Subsequently, as from the end of 1900, the bank was put
under the control of a Board, with an experienced banker
(Alex. Mclntosh, ex-general manager of the Bank of
New Zealand) as president ; and the heavy loss of the
previous years was promptly turned into a profit. The
proceedings have been briefly as follows :
Profit. Reserves.
Dec., 1900 .. .. £25,008

„ 1906 .. .. 21,663 16,175
Dec., 1906 .. .. 4,484 11,691
