posit with Commonwealth, £5OlB J
The only explanation given for the heavy losses made in
1904-5-6 was that the commission charged by the Postal
Department was excessive. The new management
doesn’t repeat this State grievance; instead it set
to work to straighten things up, and the result
is shown above. As a matter of fact, the com-
mission paid to the Post Office is no more
than 4s. 6d. per cent, on deposits and withdrawals. It
wasn’t the Commonwealth that was victimising the un-
fortunate Ma State —it was, among others, the Private
Enterprise person who, to dodge ordinary bank exchange,
made use of the Savings Bank to effect his remittances ;
and it was mainly to get at this enterprising gentleman
that the Board a while ago took power to charge the de-
positor for keeping his account. In 1904-5, when the bank’s
loss was very heavy, the interest earnings averaged £3 6s.
9d. per cent, on the mean funds ; for last year the average
was £3 ss. 3d. That shrinkage, however, was counter-
balanced by a reduction in the interest paid and credited,
the average being £2 15s. 9d. per cent, on the mean of the
funds, as against an excessive £3 ss. 2d. per cent, in 1904-
1905. A concern that earned £3 6s. 9d. per cent, and
paid out in interest £3 ss. 2d. percent., surely couldn’t
be surprised that it was losing money. But instead
of increasing the margin between interest earnings and
interest payments, a State grievance was made o.ut of
the fact that the Commonwealth charged the State a
commission of less than J per cent, for transacting the
Post Office agency business.
When the change was made in 1906, an Ad-
vances Department was created to straighten up and ex-
tend the operations of the Advances to Settlers Board.
The latter began on a wrong basis. As part of the Lands
Department it was regarded as a semi-charity. Men
went to it as others go to the Prospecting Board in con-
nection with the Mines Department—that is, when they
didn’t have good enough security to take to the ordinary
financier. It is alleged that when the Advances Department
took over the Board’s affairs, there was a profit balance
(reserves) of £14,320. But the arrears of instalments
under the old Act amount to £30,989, while under the
new Act they are only £7O. Apparently these old arrears
have not been valued ; consequently, when the bad debts
among them have been written off, the alleged accumu-
lated profit on the working of the Advances to Settlers
Board will pretty certainly be all wiped out. Under the
new Act the Government guarantees the Board against
loss or deficiency in connection with these old advances.
Nominally at Dec. 31 this was the position of the Advances
Department:—
Reserves.

The £106,025 advanced during the year was in 424 ad-
vances. Even allowing that the early part of the year
was consumed in preliminaries, and that 287 approved
applications were awaiting acceptance or completion of
securities at the end of the year, the business actually
transacted can only be regarded as disappointing, con-
sidering all the recent talk about land settlement. The
explanation must lie between these two things : (1) The
private banks and mortgage cos. are offering better terms
or more prompt assistance than the bank ; (2) in spite of
all the talk, there has been very little actual land-settle-
ment, and there haven’t therefore been the clients for the
State Bank to do business with.

The disclosed profits of this bank have increased rapidly
during the past few years. Possibly after the big troubles
the cleaning-up was not very thorough ; in that case
more or less writing down remained to be done out of
subsequent profits. That might account for the com-
paratively poor returns shown for several years. The
actual balance-sheet figures certainly show no very tre-
mendous changes. Ten years ago the advances totalled
£5,208,896 ; they now amount to £5,981,612. The in-
crease in quantity certainly wouldn’t account fora spring
in profits from £24,380 to £50,063. The most obvious con-
clusion is that the advances 10 years ago included many un-
remunerative assets which have since been written down,
and probably got rid of, while profitable business has
been secured to take their place. It is just as well to
remember now and again that the unprofitable business
of 10 years ago was no doubt very remunerative business
16 or 18 years ago ; and what looks highly profitable
business now—business that is highly profitable, so far as
current revenue is concerned—may turn out shockingly
unprofitable a few years hence. In that case there may
be another drain on the profits in order to write down
assets and fill up holes in the accounts. In the past four
years some outrageous prices have been paid for country
lands: and, though banks and other financial concerns
haven’t lost their heads as they did when city and subur-
ban lands boomed 18 or 20 years ago, it seems almost im-
possible for them to escape some trouble. Certainly a
smash in the values of country lands won’t be as
heartbreaking a business as the other smash was; for
while most country land can be put to some use, there
isn’t any deader weight in all the world than the unused
suburban allotment. Nobody can teil from a published
balance-sheet how prudent or imprudent a bank manage-
ment was during the preceding six or 12 months. All
that can be said is that in the past two or three years
any Australian mortgage-dealing concern could very
easily have laid the foundations for a large amount of
future trouble.
Getting back to the Bank of Victoria in par-
ticular, the principal items now and 12 months ago
compare this way:—

The fairly large increase in advances looks all right; but
it isn’t altogether satisfactory. For this increase in
advances has been accompanied by a considerable
reduction in the amount of public money on hand.
The increase in the advances and in the premises
account during the 12 months, added to the re-
duction in public money (deposits, etc.), totals
£524,378; of this the undivided profits provided £26,084,
leaving £498,294 to come out of the liquid, assets. The
latter have thus been reduced until they represent only
23 percent, of the total debt to the public. The free de-
posits (current account money) alone total £1,654,561;
the liquid assets amount to only £1,441,549. The pro-
portion of liquid assets is too low ; yet it is quite likely
that to increase it the profits will have to be cut into.
Still, while the bank is not as well placed as it ought to
be to meet any sudden trouble, its big share capital is a
strong guarantee for the depositors and other creditors ;
the total liabilities to the public amount to only£3l2s. 6d.
for every £1 of the shareholders’capital in the business.
Largely, no doubt, because of having the use of only that
small proportion of public money, the profits are no bet-
ter than moderate. Calculated on the basis of capital
and reserves, they have lately run as follows :

“Profits—less loss on Assets Trust,” the first column
says ; and that introduces us at once to the skeleton in the
ice-chest. In 1893 the least productive of the assets were
turned over to a Trust, which was practically a new de-
partment of the bank, the object being to let the public
«-et a fair view of any progress the bank might make in
the matter of new business. In brief, it was decided to
put the skeleton in the ice-chest instead of keeping it
nailed up on the front door-post. The bank acted wisely;
for, the skeleton being kept out of sight, the business of
the new bank steadily picked up. The new bank—to all
intents and purposes a new bank, anyway—had, of
course, to make itself responsible for the debts (de-
posits, etc.) which the badly-depreciated assets re-
presented. Part of the depreciation and loss was
made good by calling up capital and forfeiting it (that
is to say, shareholders found out, as they always are
liable to do, that contributing shares are a loaded gun,
liable to go off when the holder can least stand the shock).
The balance of the loss or depreciation has to be made
good out of the bank’s profits. There is very little ordi-
nary 7 share capital left to write off ; in all there are now
only 191,088 ordinary shares, fully paid to 10s.—all that
is left of the old original lot of 300,000 shares of £lO each,
the whole of which was eventually called up.
But in spite of this tremendous blotting out of
shareholders’ capital, the unprovided-for losses and
shrinkages were still very serious. In 1901, when general
manager Heron was appointed (he is just now taking his
first “ breather”in the shape of a European trip), he made
a valuation of the Trust’s assets, and decided that there
was still a deficit of £1,534,903. In the seven years since
then a fair amount of realisation has been done ; using
the 1901 figures as a basis, things have gone like this
Dec., ’Ol. Dec., ’O7. June, ’OB.
Liabilities of Trust £3,334,903 £2,508,214 £2,382,172
Assets of Trust .. 1,800,000 1,120,000 995,000
Deficit of Trust .. £1,534,903 £1,388,214 £1,387,172
Less Bank Reserves 27,619 267,406 282,661
Net Deficit .. £1,507,284 £1,120,808 £1,104,511
At least, they have gone like that on the surface. But
18 months ago the chairman, being asked how the general
manager’s valuation was panning out, submitted some
figures, from which it appeared that in five years £479,566
of assets had realised £24,186 over the valuation. In the
past 18 months assets valued at £335,000 have been sold ;
and if £24,186 were realised above the valuation on the
first lot, it is pretty certain that more has been realised
on account of the swollen prices of the past 18 months.
As assets have been realised, old deposits have been
bought up. In July, 1902, they were worth 13s. 3d.; in
July, 1904, 135.; 1906,155. 6d.; 1907, 17s. 9d.; and at pre-
sent, 17s. 6d. What has become of the discounts? As
shown above, in 6J years the liabilities have been reduced
by £952,731. The assets (general manager’s valuation)
have been reduced by £805,000. In other words, £805,000
of assets (G.M.’s valuation in 1901) have got rid of
£952,731 of debt. But whether that includes the £50,000
(or possibly more) of receipts over and above the valua-
tion, or whether an internal reserve has been created to
safeguard the rest of the valuation, there is no way of
ascertaining. The chances are that the man who made
the valuation, being also general manager, has put some-
thing up his sleeve so that he may keep his valuations on
the right side in the end. But when H. L. Heron puts
something up his sleeve he is the last man in Melbourne
to mention it.

The Commercial of Australia is one of the very few banks
which show smaller advances. It may be that the bank
didn’t have the money (the deposits have also shrunk) ;
but the liquid assets represent no less than 45 per cent,
of all the liabilities to the public. There are only one
or two other Australian banks in so strong a position
in that respect. It seems, therefore, that it isn’t from
want of money that the bank hasn’t lent out more money ;
it looks more as though the bank has been a bit afraid of
recent conditions, and preferred to keep its resources for
a while earning nothing. And since so large a proportion
of its deposits consists of call-money, carrying no interest,
it can afford to do that better than most banks.

At the shareholders’ meeting, the othe’r day, somebody
asked how it came about that the profits were always just
about the same. As .'’ill be seen from the table, when
the bank paid 4 per cent, the profits were £9351, £9052,
and £9045 in a half-year. Then, when things in general
became more prosperous, the dividend was cut down to
24 per cent., and in spite of the general prosperity the
profits were £6158, £6060, £6066, and £6049. Asked to
explain this, the chairman said that shareholders must
not forget that the bank was making internal reserves.
In other words,. shareholders must not forget that
the published figures were of absolutely no value as
a guide to current business, and very little value as
an indicator of the present position of the concern.
The trouble certainly didn’t begin with the present
management. When the bank made heavy losses in the
boom time, it muddled through somehow, and didn’t face
the situation and provide for the losses. Instead, it pre-
tended (on paper) that it didn’t have losses—pretended
that, even when its shares were at over 50 per cent, dis-
count. Not having frankly faced the situation, it has
now to provide for the losses out of profits instead of out
of capital. It was wretchedly bad policy. Few people
remember now that the Commercial Bank of Sydney
wrote off capital ; but it did, and so it pays a 1 "l per cent,
dividend, and makes fine progress, with over i 5 millions
of deposits. But the City—well, few remember that
the City didn’t close its doors. The fact that it
didn’t is worth nothing Whatever to it in the way
of advertisement. But while the fact that it didn’t re-
construct is worth nothing to it, the fact that it only
pays a 3 per cent, dividend is a standing advt. of the
other sort. The public is no doubt very foolish in pre-
ferring to trust or “do with” a bank simply because it
pays a big div. ; but the simple, if lamentable, fact is,
that 95 times out of 100 the big dividend pays. The
National Bank of Australasia put up its dividend re-
peatedly a few years ago, when the figures didn’t justify
the increases ; and business rushed to it. The City
Bank, with some of its old losses still hanging to its neck,
has to crawl along on the 2£ per cent, and 3 per cent,
mark ; and it is thereby handicapped terribly.
Compared with 12 months ago, this is how
things look at present: —
June, ’O7. June, ’OB.
Notes .. .. £93,449 £98,502 Inc. £5,053
Deposits, etc. .. 1,233,382 1,253,353 Inc. 19,971
Liquid assets .. 451,737 335,496 Dec. 116,241
Advances .. .. 1,148,542 1,289,402 Inc. 140,860
Premises .. .. 144,596 147,049 Inc. 2,453
Advances have increased by £140,860, and premises
account by £2453: together £143,313. Of this only
£19,971 has been provided by increased deposits. Five-
sixths of the total increase of advances have been made
by reducing the liquid assets ; wherefore the latter have
been so heavily drawn upon that they now represent only
25 per cent, of the total amount owing to the public.
However, the proportion of current account money is
smaller, no doubt, than it is with most banks;
wherefore, less than the average proportion of
liquid assets may be allowable in this case.
Still, it is fairly clear that the bank is already working
about to the limit of its present resources. What real
progress it is making, and how far the book-value of the
assets is from real value, it is impossible to say. Pre-
sumably the directors know’; but neither shareholders
nor the investing public, with nothing but the published
statements to go by, can form any certain opinion. To
those alleged internal reserves that the chairman talked
about. £lOOO or £lO,OOO a year may be passed—there is
no wav of telling which. Certainly the advances have
only increased in 10 years from £1,222,764 to £1,289,402 ;
but that is no test, because “ £1,222,764,” when printed in
this bank’s balance-sheet, may really have meant £750,000.
Wherefore The Bulletin once more says that the law
which allows of the issue of such bank balance-sheets is
really too foolish to be at large, since its folly constitutes
a public danger. * #

The extra dividend at the rate of 2 per cent, per annum
has been paid by w’ay of a bonus, which doesn’t commit

has undergone one change. For very many years the co.

the difference between payments and receipts amounting
to £265 or £275 a half-year. That Old Man of the, Boom
has now been got rid of; the final payment (£785) under
the old lease was charged to Profit and Loss in December
last. This term, apparently to celebrate the event, a
5 per cent, bonus (£429) is paid to the staff ; in the fore-
going table this amount has been deducted from profits.

In this comparison the past half-year’s expenses have not

has now’ been conducted for 30 years—is large and profit-
able ; but it gets comparatively little assistance from the
investments. As previously mentioned, the co. went
into Collins-street during the boom, and put £70,000
into premises. These have now been written dow’n to
£65,000; yet last term they only returned £9l net,
in addition to providing the co. with its own office
. accommodation. If a reasonable amount for rent
were added to the expenses, a few pence w’ould have to
be added to the 3s. per cent, of trust estates shown
above ; but still the record w’ould be among the very best
in Australia. Nevertheless the co. does not aim at cheap-
ness : the staff appears to be given more than ordinary
consideration, and that is a point of importance in con-
nection with a trustee co. The shareholder, with a nose
for dividends, may want economy ; the client, however,
wants efficiency above all things, seeing that the trustee
co. only comes in when he goes to some place from whicn
personal supervision is difficult, not to say impossible.

The directors don’t say a word in their report about t e
notice which they received from the Perth City ' jOU “
awhile ago relating to the transfer of the works from tn
co. to the municipality. The Council unquestionably l
the right to take the works over; what is disputed
whether the co. has the right to make the city pay i r
the goodwill of the business. The co. has had a remarK-
able record. Up to 1898 it had 100,000 shares ; m that
year 50,000 more were issued, and 6s. Bd. was paid, upon
upon them. In eight years 245. 9d. was paid in divs. on
these shares, of which 13s. 4d. was called back and applied
to pay up the shares to £l. Thus in those eight years
the consumers paid up two-thirds of the capital and pro-
vided in addition an average dividend of about per
cent, on the cash actually put up by the share-
holders ; and now the consumers are paying 20 per cent,
all round, on the two-thirds they provided as well as
upon the one-third the shareholders themselves provided.
Private Enterprise has had a few “ soft things in Aus-
tralia, but it is doubtful if it ever before had anything
quite so “soft” as this. The co. has an electric light as
well as a gas department. The former had a re Yf” u ?,?‘
£23,481 last half-year; the gas department, £11,246.
Apportioning the general capital expenditure between
the two departments, the electric light business last hail
year appears to have returned about 19 per cent, net and
the gas business about 14 per cent. net. The amounts
shown as written off are covered by the item Deprecia-
tion on plant and buildings.” Apparently this money is
spent either on replacements or on additions to countei -
balance the general wear and tear. 1 os ® lb *y •
however, repairs and replacements are covered by
“ manufacture and distribution.” Naturally, all the
co.’s funds are in the business; cash and bank balances
and book debts at May 31 totalled £15,390, and the lia-
bilities were £1996. And the co. proposes to go on
extending, and has more electric-plant additions on
order. The Council’s right of resumption apparently
isn’t limited as to time: the co.’s Act, passed in 188 b,
gave it a 20 years’ life certain ; after that, the Council
can come in at any time on six months] notice. The price
to be paid is to be settled by arbitration ; but, as men-
tioned above, the lawyers can’t agree as to the basis on
which the arbitrators should proceed.

Broken Hill moves in spasms. In 1903-4 the co.’s total re-
venue, apart from investments, was £6093 ; during most
of the past year lead boomed, and Broken Hill didn t
care whether it turned the gas off or not, wherefore this
co.’s revenue reached £11,175. In four years profits more
than doubled ; for the next year or two they will be
shrivelled and shrunk. Fortunately the co. is run by
men who know 7 Broken Hill’s curious little w'ays, so the
directors don’t pay 17 p.c. in dividends because once in a
way the capital happens to show that return gross.
Instead, they continue the old modest 6 p.c., and put
the rest aside, so as to allow for wear and tear later on
when probably the current profits won’t run to a de-
preciation allowance. So far as the published accounts
sho\v, mains, plant and meters- have cost £82,807, and
£6593 has been written off, leaving these assets standing
at £26,214 ; and it is doubtful whether £6593 would cover
the general wear and tear and depreciation. If it w-on t,
then of course some of the profits, as set out above, are
over-stated; also part of the reserves are not true
reserves. In fact, that is to some extent admitted, as
£4950 of the reserves forms a replacement fund. The co.
has no debts except a few small current trade accounts,
while its liquid assets, apart from stocks, total £7803.
The future of this co. is as uncertain as the future of
every other co. which hangs wholly upon the uncertain
metal markets ; and this co.’s future is further obscured
by the recent undertaking 1 of the municipality to run the
local electric light works.

The profits of this co. are remarkably uniform. In fact,
until you look at the figures of some brewing cos., you
can’t have any idea how regular the drinking habits of
the people are. No matter how things go they don t
seem to vary more than a pint or so from year to year.
Certainlv there is another explanation—that these par-
ticular breweries don’t tell you more than they want you
to know, and that, if they make a good profit one year,
they can always find something to do with the extra-
ordinary part of it, in the way of writing down assets,
without showing anything unusual in the balance-sheet.

Mining and engineering men will be interested
in the development that has taken place as regards the
use of Dunlop Conveyor, Elevator, and other rubber belts
in Australia. A few years back, the number of Dunlop
Conveyor belts in use in the Commonwealth would not
have totalled a dozen ; whereas to-day they are numbered
in the hundreds. The Dunlop Rubber Company (who
have depdts in all the States) have, during the past year,
sold large belts that alone totalled up about six miies in
length, and weighed over 80 tons. Some of the belts
supplied weighed over 50001 b. each, and were 600 ft. long.
The success of the Dunlop Co.’s belts has been so re-
markably consistent on nearly all the leading mines that
it is little wonder that the Conveyor belt is coming into
such general use in this country. The Dunlop Company,
who have the best-equipped plant in the Southern
Hemisphere for this class of work, are prepared to supply
belts in any length (endless if desired) up to 40in. wide.
Tenders and samples submitted.*
In the calculation of working costs in concen-
trating mills and workshops, ever}'small item should be
taken into consideration. It is the small and uncon-
sidered factors, involving waste of power, that make for
high working costs. The type of bearing employed has a
marked effect on the power consumed in overcoming
friction. Recent exhaustive tests emphasised the marked
superiority of the Hyatt Flexible Roller Bearings over all
other types of roller, sleeve, and other anti-friction
bearings. Under equal conditions the loss of power due
to friction in Hyatt Flexibles is 23 per cent, less than
with the best solid roller bearing, and 65 per cent, less
than with babbit metal journals. Messrs. W. and J.
Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, will be
glad to supplv further information to those interested.*

* * * *
After conducting exhaustive tests in the con-
centration of the ores at the Broken Hill South mine, the
manager, Mr. Wainwright, has finally decided to instal
the Wilfley type of table, modified in the direction best
adapted to his particular local requirements. The great
advantage with this type of table is that it admits of so
many slight variations which adapt it to meet all classes
of pulverised ores. Provided the pulp for treatment is
graded it can be used to treat all varieties of ore from
slimes up to coarse grades. When treating slimes and
lightly mineralised ores, care must be taken to have the
slope of the table accurate, and a fine, smooth dressing
surface beyond the feather edged diagonal termination of
the riffles. When treating coarsely ground ores or ores
heavily charged with mineral, as at the Broken Hill South,
the riffles or grooves can be carried further along the table
and in some instances right up to the concentrates dis-
charge end. Mr. Wainwright ordered six tables as an
experiment, with fewer riffles or grooves than customary,
and with some of them—the lower ones—carried to the
extreme end of the table. So successful has this type of
Wilfley table proved that he has ordered 50 more from
the Austral Otis Engineering Company. This same firm
has been placing several of the patent Callow Screens at
Broken Hill and elsewhere. This ingenious and simple
mechanism has solved the problem of grading prior to
concentration.* [6

of the machinery plant and stock of the Australian Forge
and Engineering Co. Proprietary, Ltd., Williamstown, has

everything to his engineering works, Footscray.
Machinery merchants, engineers, mining companies, and

catalogues are being prepared and will be posted to any

Barrier Wharf, Ltd., a co. formed mainly in
Melbourne, in 1905, to take over wharf-property at Port
Pirie, where the Barrier silver-lead field does most of its
shipping (the deficit to start with represents preliminary
expenses)
To Re-

The right people got hold of this property—people who
could influence most custom that way : managing direc-
tors and directors of many mining cos., and representa-
tives of big foreign metal-buying firms. The capital to
begin with was £27,350; at present £36,600 is paid up,
leaving 13,400 shares unissued. Of the 36,600 shares,
9000 are “special.” These have been issued from time
to time to “ certain shareholders of the Spencer’s Gulf
Stevedoring Co., to obtain the advantage of their busi-
ness,” and these special shares, according to the directors’
report of November, 1906, carry “special privileges as
regards distribution of profits.” These are evidently pre-
ference shares of some sort; yet Barrier Wharf shares are
dealt in by investors as though there were only an ordi-
nary issue. The co. has hitherto been favored by the
extraordinary prosperity of Broken Hill and the boom
output of metals. The boom is, however, past, and a
shrinkage in this co.’s revenue is pretty certain.
During the boom the co. might very reasonably
have been expected to write off something for de-
preciation of assets ; but all property—even down
to the office mat —remains in at cost. Possibly
there may have been a temporary increase in the value
of some of the assets ; but, even so, where there was de-
preciation it should have been provided for out of boom-
time profits, the more particularly as the property still
carries a mortgage of £lO,OOO. At May 31 there was an
overdraft of £1361; but this was rather more than
counterbalanced by book debts and credit balances in
S. Aus. The present dividend (£2745) will apparently be
paid either out of this current half-year’s profits or out of
overdraft; £702 (4 per cent.) of the dividend credited
above to the past half-year was paid in March. The co.
got hold of a very profitable, though most likely an un-
certain, business ; but it is fairly clear that dividends so
far have been paid up to the very hilt.

Sydney got a hint, last week, thatN. S. Wales
has been having a boom, and that already the boom isn’t
what it was, by a long way. The hint came in the shape
of a notice to attend a meeting of creditors, and the
figures were the biggest that any Sydney meeting has had
to run its eye over for several years past. It was mostly
a coal and shipping business.
* * « *
From Thos. J. Heskett, of the Heskett-Moore
iron and steel process : —The reference made by “ Engi-
neer ” to the business of The Iron, Steel and Metals 00.
(B. 9/7/’oB)is unfortunate in its collection of facts, and in its
representation of events connected therewith. It is not
true that this co. had anything to do with a process pro-
posing to reduce oxide of iron (iron sands) to metal by the
agency of aluminium. The process owned by the co. is not
“ merely a combination of the ordinary reduction pro-
cesses,” but an adaptation of known chemical and metal-
lurgical .principles in a new way to effect important
economic results. Shareholders have received due notice
of all deposits paid on options, viz., the Commonwealth,
Maoriland and Indian rights, and are not likely to be
influenced by “Engineer’s” suggestion of large sums
in the way of forfeits, the outcome of his imagination.
The co. having proved, at a cost of approximately
£30,000, the correctness and practicability of its process
to its. own satisfaction, and also to the satisfaction of
many able experts, is accumulating data as to cost from
actual working operations, a tedious undertaking con-
ducted under great difficulties, but now nearing com-
pletion, to place before Krupp, Carnegie and others in
various parts of the world where patents have been
applied for, and in every case granted. In the meantime
it ill becomes “ Engineer” to sneer at an honest effort to
solve a great problem and to establish a new Australian
industry because the necessary experimental work cannot
be completed in a day or a year. Who is “Engineer,”
anyway ?
*** . *
The annual meeting of Mount Morgan G.M.
Co. was held at Rockhampton (Q.), and took about four-
teen seconds longer than usual. Mostly three minutes
sees the whole thing through, but an old chap, known
colloquially as “Hugh Kalyptus,” got even with the
company at last, and had his say to the tune of fourteen
seconds. The annual meeting of Mt. Morgan for some
years has been the event of the old chap’s life. His
shareholding is a bit.on the small side, but it’s of
vital consequence to him; so for a whole year he
prepares for “the next annual meeting.” For eleven
months in the year he potters about in the bush
amongst rocks, etc. But he bobs up, with his
hair brushed, in time for the annual meeting, armed
with a lot of questions concerning the working of
the mine. Up till the present meeting he had always
had hard luck with his questions. He was not quick
enough to get them in, as all the business was completed
and the meeting closed before he realised that the game
had started. This year, however, he was on the door-
step early, and nailed Captain Richard. The captain
breathed something into his ear, “Hugh Kalyptus” got
up and asserted that he did not want to put any ques-
tions to the meeting, and now the future of Mt. Morgan
is assured.

The old thing, about which The Bulletin has
been talking for weeks, happened in connection with
Charters Towers. The keen local jobber (and practically
all Charters Towers is a jobber) fully 7 discounted probable
developments, and put some stocks up 300 or 400 per
cent. ; and then the outsider came in—and likewise fell
in. New Brilliant Freeholds, Bonnie Dundees, and others
all fell from the high values to which outside buying
bumped them ; and now there will be a lot of weary
waiting to do.
• * * *
Bonnie Dundee, Charters Towers, crushed 270
tons for £1065. Instead of being better, this was poorer
than the previous lot. Besides, people began to realise
that a patch of good stone near the boundary was not
enough to boom shares on ; if the main level picks up the
shoot, things will be different. This reef is supposed to
be the old Queen Cross, but it is very different from the
rich shoot discovered and worked some years since. The
Queen Cross itself has given some dissatisfaction to
London shareholders. Nothing happened at the mine, so
there were no good reports to cable ; but the fact that
shares rose smartly to ss. aroused a good deal of sus-
picion, for the Londoners were quite out of touch with
the Day Dawn excitement.
» * » *
The East Mexican, Charters Towers, is breathing
more freely now that freeholders have accepted paid-up
shares in return for mining rights. One of them (Staple-
ton) exchanged his freehold with the E. Mexican Co. for
another allotment that is an intrusion across the boundary
of the New Brilliant Freehold ground, and some distance
to the east, and ahead of the level that is causing so much
excitement. The East Mexican, having a market boom to
engineer, did well to secure these allotments ; for it was
known that Mills United would have purchased them. It
may well be wondered why the freeholders were not
bought out long since, but things are run on the Towers
to suit big speculators, who have more than one interest
to serve. There are, for instance, 15,000 E. Mexican
shares in one man’s name. The three mines that attract
most attention on the Towers are involuntarily playing
into each other’s hands. When a rise is put up by the
N. B. Freeholds above No. 7, towards the E. Mexican
boundary, it is likely to cause a lot of speculation. The
most recent information is that the reef at the end of
No. 7 is looking very well, and that No. 8 is likely to pick
up the shoot.
* * * *
Charters Towers people got out of their depth
in handling a copper proposition. Up to the end of 1906
the ore raised from their Carrington United was 6600
tons, worth £23,290. A dividend(£2soo) was paid early in
1907, but ever since then things have been drifting, until
even the directors at last admit that they have reached
the end of their tether. The bank overdraft
is about £lO,OOO, and a proposal is now before
the shareholders, offering, on behalf of southern
investors, to take half the mine on a two months’ option at
Is. 3d. per share contributing, and 3s. 6d. for paid ups.
The co.’s issue is 240,000 ss. shares, of which 80,000 are
fully paid up, and the balance paid to Is. Bd. The ore is
picked to between 7 and 9 per cent, copper and 3dwt. or
4dwt. gold, but thesmeltingmixtureavailableisnot a good
one, and the process of reverberatory smelting is far too
slow. Also the cost of handling the material generally is
far too high. Operations have, in consequence, of late been
conducted at a loss, and the advice of two experts has been
sought. Poole, of the Charters Towers School of Mines,
reports that the mine is a good one, but the treatment
much too costly 7. He estimates the cost of rectifying
matters connected with smelting at £3OOO. Shepherd, of
O. proposes to spend £2OOO to put the house in order.
In addition, the cost of development will be considerable.
The general manager was imported from Queen Bee
(N.S.W.), but he has not had a free hand.
* * * *
Centreing the gems with a sieve is the present
method of getting the sapphires from the washdirt at
Sapphiretown (Q ); but a recent arrival from S’Africa,
one of the great Smith family, is making a machine
which aims at a revolution. The weapon is worked by rota-
tion, and is similar to theapparatus successfully used on the
diamond fields of S’Africa. Smith reckons that he will be
able to put through about 50 loads of wash per day, and
will use only a very little water in the process. If the
machine answers, it will be able to work a large area of
ground near Sapphiretown hitherto regarded as too poor
to pay.

The original scheme for the flotation of the Ida
mine, Ma.vtown (Q.), has been modified. The issue is
now 25,000 shares of 10s. each —15,000 to be put on the
market at Is. on application, and Is. a month later ; 6000
fully-paid go to the vendors, and 4000 are held in reserve.
It is intended to sink a new shaft 300 ft. ; also, it will be
necessary to repair and add to the old machinery on the
property. The £l5OO to be raised in the first two months
will not go far for all this, especially in that remote dis-
trict, and further calls will soon be required.

Smith’s Creek tin show (N.Q.) which, since its
discovery in 1902, has been in trouble more than once,
has again gone under. The lode is big but low grade, and,
in spite of fairly economical handling, the management
has been beaten by what is termed the “ low ” price of the
metal —£133. The closing down is said to be temporary
—one of the most indefinite of mining terms.

Mitchell River Antimony Co. (N.Q.) is in
trouble. After having been filled with hope at the pros-
pect of a start at last, Northern shareholders were
shocked to hear recently 7 that all hands had been knocked
off. The explanation now given only goes so far as to say
that the plant was quite ready 7, but McArthur’s repre-
sentative left without having a trial of the process on a
working scale. Is there anything the matter with the
process, or is it the mine ?

“Vigilans” : While boring for water, a strong
current of gas was struck at 3709 ft., some years ago, at
Roma (Q.). This was stored in a gasometer, and the town
was lit up by the gas for six weeks, when the bore col-
lapsed. The business cost the council £7OOO. The Roma
Mineral Oil. Co. is now putting down a bore some yards
away from the old orifice, and is down about 3000 ft.
Shares are not to be had.
* * * *
It is just 50 years since gold was first discovered
in Bananaland, then part of N.S.W., but separated the
following year. Canoona, in the Rockhampton district,
was the scene of this discovery 7. It was only 7 a small field,
but was rushed by 7 many thousands, who, at that bad old
time, were mostly engaged stampeding from one magnet
of attraction to another in East Australia. Chappie, a
Cornish miner, was credited with the honor of first find.
He had been prospecting about Gladstone for sometime,
and struck the alluvial patch at Canoona Station. After
a few nuggets were exhibited in Sydney shop windows
every available sailing craft was headed for Port Curtis,
filled with men, some of whom had no more than they
stood up in after passage was paid. Hundreds, dis-
appointed at the unfavorable reports which reached them,
refused to leave the vessels, and a few hundreds more had
to be helped back by the Government.
When it set itself to the job of producing
metallic zinc in Australia, the Broken Hill Prop, faced
two big difficulties. One u 7 as the discovery of a clay suit-
able for making retorts ; the other was a labor trouble.
Zinc smelting is done in small retorts which at the best
only last on an average 30 days. The fire is applied ex
ternally, and the least irregularity in the making or drying
of the vessel means that it cracks almost at once, and is
valueless. Clays have been tried from all parts, and very
good results have been got from two places—Kangaroo
Island and a bed near the Iron Knob (S.A.), where the co.
gets its iron flux. The labor trouble is, however, still a
worry. As mentioned just now, the heat is applied ex-
ternally, and the job of attending to the retorts isn’t one
to be rushed. Where men are almost born and bred to
the business, the attendants can get through their task
in a very few hours; but the new chum, who has
to put in a full shift, has a job beside which sugar-cane
cutting is almost a picnic. No doubt it is a question of
money in the long run ; there are, for instance, other
severe mining jobs that have to be done on six hour shifts.
But, unfortunately, zinc is down to £l9 per ton, as against
£24 6s. 3d. this time 12 months ago, and £26105. this time
two years ago ; so the early prospects of a big Australian
spelter industry are not at all brilliant.
* * * *
The long-distance duel between the Adelaide
directors and Broken Hill shareholders in the B. H.
Junction still goes on. The Broken Hill shareholders
having accused the directors of loafing, the directors in-
genuously assume that that means that they ought to be
working the mill ; wherefore they issue another circular
to prove that, if they did that, they would lose about
£2 17s. 4d. per ton of concentrates, or 7s. 2d. per ton of
ore. It certainly would be absurd to work the mill now ;
the Junction hasn’t got enough metal to waste on a poor
market. Besides, as the mine stands, with hardly any
reserves, the mill could only be kept going by rooting out
ore regardless almost of either cost or consequences. The
real offence of the Junction management is, not that it
won’t run the mill now, but that it missed its chances
hitherto.
* * ■» *
The little bit of ore that all the fuss was made
about in King’s 400 ft. level of theß. H. Junction continues
to hug the British boundary. The “ north ” drive on the
lode, which is now pursuing a north-easterly course, is
44ft. from the boundary, and it is close to the footwall.
(The hanging-wall is in British ground.) This develop-
ment work is very necessary, however, as it will open
the way for sinking King shaft later on.
* * * *
There is a good deal of bitterness between the
Zinc Corporation, which is using the Elmore oil process,
and the Sulphide Corporation, which is using the Catter-
mole oil process. The latter, owned by the Minerals
Separation, Ltd., was alleged to be a piracy, and litigation
has been dragging its expensive and python-like length
through the English courts. The last point has been
scored by the Cattermole people, who cable out that the
High Court has dismissed the Elmore people’s action,
with Costs. Unless this should chance to be upset by the
Higher or the Highest Court, the Sulphide Corporation
and the Minerals Separation, Ltd., will feel a lot better,
for they have both had hanging over them the possibility
of a big claim on account of royalty on all the product
hitherto turned out from the works.

on the complex gold ores recently described in The But
letin. A theoretical success was assured ; but practical
results are still awaited. In any case, however, the

• • * *
The King’s Bluff gold mine, Olary (S.A.), is a
Broken Hill-managed concern, and the methods of
development belong to the Stone Age. At the far end of
an incline shaft, which is about 540 ft. long, equal to a
vertical depth of about 200 ft., there is a bunch of stone of
uncommon richness, but whether it is a ledge of quartz
resting on the slate wall, or a permanent reef, or merely a
pocket, the work done up to the present has not made
clear. And if the present policy of development is con-
tinued, it never will be clear. A few of the old-school
minefs got bold of the property in the first instance, and
when they found they really had a gold mine, or what
looked suspiciously like one, their first impulse
was to run away and hide it for fear that
some one would take it from them. For months the
directors have been dawdling along with a five-head
battery, and something that was once an engine. Both
of these contrivances would fall to pieces and lie down if
they got near a scrap heap. Now work is about stuck up
for want of a pump. A new boiler was put in because the
old one became so tired that it refused to get up steam on
a cold day, and after that burst of enterprise the directors
sat back and wondered at its own daring behavior. What
the King’s Bluff wants is a more progressive management
—a Board that will hit things up, and not be afraid
of knocking the bottom out of the mine by doing a bit of
work there.
* * * *
The paper published on the spot accuses Tin-
dal’s management (W. A.) of want of system and intoler-
ance of criticism ; and no wonder. Neither the value of
gold won nor particulars of working expenditure are
given with the occasional output figures, and any share-
holder who wants information must go to the mine
authorities and ask it as a favor—and then the chances
of his getting it are not bright. The co. badly needs to
mend its ways.

“Gip” : Did Mr. Johnson, M.H.R., really
want to organise a winter picnic to Monaro so
that the party would realise the full horror of the
climate? Someone had better advise him not to
come just now, anyhow. This 14th day of July
would have surprised him some methinks. Since
early morning the sun has been shining hotly and
gloriously—so hotly that the men rode coatless, and
down in the rocky basin the spring water grew
warm. In the patch of garden the autumn-sown
pansies are casting up their buds, the strawberry
plants are covered with new leaves, and the La
France violets are as big as florins. And, by
the way, who was it said we couldn’t grow cab-
bages? Our winter-grown Savoys might open his
eyes *, Brussels sprouts we have been using for a
month past, and the tall broccoli are folding their
leaves over the coming flower. But it was the
day I started to write about—a still, blue-topped,
golden, midwinter Monaro day. Surely there’s
no other day like it.

“Quineecha ” : Your contributor, truthful
“ James Outback,” seems to have absorbed most
things in his time, but I beat him, I fancy, by

was little variety about the foodstuffs provided.
We got either “mutton” (which
was in reality goat) or a mystic
medley hight “ stew.” One day I
inadvertently wandered into the
kitchen, and there found on a
table, grinning hideously in
death, and looking gruesomely
human, a monkey. I took it shud-
dersomely, and threw it away. The
eook (a nig of infinite dirt) expostu-

I queried. “It’s stew day,” he re-
plied. When I came to I stowed
away on an Australia-bound vessel, and
have clung, leech-like, ever since to the Common-
wealth because it is monkeyless.

“F.S.P.” : If any scalping is in progress
on the “Abo.” page, I should like to reach out for
the top decoration of your contributor, ‘‘ W. H.M.,”
who lies most villainously (B. 11/6/’08) re river
cod. In the first place, it is not the practice
among fishermen to tether cod by the lip at all;
they almost invariably tether them through the
nose. And, in the second place, “W.H.M.” to
the contrary notwithstanding, it is a common
thing for cod to be eaten after they have been tied
up for as long as two weeks. I know of an in-
stance where a cod was forgotten and left tied up
for three months, and in-the meantime it had fat-
tened and thrived. It is a regular practice among
fishermen on the lower Darling and Murray to
keep their catch tethered for a fortnight pending
the departure of a down-river boat to Morgan,
whence it is trained to Adelaide, and there sold
freely at 9d. per lb.
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : In reply to “ The
Prooshan,” I desire to say that crows and
gohannas both eat the flesh of the fox, though
most bushmen swear that fox-flesh is never eaten.
Recently I shot a fox near Gunongjugrowah (I
can’t spell the rest of the name) on the Muddv-
bidgee. Three days later I was duck-shooting,
when I found that the greater part of the carcase
had been devoured. At my approach a couple of
gohannas of pronounced embonpoint waddled
away towards the gums on the river bank. As a
rule, the fox carcase is skinned because the fur is
valuable. It is possible that the bush animals
fight shy of a skinned carcase, fearing poison.
Unskinned dead rabbits are eaten much more
quickly and readily than the skinned dead ones.

“ Cicfc.” : “ Agricola ” (B. 2/7/’08) grossly
libels a very gamesome bird when he accuses the
turkey of cowardice. He throws up at the entire
breed the conduct of a bird he saw being assailed
by two white magpies. Such a happening is,
however, no criterion in this regard. The mag.
is the Malay-run-amok of the feathered world. I
will- back it to disperse, when so disposed, any
other sentient thing on earth in short order, not
excluding crows, hawks, horses, dogs and men.
I believe whales would flee from it. As to the
gobbler, I have seen one of , him sicken a trio of
gamecocks, backed up by their , entire harem, in
well under the fifth round, and accept stoically all
that was handed him in between' the clouts he
himself was busy dealing aroiind. Has “ Agri-
cola ” ever essayed to tear asunder a couple of
warring turkeys ? I have, and Can testify that
the job was as lively and as difficult a one as I
could do with. The wattled fowl is handicapped
by his cumbrous figure, and when side-stepping,
feinting, or manoeuvring for a short-arm jab, is
likely to get on his back and be passed out before
he regains his equilibrium. But mostly he makes
good. His pluck, weight and staying powers, as
a rule, cause the other side to sky the towel While
the radiant orb of scrap is barely up over the
horizon.
* * * *
“ Veldt ” on a fodder question :
“ Prooshan ” (B. 9/7/’08) wants to know at what stage
sorghum may be fed to stock without causing any
injurious effects. The subject is of economic interest,
but so far has not been handled to any extent. Investi-
gation has elicited the following facts :
(1) Two varieties only have been known to cause death :
sorghum saccharatum and early amber cane.
(2) There is no case on record of horses or sheep
succumbing after eating either variety. Cattle only seem
to be affected.
"(3) The active agent is either hydrocyanic (prussic) acid,
or more probably an alkaloid possessing similar properties.
(4) Sorghum, cut and allowed to wilt for a day or two,
may be fed to cattle without danger.
(5) Known cases of death have generally resulted from
turning hungry cattle on to an immature crop or on to a
crop that has been stunted by a period of dry weather.
(6) Liberal quantities of molasses, given at the time of
feeding, or previously, act as an antidote.
(7) Ensilaged sorghum, no matter at what stage the
plants have been put into the silo, has never been known
to cause injury, and a crop turned into silage has
twice the feeding value, from a digestive standpoint, of
the same crop when used in a green state.

“ Agricola” : About once a month one of
our imported naturalist experts sharpens his teeth
and files his toe-nails, and delivers himself to the
effect that our native birds are not adaptable to
changing conditions, and must therefore disappear
before the imported article. I wish he could
happen along among the grain sheds at t'he
Riverina railway yards, what time the cookies
come to view their stacks, and collect the opinions
and profanity and blasphemy. The parrots are
adapting the wheat in a way that would astonish
him. Shell parrots and rosellas do the most mis-
chief, and they just swarm in the sheds. The
locust can take a back seat these days. Each bird
selects a different bag, bores a hole in it, and con-
sumes the gram, meanwhile remarking that it is a
lot easier than cracking pine-seeds, and 11 times
as satisfactory. When the owner of the stack
comes along the parrots depart, and he spends a
pleasant hour plugging the holes (and, great
Scott, how he does plug !) with old papers and
bits of Sacking, and tries to work ou-t mentally
how much a day it costs him to feed the poultry.
Then he goes mad, and after trying to slay the
whole family with an axe, his eldest son and his
second wife seize him by the collar and the be-
hindment of the pants, and rush him off to the
asylum.
A SKETCH FROM LIFE ON THE MARANOA
(QUEENSLAND) STOCK ROUTE.
At the tail of the mob; the calf-cart.
“Aural” : Allow me to answer the query
of “Prooshan” (B. 9/7/’08) re sorghum and
spotted thistle poisoning. Investigations con-
ducted by the Imperial Institute in 1902 disclosed
the presence of prussic acid and cyanide of
potassium ip sorghum. The poisons are present
during the first five to seven weeks’ growth, and
disappear after the blossoming stage, or at any
time shortly after the plants are cut. Crops that
have been checked by dry weather or other causes
are most dangerous, and these are the kind that
Mr. Cocky likes to graze his stock upon. The
more vigorous the plant the less poison is present,
but crops manured with nitrate fertilisers are so
lethal that 41b. of the green horror would assassin-
ate a cow. Sorghum, millet, amber cane, Egyptian
and Kaffir corns are all equally dangerous, and
should not Be grazed off, nor fed green, nor used
at all when young or stunted, unless made into
ensilage or hay. “Prooshan’s” examples of
poisoning from older crops might be explained by
a possible check in the growth through a dry spell,
a circumstance favoring the development of the
poisons.

“ Wearris ” encounters a brace of eccen-
tric drunks:—
Was in a Birdsville (Q.) pub recently (Birdsville, with
an adult population of 15, has three pubs), when "a beer-
worshipper struggled in and demanded a drink. He was
handed a bottle, but he took the gong instead, and pour-
ing from it what he considered a fair nip, thirstily drained
the imaginary contents. As he planked down a “ bob ”
an ugly leer overspread his rugged features, and he with-
drew muttering, “That’s the rott'enest damn drink I ever
had.” Later I struck another in the same establishment
who was haranguing the barman re what change he
would get if he shouted and paid for the drinks with
“good money.” The servitor explained that “goo'd
money ” would be tendered in change. The reveller was
satisfied. “ Right ho !” he said. “ Fill ’em up, old buck,
but blime if you’re goin’ to ring enny dawg scalps
inter me.”
* * * *
“ Dandalup ” : “ Groper, ” in his endeavor
to lead The Bulletin (2/7/’08) from the paths of
virtue, brands himself as a member of the
Westralian Stinking Fish family. He screeches
through his decayed belltopper when he says,
“ There is practically no land available for selec-
tion in W.A., except 40 or 50 miles from the
railway line.” “ Groper ”is a liar ! Not so very
far from Geraldton, and within easy distance of
the railways, are millions of acres of some of the
best wheat country in the Southern Hemisphere.
Stretching from the Murchison to Northampton
are the Alpha Downs—a mighty wheat belt with
a magnificent annual rainfall. Through this un-
inhabited paradise runs the Murchison railway
line. Nearer to the coast is the Chapman Valley,
a huge tract of country of such good quality that
Victorian cookies would rave over it. All this
land is within easy distance of a market, yet the
domicile of the cocky is few and far between. 1
Then, where there are no railways—around Car-
narvon —there is 200 miles of rich alluvial soil,
capable of growing anything, from cotton to jim-
jams. If “ Groper” wants land. I’ll guarantee to
get him a ten-billion-acre selection if he can only
cut himself loose from his little southern fowl-run
and steer north. We want a Progressive Land-
Settlement Policy—the land is there in abundance.
Finally, “ Go north, young man !”

“ Loco’s ” unreliable tale :
“ Yairs.” said Jim the engine-driver, as he shifted the
empty pewter from his mouth, “ I’ve had some wild ex-
periences in me day. I remember one night I was ordered
ter take a doctor from Melbourne ter Birrigurra in th’
quickest possible time. Ter make me ingine lighter I
uncoupled th’ tender an’ left it on a side track. When
th’ doctor took a seat on th’ fireman’s box I threw th’
lever down in th’ corner an’ gave ’er steam. Away we
jumped like a scared kangaroo. The doctor’s eyes bulged
out like a pair of doorknobs as we went like Hell over th’
track towards Geelong. ‘ Ripity-click ’ we smashed
erlong over th’switches an’frogs an’bridges, th’doctor
’angin’ on fer ’is life an’ lookin’ like an uninsured man
sittin’ down to ’is first dish er cucumbers.
“‘What’s that—a post?’ asked th’ doctor as we
passed somethin’ in a jiffy.
“It was a shed 120 ft. long. So yer can see ’ow fast we
were goin’.
“ ‘ What’s that funny-lookin’ fringe on our left?’ asked
th’ doctor.
“ ‘ Them’s the telegraph poles,’ answered the fireman,
as ’e stopped ’arf a minute from shoveilin’ coal, just as we
zipped through Geelong.
“ Well, we made Birrigurra, without er stop, in 41
minutes, just two miles ter th’minute, an’l boiled th’
cawfee in me dinner billy on th’ drivin’ boxes.”
The barmaid heaved a sigh of relief. “ Well, I’m glad
you got there safe,” she said. “ I expected you would
tell us that the engine jumped off the track, and all three
of you were sent to glory.”
“ No, we were all alive an’ kiekin’,” replied J.im, confi-
dently.
“What er long-armed fireman yer must ’ave ’ad,” put
in Billy, the navvy, as he wiped his mouth with his
sleeve.
“ ’Ow’s that ? ” asked Jim.
“ Why, ter shovel coal in Geelong from er tender that
you’d left on a side-t,rack in Melbourne.”
Jim went outsidp to cool.

“ 11. G.” talks sedition and blasphemy
• r j
A controversy has been progressing as to the name of
the more or less disreputable character to whom belongs
the “honor” of introducing the trout into Australian
waters. Presumably when this is done a movement will
be started for erecting a statue to the benefactor who
introduced the rabbit and the fox and the prickly pear
and the fruit fly. So deeply ingrained into the nature of
scmie people is the mania for importing things that the
m m ded altruist’; and,’had some crank* in the early da.vs
let loose a few hundred hyenas in the West, he would
probably be held up to public admiration on Empire days,
and Pulsford would write yards of letters to the papers
gb'ing the statistics of the hyena production from 1852 to
% P e T" r ™; n e citizen should class the man who first
p] aced trout in Australian streams with the man who
brought the rabbit and the fox, for he has done precisely
the Same for the Australian waters as the rabbit idiot did
for Australian lands. When the white man came to
Australia, the streams were fairly well filled with native
fish, almost all of them edible and of commercial value. In
mos ’ t of those streams into which imported fish have been
introduced, the native fish have disappeared, and in their
place are a handful of inferiorimported things, which serve
solely for the amusement of a few alleged sportsmen,
When it is said that the trout is good to eat, nothing
™ re ca ". be P ut forward in his favor. He is a small fish,
only mediocre in flavor, arid his sore virtues are what are
called his « flghting qualities.” That is to say, instead of
submitting to be killed in a humane manner, he struggles
against the inevitable in order that so-called sportsmen
may gloat over his pain and terror for a more or less
lengthy period. The harder the trout dies, and the more
cruelty that is imported into his killing, the better the
“sport”; and the noble sportsmen, many of whom
shudder at the thought,of a boxing contest, spend many
pounds in order to gratify their atavistic instincts,
For all practical purposes, there is just as much gen-
uine sport in angling as there is in decapitating the
family rooster on the box-block. But as the family
rooster’s death is accomplished speedily, and the attempt
of a sportsman to compass it by'long-drawn njeans would
lead to a prosecution by the Society for the Prevention of
Brutality to Fowl, he is forced to turn his attention to
trout, which apparently are regarded by the same society
as outlaws. Meantime, while he isn’t being tortured to
death for the delectation of sundry degenerates, the trout
works off his fightability by killing other fish, and he has
so far succeeded in this that it takes very few months to
clear the streams he occupies of all the native fish. The
resultis that in streams wherein there were formerly tons of
big, fine-flavored Australian fish which might to-day have
provided hundreds of men with a living, and added
materially to the food supply of the State, there are only
a few pounds of trout, and these are looked after at State
expense, in order that once a year a gang of fat gentle-
men may come up from the city and make merry,
It is not only in respect of trout that the importing
mania has helped to deplete Australia of what should be
one of its most important resources. When Melbourne
was a small township, the black fish from the Yarra
formed no inconsiderable factor in its food supply. To-
day there are no black fish. They have all been killed by
the roaches—small, bony, insignificant English fish, prac-
tically uneatable, which some maniac introduced in order
that he might enjoy “sport.” The extermination of the
native fish from Australian streams is on all fours with
the practice of European lordlings in throwing wheat
lands out of cultivation to fatten a score of deer to be
shot at intervals of a year when the cafes of London and
Paris begin to bore the obese landowners. The only
difference is that in England the State itself doesn’t
directly encourage the lunacy of restricting sources of
production, while in Australia the general body of tax-
payers is called upon to weigh out in the cause of
national starvation.
A SOUTH SEA FANTASY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh ! wouldn’t it be
A glorious spree,
A jubilant, jibbering jamboree,
To live as a cannibal, bold and free,
On an isle in the South Pacific Sea,
Near far Fiji ?
Any old island would do for me.
AVhen the mission man
His “ work ” began,
With his carpet-bag and his boots of tan,
And his specs and gamp and a palm-leaf fan,
He’d be welcomed there by the whole dashed clan
With eclat and elan,
While the villagers danced a wild can-can.
And then as he sat
On a native mat
For a soulful, sanctimonious chat,
I’d notice the rabid and rampant “ rat”
That he kept concealed in his white cork hat;
And the back of his black cloth suit I’d pat
To feel the fat,
And then, in the native tongue, order that—
The POT be got,
The three-legged pot—
The biggest among the blooming lot;
And straightway set in the same old spot,
Filled to the brim and made boiling hot.
Then, having arranged the regular plot,
I’d show him WHAT—
This holy person who talked such rot.
When he chanced im stoop
I’d raise a whoop
And swing my club with a mighty swoop,
And bring it down with a “ scrunch ” and “ scroop,”
And the man with the mission would sag and droop,
And a festive group
Would tumble him cheerfully into the soup.
And what a shout
The rowdy rout
Would raise as they revelled and romped about;
Till the cook-in-chief should serve him out,
And We’d all tuck-in at the great tuck-out
Of sanctified tucker, till all grew stout;
And beyond a doubt
He’d have made us happy if not devout.
So now you see
Why I’d like to be
A cannibal chieftain, bold and free,
’Neath the cocoanut palm and the bread-fruit tree,
With a gentle shepherd at times for tea,
On an isle in the South Pacific sea,
Near far Fiji.
Any old island would do for me.

“The Lizard”: Be “ Boronia ” in your
issue of 2/7/’OB. Selectors are not all mad, surely,
or they would not be taking up over 1,000,000
acres of Westralian land every year. However,
I’ll leave the land matter over to a further issue.,
and have a go at “Boronia’s” scalp over the
poison plant. To start, leaving out personal ex-
perience, let me quote two men who are perhaps
the best qualified in W. A. to speak on the subject,
one residing in a lightly-infested district and
the other where the poison is heavier. I
might mention that the extracts are from the
Westralian Royal Commission on Immigration
(1905): —
Warren Marwick had a poison lease of 5970 acres, about
12 miles from Greenhills. He had acquired the lease in
July last (1904), and had eradicated the poison, and the
land was stocked. He considered that Sixpence an acre
would be sufficient to eradicate the plant from the worst
poison- land he knew of. He was not afraid of it.
Chas. A. Piesse, M.L.C., had been settled in the liatan-
ning district for the last 25 years. He had had consider-
able experience with country infested with poison. Had
one 800-acre paddock, 450 acres of which had been badly
infested with poison, but he had grubbed and burnt it off
at a cost of 3s. 6d. per acre. The second year after com-
mencing operations he had had the paddock Stocked with
sheep. Last year he had 1134 sheep on this 800 acres for
12 consecutive months, and had not lost one of them.
They were fat when taken out.
So what is “Boronia ” snorting about ? I reckon
that the poison plant, instead of being a curse, is
the greatest blessing Westralia could have had,
in that it has prevented practically nine-tenths of
a magnificently-watered wheat belt from being
held by the big squatters. If “Boronia” wants
to know whether poison can be eradicated, per-
haps Mr. John Wilkie (Coolgardie railway
contractor), who has cleared 60,000 acres of it,
will enlighten him further.
A HEARTLESS ADMINISTRATION.
FARMER: “I hev my troubles as well as you.
There's my poor boy as two doctors ’as ffuv

“ Alpay ” : At Narrandera (N.S.W.) wit-
nessed a scrap between a buck rabbit and a craw.
Crow got Bunny by the ear, and made him
squeak. Then the rabbit made a bound and dis-
lodged his antagonist. The bird tried for another
hold, but missed. The buck made a flying leap,
kicked the black fowl hard on the head, and then
landed on his back. The crow was stunned, and
the rabbit and others sat down upon him, and
clawed his eyes out. Make no mistake about the
rabbit as a fighter. He could take Australia, if
he only knew it.

“Jimmie Pannikin”: “ W.H.M.” (B.
11/6/’08) does not know what he is writing about
when he asserts that a cod cannot be tethered by
its top lip. The Menindie fishermen, who sup-
plied Broken Hill with tons of lovely Darling cod
and perch during my three years of Outback exile,
used either to tether their big fish or keep them
inside a netting enclosure while waiting for the
fish-waggon. Along the Muddybidgee, even the
townies from Gunda, Coota, Murra and Mutta
have sense enough to tether the fish they wish to
take home fresh. The big cod are often tethered
for two or three days, and fed twice a day on
worms, oak-grubs, crickets, frogs, rabbit-chitter-
lings, etc. Some day I intend to find out whether
it would not be both possible and profitable to
fatten cod for the market. We used to fatten
oysters with oatmeal in wooden tubs up in the
Big Scrub.

“ The Prooshan ” : “ Elmore ” claims (B.
9/7/’08) the “fur” and rind of the wombat to be
valuable assets, hitherto overlooked. “Elmore”
should really know better than to talk of fur in
connection with wombats. They wear
bristles, and coarse ones at that; and,
so far as leather is concerned —rats ! A
neighbor of mine skinned a wombat
some years ago and sent the outer
bag of the animal to Goldsbrough’s
firm in Sydney to ascertain whether
it could be turned to any practical use.
Goldsbrough duly received it and sent
it to a tannery, and in a month or .so
wrote to say that, as leather, it was
valueless. The mere task of skinning
and the removal of the thick layers
of fat would satisfy “Elmore ” that the
hide of a wombat is a fraud. The only
practical use the thing can be put to
is to make a mud mat of it. The
Chinese value the gall of the wombat
highly, giving as much as ss. for it.
They hang the galls under a sheet of
bark in the air, placing a small china
basin under each to catch the fluid
that drips out. This they use chiefly
as a hair lubricant. The residue of
the gall is sent to the Flowery Land,
and there prepared as a highly ex-
pensive medicine. It is a strange but
indisputable fact that the gall of a
wombat is fullest at full moon. John
always gives a bigger figure for a full-
moon gall than for any other. At full
moon it is bluer or darker in color than
at other times.
“ Nym ” : A foretaste of the profit to be
derived from systematic fish culture is now being
experienced by stout hearts in the Vic. channel
country. Here, owing to slackness of graft
through the drought, they have been dragging
w i re -nets through the dams adjacent to the few
channels that were running at the tail-end of
summer, with the result that splendid hauls have
been made of cod and perch. Most of the fish
weighed close up to 101 b., and all the small fry
brought to grass were put back. These fish came
down, in the fry-stage mostly, with the channel
water. Those marketed were eagerly snapped up
at 6d. per lb., uncleaned, and heads included,
One thing noticeable is that where yabbies were
caught in the nets there were no fish ; where fish
inhabited the dams, no yabbies were caught. A
few cod-fish policemen patrolling the channel-
ways, and protection against molestation by
poachers, would do much to minimise damage
from this yabbie pest in the big Waranga-Goul-
burn canal when it is finished and filled.
“ Harry Stockdale ” writes :
Will Ogilvie, in his new book, “ My Life in the Open,”
says with much truth, “ They say that you can tell a
man by the company he keeps, but I would rather judge
him by the horse he rides.” In his chapter called “ The
Blood of the Desert ” he says also
“ You may have ridden some good horses, but the
Arab is a thing apart, for in his veins runs the royal blood
of a line of kings—the matchless blood of the desert!...
But if you have not ridden such, let me say thiat you have
fallen short of learning the possibilities of equine excel-
lence raised to its highest power. ... We ride him—a
royal privilege—ride him fast and ride him far. Behind
him our companions, on their tried old favorites, drop
backward one by one ; but the further we go the gayer
becomes the dancing step of our scion of the desert chiefs.
The red light kindles in his anxious, resolute eye ; it is
the light of battle, the love of speed, the pride and
power of pre-eminent endurance, the badge of irreproach-
able courage. Every jaunty flash of his white hoofs,
every sweep of his blown white forelock, is a menace to
the miles and a mockery to the toiling rivals in his train.”
This may be fine writing and makes pleasant enough
reading, but is altogether contrary to fact, as proven
by many trials, not only of speed but of endur-
ance ; also, of speed and endurance combined—trials
that have been made between picked Arabians
and second-class thoroughbreds. In every instance,
over both short and long courses, the Arabians
were defeated and became “the toiling rivals in his
train,” That the Arabian is a grand horse and a very
desirable one to cross with our mares few horse-breeders
will gainsay ; likewise, that he has much to recommend
him in his good looks, his docility, his courage, and, above
all, in the incomparable soundness of his legs and feet;
but, as a rival to the British thoroughbred, either for one
mile or ten, he has no pretension whatever under any
weight, light or heavy, either in his own land, or here in
Australia, or elsewhere. This has been proven over and
over again where all the advantages were on the side of
the Arabian, the contests taking place in his own country,
the inferior class thoroughbreds giving away no end of
weight and beating the Arab all the way. Yet the Arab
is a noble steed that might, perhaps, be ntore properly
classed as a galloway, as those I have seen were all well
under 15 hands ; and I venture to say we have had, and
still have, galloways under 14.2 here in Australia that
could beat the best of Mr. Ogilvie’s “royal blood of a line
of kings.”
As far ba'ck as King James’ time the then Duke
of Newcastle wrote—“l never saw any but one of
these horses (Arabian), which Mr. John Markham, a
merchant, brought over and said he was a right Arabian.
He was but a little horse, and no rarity for shape ; for I
have seen many English horses far finer. Mr. Markham
sold him to King James for five hundred pounds ; and
being,trained up for a course, when he came to run every
horse beat him.”
Gervase Markham in his old horse book said—
“ When the best Barbaries that ever were in my
remembrance were in their prime, I saw them over-
run by a blapk Hobbie at'Salisburie of Maister Carlton’s ;
and yet that Hobbie was more over-runne by a horse of
Maister Blackstone called Valentine, which Valentine
neither in hunting or running was ever equalled, yet
was a plain bredde English horse, both by syre and
damme.”
And so it has been right down to the present.
Captain M. Horace Hayes, F.R.C.V.S., one of our greatest
authorities on thi? horse, says of breeding in India :
“ The country produce bred by General Parrott from
nearly pure English blood, although incapable of holding
their own against Australians, have been at weight for
age and class capable of defeating Arabs at all distances.
The Iftdian weight for age scale compels English and Aus-
tralian horses to carry 3st. more than Arabs at three and
four miles.”
And though up to Hayes’ time Bravo was the only
first-class Australian Which had run in India, and he, from
unsoundness, was quite unable to show anything like his
true form, yet, Hayes says, speaking generally, “ no weight
within reason can bring Arabs together with even second-
rate colonials ; and for the last 20 years we have not had
a single Cape horse that could hold his own against
Colonials.”
So much for the pretensions of the pure-bred Arab
when put in the scale against our own Australian-bred
horse
WHERE THE BIG TREES GROW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Despite the ancient grandeur of the Danube and the
Rhine;
The castled heights, the gardens of the olive and the
vine ;
The old and crumbling splendors ; the forests swathed in
snow,
I know a better country, where the big trees grow !
From the blue Dividing Ranges to the Baw-Baws, glim-
mering white;
From Nar-Nar-Goon to Marysville, across the timbered
height;
From Neerim, through the passes where the bullock
waggons go,
Lie the red-soil hills and valleys where the big trees grow.
Old veterans and warriors of many a roaring fray,
The scars of countless wars they bear, yet stout and
strong are they,
With lofty branching heads upreared to all the winds
that blow—
There are giants in the country where the big trees grow.
Where the hardy brown selector carves his home by dint
of axe
They dwarf his little clearing ; and they hem the winding
tracks, s
Where the bullock-teams go crawling, axle-deep, and
moving slow
Round the shoulders of the mountains where the big
trees grow.
From the meadow lands of England to the apple-groves
of France—
Though tinged with ancient legend, and though haunted
by romance—
From the cities on the seaboard to the summits and the
snow
There is nought to match the country where the big trees
grow.

The Bishop of South Toklo (the Right
Rev. W. Awdry), in a letter to The
Times, says: “The Japanese do not
entertain the slightest idea of invading
Australia. The rumor rests on abso-
lutely nothing but the uneasy con-
sciences of those who are keeping lands
they cannot use themselves.”

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The oldest legislators in Adelaide are
John Carr and Alfred Hardy, both of them 90.
Carr was several times a Minister, but Hardy
never climbed that high. He is the oldest soli-
citor on the S.A. roll. Both men are to be seen
almost daily in the streets of Adelaide.
There is a story of the late Tom Fitz-
gerald, Australia’s most adept surgeon, that he
was one day approached by a wooden-legged
youth in the bird-cage at Flemington. The per-
son with the hickory limb gave the sporting free-
lance a dead cert., and said : “ I don’t forget you,
guv’ner, and I’m a grateful bloke, I am. I was
poor ’n’ alone—hadn’t a friend in the world—and
you cut off me leg.”
CHANTER , M.H.R.
“X.Y.Z.”: The Bulletin is wrong
(2/7/’08) in so much of its par re Sydney University
Medical School as states that “Dr. W. G. Arm-
strong can really claim to be its first graduate.”
Says the University Calendar of 1888 :
Honors at Graduation.
First Class.
P. Bancroft.
A. E. Perkins.
Second Class.
W. G. Armstrong.
A. G. Henry.
N. G. Davidson.
Of these A. G. Henry is dead. Peter Bancroft
practises in a neighboring' State, and has been
president of the Medical Board there. A. E.
Perkins is a busy suburban healer, and was
awarded the D.S.O. in the late farm-burning
shivoo. N. G. Davidson is a well-known Balmain
specialist.
Two Queensland pioneers, Torn Petrie and
Pan Donovan, ea6h Of whom is 77 years old, were
photographed together recently. Petrie has been
71 years in Bananaland and Donovan 63. They
will probably die w.ithin the next 20 years. This
Northern climate always kills white men sooner
Or later.
“ Myrtle ” : Died at Yeppooh (Central
Queensland), early this month (July), at the age
Of over 70, ( 0ne Lane, father of “ Paraguay ” Lane,
Of Rue Australia fame. He was a sturdy
Socialist, and died, as many prophesied he would;
arguing politics. ThO old inan iyas caretaker of a
small farm, owned by Senator “ Jiffliny ” Stewart;
and it was with the Senator that he was holding
converse when his heart failed for the first time ini
his life, and he quietly lay down.

Of Maoriland, in an Auckland Herald interview;

are one of the 10st ten tribes.” It’s Very evident
the Maoris are going to be found out; they can-
