an archaic opinion.
“Dandalup” sends a memoir:—
Died recently, in the Cold Country, aged 82, Arch-
deacon Lecaille, known for many years in Westralia as
“ the little bushman-priest.” He landed in the West in
1857, and was at once handed a diocese which stretched
from Busselton in the south to Northampton (then a
booming lead field) in the north. (Bunbury, at this time,
consisted of a few bark humpies and a dusfrbox ; “ Big
John” Forrest was known as “Tiny Jack,” and hadn’t
begun to trouble about the boots of civilisation ; Premier
Moore didn’t exist.) Lecaille did his extensive rounds on
horseback. He was a game horseman, and would straddle
the hottest outlaw this side of Sheol. Likewise he was a
mighty builder. He raised churches at Geraldton,
Greenough, Northampton, Bootenal, Back Flats, North-
ampton, Bunbury district, and Lord knows where
besides.
Nicholas Hawken, M.L.C., dead at the
age of 72, passed through both Houses of N.S. W.
Parliament. No man took llimself more seri-
ously, but no man in either House was taken less
seriously. His portentous gravity was accepted
as a mask of comedy on most occasions. His
ideas were bizarre, as a rule, and his words made
them more so. “I know I alone hold this
opinion,” he told the Council; “ but the feeling
that inspires me is one of pity for you.” He pro-
duced things he called verses—which were worse
than the perpetrations of Neild. He had spent
over half a century in N.S.W., and was highly
esteemed in the electorate of Newtown; but,
though professing to be a politician, he was inno-
cent both of theory and practice.
Died last week, at South Yarra (Vic.),
Miss Helen Hart, woman suffragist and many
other things. She was an untidy woman and
much given to wearing shapeless shoes and a
baggy ulster, and she was a rebel and unorthodox
from her fept to the top of her bashed hat. She was
clever and eloquent and democratic and in deadly
earnest, and she was wholly neglectful of her back
hair. In some sense she was the counterpart of
Jos. Symes, the bigoted atheist of Melbourne.
She was so insurrectionary that she could never
have settled down, even if everything she wanted
had come to pass ; the instinct of the fighter was
in her, and she would have bitten the hind leg of
the millennium rather than have had nothing to
battle with. Between her and sleek, persuasive,
nicely-groomed Annie Besant there was a sort of
kinship, yet a great gulf. One was akin to the
drawing-room Socialist and the other to the women
who stormed the Bastile. It is to be hoped Helen
Hart is resting where she has gone. While she
was alive the capacity to rest was not in her.
“Jean Sibi,” of Melbourne—the wail
thereof: —
The immense indefiniteness that the Nash case has
reached to date has been characteristic of the proceed-
ings all along. Everything has been as vague and dis-
ordered as a Chinese ode—and quite as exciting. Mystery
has been heaped on mystery, until the top of the
substance has got so high that people with weak chests
dare not look at it. And all this time —some years it
seems—there has been nothing but speculation, com-
ment, abuse, and a heap of archiepiscopal capers. Except
among the people immediately interested, nobody knows
what the parson did, or why his spiritual boss said he
did and then said he didn’t do what we don’t know
whether he did or not. The parson is without a position ;
the Archbishop is in the dim distance hurling vetoes and
contradictions; while the parson’s parishioners censure
his far-away Nibs and swear that they will stand by their
Nash until they get footsore or something. And, while
the circus is developing all the fascinating qualities of
a religious phantasmagoria, without beginning- anywhere
and ending in the same spot, Nash’s old parish at
Hawthorn is in the throes of internecine strife. The
vestry out in those parts is divided over some
international question about as attractive to the
average man as a milk diet or vegetarianism, and
are calling each other “falsehoods” in the daily
press. Every morning, on opening the daily paper,
one is confronted by about half a column of correspond-
ence, of superfluous explanation and equally-superfluous
accusation about their lost box of dominoes, or whatever
it is—superfluous in so far that 101 per cent, of the popu-
lace never read it. In addition, there is anything from
half to three-quarters of a column about the people who
are goingto maintain the vertical down at Geelong—stuff
which is as dull and unedifying as a treatise on crema-
tion. And there the case remains. It has long since
ceased to be notorious ; it has reached the apogee of
wearisomeness ; and has produced nothing but 2487 miles
of “guff.” Who are Archbishop Clarke, Nash, anld
Nash’s parishioners that they should be inflicting their
unexplained trouble upon me for ever and ever? I
should never dream of dragging my disputes with my
fishmonger or bottle-oh into the arc-light of publicity.
The death of James Kenniff, senior, of
Lethbridge Pocket (Queensland), recalls the last
faint flicker of the bushranging art in 1902. Dalke
(station manager) and Constable Doyle set out to
arrest James (junior) and Patrick Kenniff for
horse stealing. Only Sam Johnson, a black-
, tracker who accompanied Doyle and Dalke, came
back alive. Some charred remains, some odd
buttons, and some ashes in a bag on a pack-horse
—a walking sepulchre—expounded a tragedy.
The two Kenniff brothers appeared before a Bris-
bane Court (Roma as a court centre was tabooed —
local sentiment buzzed too strongly there). Much
circumstantial evidence and the black-tracker’s
testimony led to a conviction. A Pull Court of
four Judges, at Brisbane, confirmed the verdict —
Justice Real, the perpetually cantankerous, dis-
senting in favor of James Kenniff, which fact
accounts for James’ life sentence at St. Helena.
The hangman’s rope disposed of Patrick. There
was a great haze about the matter, and a lot of
people still believe that the mystery was never
really explained. The fact that a pack-horse
was found walking about loose with a bag of
remains that might easily have been buried, was
a bewildering circumstance. Some earnest readers
of Poe’s “Rue Morgue ” story had wild ideas that
a gorilla or a devil or an insane humorist was at
the bottom of the matter.

A firm of London solicitors has been
advertisim.’ - in Brisbane Cqukier for the next-of-
kin of one Louis Smallwood O’Connell, and states,
among other things :
Daniel O’Connell, a brother of the Settler’s father, is
believed to have gone to Australia in 1869, and is alleged
to have been killed there by a pirate.
Whereupon The Bulletin hunted up an old,
musty directory—the father and grandfather of
all directories, by its appearance—and looked for
“Pirates” in the “Trades and Professions”
sections, but found none. There have been bush-
rangers, solicitors, land sharks, cannibals, and
motor hogs in Australia, but the crop of pirates
has always been thin.
Chairman Meeks and general-manager
Teece have fixed up the London directorate of the
A.M.P., with N. S. Wales’ old-friend Jersey at
the head and that enthusiastic guinea-pig, the
ex-S. Australian Dr. Cdckburn, at the tail, and
one of the numerous Gibbses and H. S. King in
between. Dr. Cockburn made a pretty-consider-
able rise out of Mfi. Lyell, through its amalgama-
tion with the North Lyell, and isn’t in poverty
these days ; the other three are all disgustingly
rich. Jersey is a partner in the Child’s private
bankery; Gibbs belongs to a firm that has
probably more tentacles than any other jn Eng-
land (Gibbs, Bright and Co. is the Australian
house, and chairman Meeks is the senior partner
in that firm’s Sydney house); and Henry
Seymour King is a yachtsman who is a banker
and insurance man in his spare time.
One man in Melbourne has lately been
living the strehuous life all right. First he tran-
spired as acting sec. to a Sectional Committee
for the Fleet Decoration of the Collins-street
Block. He advertised for workmen, and
got some 300 answers, but selected nobody.
He next appeared as a spokesman for the
unemployed, but was repudiated by them,
and urged to remove himself from a de-
putation that was interviewing the Pre-
mier. He then fell foul of Opposition-
leader Prendergast, and was chased by
that irate party down the Treasury stairs,
escaping with his bell-topper over one
ear and his coat-tails flying over the
other. A few days later the police had
to escort him from a Yarra bank meeting
under a shower of stones. Next he ex-
cused himself to the Malvern beaks for
his failure to pay a small furniture bill,
and was ordered to settle up immediately.
Incidentally he informed the beaks that
he was somewhat flurried as he “had
had no breakfast that morning, and had
broken away from his prospective mar-
riage feast” in order to meet their Wor-
ships. He then lay quiet for a day or two,
his next appearance being at the City
Court on two charges of giving valueless
cheques —for trifling sums. He once
more assured their Worships that he was
to be married that morning and had ample
moneys to honor the blessed cheques. Still,
they committed him for trial. And at
almost the same moment the Malvern
Court, in his absence, was handing him
out £lO or a month for a trifling disagree-
ment with his landlord. It wasn’t the
proper thing to do, as the strenuous person
might have had something to say which he
wasn’t able to say, not being where he could
be heard ; but when a man has so many engage-
ments as this one has it seems almost impossible
for him to give proper attention to everything.
An early Victorian pioneer, Mrs. Jane
Millar, of Alberton, Gippsland (Vic.), went over
last-week at the age of 90 years. She arrived with
her husband in the early days of Port Phillip.
Mrs. Millar witnessed the last great fight between
the Gippsland tribe of blacks and their hereditary
. enemies, the Monaro tribe. The rows of these
two tribes spread in a general way from the site
of the Federal capital down to the Lakes.
By this paper’s Oldest Inhabitant
A much-administered estate came up for probate be-
fore the Victorian Court last week. Over half a century
ago Thomas Warburton was engaged in tinplate and iron-
work in Melbourne, where he built up a big business. He
was one of the first to issue copper tokens, which passed
as pennies in the absence of the re&l thing ; on the ob-
verse of the cojp one could read, “ Irpn and Zinc Spouting
Works, T. Warburton, 11 Little Bourke-street, West
Melbourne,” and on the reverse, “Victoria, 1862,” with
the Australian arms and motto, and an advertisement for
Stokes, the die-sinker who turned out most of the Mel-
bourne tokens of the time. In 1871 Warburton went to
Fiji on business, and was killed by natives. He had a
large estate in Victoria, and probate of his will was
granted to his widow. She, however, did not long sur-
vive the husband, and the administration of the estate
was granted to the testator’s eldest son, T. W. Warbur-
ton. In April last this Mr. Warburton died, and on
Thursday administration of the still unadministered
estate was granted to Mrs. Aikins, eldest daughter of the
testator. It seems a rather fatal estate.

the Colonial Office came from
Victorian Governor
Tal bottle, it appears,
and not from G.-G.
Northcote, who had
previously been
sounded on the
subject. The Tal-
bottle recommenda-
tion would hardly
have sufficed to do the
trick, hence the sending
forth of Weedon to do
battle in London.
' Windsor (N.S.W.) has lost an ancient
native, Mr. William Gosper, who died at Mac-
quarie-street in the old town on July 12, aged 81
years. He was an innkeeper in the old drinky
days when Windsor boasted 30 pubs. Windsor
hardly drinks enough for two pubs nowadays.

Some people have an amazingly weak hold
upon life. Two recent suicides were (1) that of a
young man in S.A. who shot himself because he
received an anonymous letter reflecting on his best
girl: and (2) that of a N.S.W. girl of 18, whose
mother had spoken harshly to her about the in-
efficient washing of certain plates.
LONG LAN TUN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow, has been
appointed Chinese Consul-General for the Common-
wealth.—News item.
Silence the lettuce ! He’s here with us how.
Hail! Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
iurn off the cabbage ! Respectfully boW
io Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Arrange ev’ryone for solemn pow-wow
\Vith Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Get the best joss-sticks, and murder the sow
For Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Each on the carpet must bump with his brow
To Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Pour not in the tea the juice of the cow
For Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
If you do, you’ll have the deuce of a row
With Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
That’s one of the things he will not allow
This Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Milk’s stuff for the cat that yelleth “ Mee-yow ! ”
Not for Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
He doesn’t keep it at home on his scow,
Ah Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Here’s luck ! May your gods with pleasures endow
You, Long Lan Tun, taotai of Swatow.
Fill up the teapot ! old fellow —“ Here’s how ”
Hi-yah 1 Whaffor ! Taotai of Swatow !
N.S.W.
E. O’F.
ADOLPHUS BENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thomas Bent has hinted that his second name is
Adolphus. Following the example of Hector Oarruthers,
he will probably be known as “Sir Adolphus Bent.”—
News Item.
T. Adolphus Bent,
Tell us what you meant
By so long concealing this superb embellishment ?
Think you, we’d have sent
You to Parliament)
Had we known you postured ’neath a title fraudulent ?
Shame upon you, shame !
What a blessed name
To affright the masons as they chip the Scroll of Fame !
Has some lovely dame
Happened to inflame
Feelings that were dormant, this agnomen to proclaim?
Tom ! Oh, tell us where
Happened you to snare
Such an appellation, so bizarre and debonair?
Can you truly swear
It was on the. square,
Or of some job that’s shady does it represent your share ?
Tom of old, relent,
Let us not lament
That our earnest praises of your prowess were misspent;
Dire despair prevent,
Close the incident,
Seek not painting lilies nor adorning Thomas Bent.
VIC.
MILTON MACGERGOR.

Finally Cured by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills.
“ When I was 17 I became anaemic, and I was
ailing until I was twenty-two,” says Mrs. Francis
McGoldrick, 10 Randle Street, Sydney. “It
came on gradually. I found myself getting very
weak and run down. All my strength went. My
appetite left me altogether. For (lays at a time
hardly a morsel would pass my lips. I mostly
lived on bread and butter. I had a mad craving
for vinegar ; if the bottle wasn’t locked up I’d get
up in the middle of the night to get at it. I fell
away till I could count almost every bone. You
could see through my hands, they were so trans-
parent. My head would ache for days at a time
till I was nearly distracted. The top of my head
seemed as if it was opening and shutting. I’d get
faint turns perhaps three times a day, sometimes
I’d go right off, even if I were in the street, and
other times the room would spin round and every-
thing would go dark and I’d have to sit down at
once. I couldn’t do any form of work, sewing or
reading were beyond me. My feet and ankles
were all puffed up. My eyesight began to fail.
All the color faded from my face and lips. I was
the color of parchment. I was so bloodless I
dreaded a cold day—l’d feel perished. My eyes
were sunken and of unnatural brilliancy with
dark lines under them. I had advice from eight
doctors and spent quite a large sum of money try-
to get relief. I went down in weight to seven
stone. If I walked quickly my heart would throb
and jump and then seem to stop altogether. I’d
get hot flushes and feverish turns and then go
quite cold. I fully believed I was in consumption.
I was a victim to hysteria and would cry and
laugh in turns until I was thoroughly exhausted.
I never slept well and never got up feeling re-
freshed. I was so utterly wretched that I didn’t
care whether I lived or died. At last, through an
article in the newspaper, I read of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills and decided to give them a trial. I
could feel the benefit before I had finished the first
box. I felt my circulation better. I began to eat
a little solid food and lost my mad craving for
lemons and vinegar. A tinge of color came back
to my lips and cheeks. My strength began to
return with every dose. In a li.ttle while I began
to put on flesh and get rosy. 1 longed for meal
time. My headaches left me altogether, and the
fainting fits disappeared, and in a short time I
was fully restored in health. lam as well now as
I could wish to be. I can go out and enjoy my-
self, and feel bright and energetic. In my case
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills acted like a miracle.”_
When you begin to take Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, watch your appetite. In a few days you’ll
feel that you can’t get enough to eat. That’s one
of the first signs that they are doing good. They
make new blood and tone run-down nerves, and
sufferers who take them find daily improvement.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by all medi-
cine dealers, or if you cannot get them locally,
send direct to The Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.
of Australasia, Ltd., Sydney, 2s. 9d. for one box ;
six boxes for 15s. 3d., post free.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A QUEENSLAND MINISTERIAL TRIP
IN THE FAR NORTH.
Photograph taken at Dalby (Queensland) of the
30-h.p. Panhard motor-car of Mr. J. W. Blair,
Q. Attorney-General and Secretary for Mines. In
four weeks Mr. Blair travelled in this car 3000
miles in Central, Northern and Western Queens-
land, much of the country having never before been
traversed by motor. The car was railed to Black-
all; starling from there Mr. Blair passed through
Barcaldme, Longreach, Winton, Kynuna, Mackin-
lay, Cloncurry, Mount Elliott and Mount Hamp-
den. He travelled from Cloncurry to Richmond
(230 miles) on the newly constructed line, motoring
thence via Hughenden, Tangorin, Muttaburra, and
Aramac to Blackall. From Blackall he proceeded
with his motor via Charleville, Mitchell, Roma,
Dalby, Toowoomba, and Ipswich to Brisbane.
The occupants of the car are Messrs. Blair,
Murphy (Northampton Downs), O'Hagan (Pri-
vate Secretary), Leach (COURIER and, QUEENS-
LANDER) and C. E. Hall.
The surprise visit of Kaiser William to
the British fleet during the North Sea manoeuvres
makes you think of the man who, seeing a crowd
on the vacant allotment next his house, strolls
over casually in his shirt-sleeves to have a -look
round and see what’s doing. The North Sea is
Bill’s vacant allotment, and he always likes to
know what’s happening there.
“ Vulcan ” : Sydney Telegraph (11/7/’08)
instances yet another of the baleful effects of Pro-
tection —the tariff imposition that was going to
“place the unfortunate Australian piano-buyer at
the mercy of a single piano-making monopoly ” :
Wertheim and Co. have decided to construct a large
piano manufactory at Richmond, which is expected to
o-ive employment to between 200 and 300 men.
The building is to be completed within 12 months.
Meantime the firm is making exhaustive experiments
with various Australian tvoods.
Lemmon, M.L.A., startled Vic. Assembly
lately by reading a letter in which the writer said
that, on emptying a bag of flour he had lately
bought, he found the inside of the bag branded
“So-and-so’s dissolved bones—best artificial
manure.” He repacked the bag and returned the
flour to the storekeeper, who sent it back to the
miller—who turned out to be a one-time lecturer
of the Producers and Property Owners’ Associa-
tion ! That was the quaint concern which, while
really a Tory electioneering agency of the deepest
dye, pretended to be above all things the farmer’s
friend. If the Association believes in putting
flour into bags which have held bone manure, it
probably meant that it was the baby-farmer’s
friend.
“ P. 0.” makes a few remarks ;
New Victorian Governor T. Gibson-Carmichael early
got an example of what a pest the State Frights insect is
when it starts to buzz. Some people in London gave a
farewell dinner to the new Ex., and J. M. Davies, of Vic-
toria, upended himself. He fixed one eye on the guest
and the other on the tired pressmen, and he droned forth
at stupendous length about “Federal tyranny,” “ injus-
tice,” “States were determined to fight,” etc., etc.—all
the usual old gags. His Ex. looked cold and unhappy at
being dragged into the matter, the company yawned,
boredly, and the pressmen laid aside their pencils, and
gaped wearily among themselves, and still Davies
droned. Then there arose calls of “ time,” “ leave off,”
and ironical applause, and when that didn’t stop Davies’
drone the company just turned its chairs and conversed
loudly about any old thing, and let Davies drone to the
flies until he was too tired to drone any more. And all
the time Davies was of just as much account as a blue-
bottle calling aloud in a pickle jar.
The Victorian Government, by way of
encouraging Australian industry, has let a con-
tract to Lysaght Brothers, of Sydney, for 500
miles of wire-netting at £1 per mile above the
price at which the goods could be procured from
England. The Bent Government has at least
some vestiges of a broad Australian outlook. It
realises that it is good policy to pay a little extra
to encourage a N.S.W. industry, simply because
N.S.W. is part of Australia, so it spends £24,000
of its money in N.S.W. on principle, and the
Lysaght firm has re-engaged 150 lately-sacked
N.S.W. workmen in consequence. The N.S.W.
Ministry doesn’t do things that Way. It refused
to give a contract to Lysaght though that firm is
in its own State; and it sent to England for
some urgently-needed electrical machinery rather
than give Victorian or S.A. machinery makers
a show On this last item it gets its re-
ward, for the blessed property hasn’t turned up,
and for want of it the Sydney tramway system
will probably break down horribly during Yankee
Fleet week.

to send up the de
feated party hacks
to the Legislative
Council, and mollify
them with a little

and the chance of

But in these three-
or four-party days,
when there are al-
ways a few men
ready to move on to
whichever side con-
trols the loaves and

been found for the

squeezed out of a
Ministry to make
room for the new-
comer is “ called ” to
the “Upper” House. The present Government
crowd has now three men in the Council whO' used

Re the legal opinion for which the N.S.W.
tram employees paid good money the other day,
and which set forth that dismissed members of
the service have no right to appeal to a court of
law, it is worthy of remembrance that the N.S.W.
Civil Service Bill, when it was before the Assem-
bly, contained a clause giving employees the right
of legal appeal. The measure passed the Assem-
bly with that clause still in it, but the Council
fixed its old and rheumy eye upon the proviso, and
it went out. The ancients were always great at
throwing things out. Even members who can
hardly totter or see recover their lost youth for a
moment when there is something to be thrown out.
Harwood, of the Victorian House of Fat,
is a very mild person, but when up-
holding Stakes in the Country, he
can be bitter indeed. When the
nice, old, unnecessary gentlemen
met after the recess, Harwood
made a speech, purporting to pro-
phesy his and his colleagues’ atti-
tude on the land legislation to be
introduced, so Bent says, during
the session. He warned everybody
that the blessed Uppah House was
going to assert its dignity, and
would not swallow anything and
everything without due and proper
time for reflection. “ The Govern-
ment desires to take the power to
deal with any man’s land as it
pleases,” he remarked, almost
broken up with amazement; “I
hope no member of this House
will entertain that proposal for a moment.”
The anti-Socialistic bodies that are scat-
tered about the Commonwealth are to hold a big
conference in Sydney next October. Mr. John
Stinson is apparently boss of the movement.
Aunty Sosh threatens to settle the following
questions at the conference : (1) The Australian
Navy; (2) the State Hebts ; (3) the Liquor Pro-
blem ; (4) the Censorship of Literature; (5) State
Rights. From this list it would appear as if
Aunty mistakes herself for a very vigorous female
indeed.
Rev. Charles Strong complained, the
other day, that “the people can’t get on the
land ”; whereupon Victoria’s humorous Bent
knight has done some more bellowing. It was
most unfair, he said—why, land was even now
being thrown open in the N.-W. Mallee district.
Which is true. Bent has thrown open at least
4000 acres in the last four months; Yet if he
threw open 10,000 acres a week it would take him
about 55 years to adequately settle the cultivable
but as-yet-uncultivated lands of the State. The
job is too big for 20 Bents ; but a steep graduated
land-tax would set a thousand land-grabbers to
work getting their waste areas occupied
in a violent hurry.
THE NEW FINANCE.
No one can witness the present trend of Common-
wealth finance without serious misgivings.— Argus.
ARGUS: “ It’s not like the State finance of our
young days, is it, George, when we pawned
every stick in the place and left the children
to pay our debts. That was finance, to be
sure! ”
Apparently, where the Customs are con-
cerned—which is to say, where the people’s purse
is concerned —the blessed importer can’t be
honest ! The other day one of him appeared
before the Melbourne Customs Court, charged
with passing a little lot of German-made alarm
clocks as British, in order to escape with the
preferential duty of 20 per cent, instead of the
ordinary 30 per cent. He was a large importer,
and he only made about 30s. by the swindle; but
apparently it is as hard for the importer to go
straight as it is for the professional pickpocket to
resist “lifting” when he gets a chance, even if it
is only a “brummy” chain. He simply to to
do it.
Bent’s Indeterminate Sentences Act came
into operation on July I—-the1 —-the part dealing with
habitual criminals, that is. The sections provid-
ing for the probation and reform of hopeful cases
are still inoperative, because Bent and Co. haven’t
yet found time to provide reformatories. Chief
Sec. Peacock has had six months to do the work
in, and both Castlemaine and Beecbworth gaols
are practically empty ; but apparently it takes the
hesitating Peacock a couple of years to do a little
job like that. Either that or Peacock’s connection
with wild-cat promoters and Red Indian insur-
ance fakirs has given him the opinion that all
mankind is hopeless and incorrigible, and that
reformatories are, therefore, wasted brick and
mortar.
THE BUSH OF POPULATION.
The country wants land settlement without land rob-
bery.—ARGUS.
Some people’s idea of nice land settlement
without robbery.
Practical Adelaide is proposing—through
the Chamber of Manufactures—that all city shop-
keepers shall fix a day during the September
Show, when the streets are crowded with country
visitors, for exhibiting in their windows only
goods of Australian manufacture. Governor Le
Hunte is backing up the movement, and has
promised to head a tour of inspection of the
various displays. Adelaide, it may be remarked,
makes cement and locomotives and fiddles and
cricket bats and many other unexpected things,
besides its familiar flour and wine and olive oil.
Brudder Snowball, of the Orange organisa-
tion, sees the insidious paw of Pome in the fact
that the Christian Brothers’ schools in Victoria
are going to supply three battalions of cadets.
What on earth is wrong with Snowball ? If war
comes, and somebody must be killed, isn’t it as
good for a Roman Catholic to be killed as a Pro-
testant ? Hoes Snowball want only his own crowd
to be wiped out on the horrid field of battle ?
“ Matai ” : Apropos a Bulletin par
(18/6/’08) re Prime Minister Deakin’s threat to
extinguish the legislative questioner “without
notice,” M.L. Parliament suffered badly from that
person until Seddon (just before he passed
Beyond) wiped out the time-wasting pest
effectively. His scheme, which is in
operation to-day, is that all ques-
tions asked must be in writing.
Each question appears on the
Order paper on the day fo -
lowing its acceptance by the
House, and on every Wed-
nesday (provided it is a
sitting day) the questions
and the Ministerial replies
are printed and appended
to the said Order paper. This
idea completely blighted the
incorrigible time-waster and
destroyed his chance of debating
at considerable length absurd ques-
tions about: “Whether the Govern-
ment will take into consideration the
advisableness of putting a fresb coat of paint on
the railway goods shed at Jeroboamville. ”
Very early in their career the new Com-
missioners of the N.S.W. Government Savings
Bank, which nowadays is really a State Bank,
bumped against the Auditor-General. When
Alex. Mclntosh and his two colleagues presented
their first balance-sheet, the Auditor-General
wanted some alterations made in it. For instance,
he wanted the value of unused postage stamps
credited back to profit and loss account; and
President Mclntosh, who has spent a good part of
his life auditing bank accounts, wouldn’t do it.
Instead, the Commissioners present their figures
in their own way, tack the Auditor-General’s
remarks to their report, and tell Parliament in a
note that “except as an attempt to dogmatically
assert the dominance of departmental officers sup-
posed to be skilled in matters affecting audit ” it
is impossible to understand what the Auditor-
General means by all the commotion ; also they
mention that what they were asked to do was
“ contrary to the principles of audit as applied to
balance-sheets.” It is a nice little row, which
Parliament is sure to chew over sooner or later.
AVE ATQUE YALE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little ones requested the Minister (Mr. Sachse,
M.L.C.) to inform Lord Northcote that the children of
Victoria feared God and honored the King ; and in the
presence of the Flag they wished him to take that
message to His Majesty the King.— Argus patriotic
report.
Peanuts strew the path of Fate,
Aldermen creep in the mud !
“Vale 1” passing potentate !
Babies of the Bulldog Blood
Shout to far off Royalty,
Pap and peanut Loyalty.
Hear our kiddies rant and roar
To the purple, to the gold ;
Speed a parting Governor
Shivering with rain and cold 1
One whole day from lessons free
Vivifies tfieir Loyalty.
Drenched on Bent’s Imperial sod
Three-year Patriots squeakers fling
Over Northcote ; fear their God,
Publicly proclaim the King.
What a nation we shall be
Fed on State school Loyalty 1
For Our Boys will learn to grope
After Sachse ; to be soon
Viscounts of the Sacred Soap,
Barons of Ned’s Gilt Spittoon,
Ushers of the Buns and Tea —
Knights, like Bent, of Loyalty.
As they clutch their lucky-bag,
Little Willie, Little Eve,
Bare of head, salute the Flag,
Edward hath no cause to grieve ;
For all’s well with Royalty,
Saved by Toodles’ Loyalty.
VIC.
L.E.
THE FALLEN IDOL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Satan, Beelzebub, Apollyon, Nick,
Mahound, the Devil, Zernebock, the deuce,
Behold me squirm, my heart is very sick,
Teach me some scorching terms of new abuse.
Lo ! this gold bullock that we reared for you,
This pleasant god our own fat hands have made
Is tumbled from his perch, and all may view
The sacrilegious murder of Freetrade.
We knew indeed that all his feet were mud ;
But it was holy mud, we taught the crowd.
We sprinkled all his altars with much blood,
And we made merry when their heads they bowed
In worship. “See, how cheap is bread !” we cried.
“ Buy in the cheapest market! ” chanted they.
“ Sell in the dearest 1 ” we with zest replied.
Alas 1 Where are those worshippers to-day ?
All lost and gone 1 Protection rears her head ;
Our altar fires are burning very low.
Our coffers empty, and the poor have bread,
And we are damned, for all the people know !
MAORILAND.
D.M.W.

THE COUPON SYSTEM.
CHAPTER VI.
A THIRD objection raised against the
system is one raised on behalf of the
shopkeepers, viz., that it creates a monopoly.
We suppose the people who use this word mean that it gives the shop-
keeper, who uses Coupons, an advantage over the man who does not.
Well, we admit at once that the shopkeeper who does adopt the Coupon
system does a very much better business than the man who does not, but
the latter has only got himself to blame if he does not adopt the Coupon
system also, because if he does not like our Coupons he can get Coupons
from another Company, and you will readily see that if there is more than
one Coupon Company there cannot be any monopoly. The only other
objection that is raised is that the goods given in redemption of Coupons
are not always of the value they are represented to be. Well, we suppose
there are some discontented people, but in our experience they are very
few, and the fact that our showrooms are thronged every day with people
redeeming Coupons, who go away perfectly happy and contented with what
they have chosen, is the best evidence that good values are given. The
goods are displayed for inspection by all, and careful housewives know the
value of these things better than anyone else, and if they saw that they
were not being fairly treated they would very soon complain and give up
the system, and so the Company would come to grief.
We hope that in these six chapters we have fully explained the system,
and shown you that it is a good one, and that it is honestly administered,
and that you will do well to adopt it, whether you are a storekeeper or a
customer of a storekeeper.
Visit the Showrooms:
387 George Street, Sydney
(Opposite The Strand).

I’m the Robur Tea Girl, ,
and I’m going to tell you one
way that experts test tea—
After the tea has been made
and poured off, they examine
the infused leaves that remain
in the teapot,
PURE TEA.—The leaves
have a bright reddish-coppery
(like a new penny) color —and,
as a rule, the brighter they
look the finer the quality of
the tea.
COMMON or FAULTY
TEA. —The leaves are dull
brown, with an occasional
dark leaf, and sometimes, little
pieces of black stuff among
them.
ARTIFICIALLY
FLAVORED TEA—The leaves
are reddish-brown, with green
leaves among them—these
Green ones vary in number
according to the proportion
of flavoring that has been used,
and they vary in color from
dark olive to the very brightest
green—whether this artificial
stuff is good to drink —we
leave you to judge—all we can
say is. nothing would induce
us to mix it with Robur—
now you can judge your own
tea !
P.S —The No. 2 Grade Robur
is splendid tea for every-
day family use. All grades
of Robur are pure tea.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 21, 1908.
My Dear Arini, —
One of Sydney’s prettiest and most popular girls
hied her up an ocean-going steamer’s gang-plank on
Wednesday last, and shortly after was whisked
through the Heads and awa’. In plain Australian,
Miss Irene Marks has left for foreign regions. An
imposing tally of unsuccessful suitors ranged up
with valedictory garden produce. The purchaser
of Glenrock looked as though news of the destruc-
tion by fire of his property in an uninsured state
had just reached him. Far more impressive in
the matter of Great Grief, however, was the
adorer of the bunch—the tame and faithful’cat,
who has fed docilely out of the hand for so
many years. He stood on the Blue-Petered
craft’s deck anchored to an immense mass
of vegetation, till rough, coarse stewards
jostled him off, and then he wandered brokenly
ashore. As the vessel edged towards the fairway
he might have been observed leaning helplessly
against a sack of bonedust, tears dimming his
specs—a classic figure of Sorrow. Indeed and
indeed the lot of a jettisoned adorer of years is a
passing sore one. He has grown used to the rdle
of human hat-rack ; he is accustomed to being a
Eiece of animate furniture on which the lady and
er friends pile their coats, hats, gamps and so
forth at theatre parties and the like. Of a sudden
he finds his occupation gone. There is no one
who wants to hang sof tgoods on him. The world
appears to him a desolate spot. He is thus
enabled to grasp the feelings of a “scrapped”
motor-car; and that seems about the only ad-
vantage of the position.
After all, it seems that women, and the people
who will be women by-and-bye if they live long
enough, make more'use of the technical colleges
in N. S. Wales than the mere males do. In
Sydney and other parts they teach about 100 sub-
jects (it is marvellous how subjects have been
divided up and splintered these days ; you can,
for instance, learn no less than 12 sorts of drawing
and even that number doesn’t include the art of
drawing a chequ e for seven figures, so that the Bank
of N. S. Wales will give you good gold - for it).
From out of these hundred subjects the figures
that relate to dressmaking stand up like
the Himalayas above all the rest; the
only other classes that have nearly as
many patrons are those that teach “ cookery,
domestic science and laundry work ” —there are
1172 students in the tacking and 874 in the cook-
ing section. The Technical Education Depart-
ment teaches iron-founding, too; but in all the
classes in all the State there are only 19 yotjng
men who want to know the mysteries af that
business. Which is pretty sorrowful when you
come to think of it.
In pushing the cookery classes both at the
Technical Colleges and the bigger public school,
the Department is, so to speak, fattening two
birds with one stove (that looks mixed, but you’ll
straighten it out if you take time.) When the
cookery business started the trouble was to get the
goods off the cook’s hands. Cakes and such like
the budding cook might take home ; but Irish stew
doesn’t carry well through a crowd. But nowa-
days dinner is served, and for two shillings or
half-a-crown a week young Sydney Augustus
and Ethel May may pass in from the other classes
and have their three-course dinner. Perhaps five-
pence is still reckoned a ha’penny or so too much
to pay for young Sydney Augustus’s mid-day
meal; but, even so, there isn’t as much surplus
Irish stew as there used to be, and the path of
the cookery mistress is made smoother. Thus
Augustus is fed in his youth, while Emmeline, of
the cookery class, learns how to feed Augustus’s
big brother, from whom she expects things later on.
A correspondent writes this item: “I saw
Mrs. Besant the other night, and she is
indeed a comely-looking Theosophist. In her
white robe, with angel sleeves, and her glittering
gem, jewel, or order at her full, round throat, she
is a picturesque and wholesome figure of a woman.
She seems what a lady bishop of the Episcopal or
Roman Church would be if ladies held office of
that kind. The head—you don’t see the grandeur
of it till the lecturer turns sideways—is massive,
and the soft, white, short spun-silky hair forms
the halo which the Besant face seems to call for.
The complexion is as pink as a rose-petal, and
most wonderfully unlined, and the hands, small,
slender, and artistic, show the same time-defying
quality. She uses practically no gestures what-
ever—just a slight outstretching of one hand and
a movement of the body now and then. The
voice, full of energy, is splendid, and, like the
artist she is, she waits for no applause. If Mrs.
Besant would only publish the occult secret of her
astounding complexion, she would make her for-
tune in six months; also she would bring her
audiences to their Heaven (if not to hers). The
road to woman’s earthly Paradise is along the
pavement of rejuvenation.”
Maggie, an aboriginal, was married to W. Kirk-
wood, at Norseman (W.A.), the other day. The
bride was “attired in black lustre, with kangarGO
and dingo-tail trimmings.” I have no doubt about
the good faith of the Age fashion writer who pro-
vides these details, but should like further infor-
mation re the kangaroo-tail trimmings. It does
not seem to me that the tail of the kangaroo lends
itself to frivolity of this kind. It makes very fair
soup, but soup doesn’t look well when cut bias and
worn in a hat. How were the tails put on—whole
or in sections ? Did Maggie drag them behind or
wear them as a boa ? It seems to me that the
bride who would go to the altar trimmed with
kangaroo tails would not disdain to wear a Ger-
man bologna as a hair ornament, and a leg of
mutton as a breast pin, with a ham or two for
general decoration. Possibly Mr. and Mrs. Kirk-
wood and friends ate the bride’s trimmings at the
wedding breakfast.
Mr. H. S. Nicholas, Mr. Arthur Adams, and some
other of Sydney’s bright young men have been
persuading Margaret Anglin, by proxy, to return
to Sydney at the beginning of next year and stage
some Shaw plays. “ Arms and the Man,” “ Cap-
tain Brassbound’s Conversion,” and “Candida”
(what an excellent poet young Titheradge would
makeJ) were talked of, and the lady, who is a
keen Shaw enthusiast, is so willing that she says
she is prepared to sink a wad of her own private
cash in the venture if J.C. W. will take it up. The
idea is to have a subscription scheme among local
Shaw-worshippers, so that there may be no likeli-
hood of the company playing to deserted
upholstery. As a fact there would probably be
no need for this. Sydney has a Shaw public, like
most other big cities, and the chances are that the
manager who first gives these vital plays a trial
will strike something of a bonanza.
We first heard distinctly and audibly of Miss
Irene Ainsley when Melba got up the famous
concert in the. Bechstein Hall for the young con-
tralto’s debut in London. The Prince and Princess
of Wales came to the Bechstein for the first time in
their august lives, and never before in the Hall was
seen such exhibitions of spine-bending. Melba her-
self played Miss Ainsley’s accompaniments. Mar-
chesi was present, and the advertisement resulting
was past all whooping, as they say in Shakspeare.
Last week, at the invitation of flautist Lemmone,
a small herd of persons assembled in Paling’s
concert room to diagnose the contralto’s quality.
She has a fine, dramatic voice—unusually fine in
the lowest register. It could do with more train-
ing and general riveting up and screwing down.
The low, stifling roof of the concert room did
not allow the voice the fairest of hearings for one
thing. And the young Maorilander, no doubt,
did not look her best. She wore a wide, smother-
ing hat and a costume with reach-me-down attri-
butes. Possibly she had no heels, or very low
ones, on her shoes, for she appeared to move flat-
footedly. Some of the disjointedness of her voice
is apparent in her bodily movements. Melba, I
reckon, would be the first to tell her protegee of the
extent to which a harmonious appearance backs a
harmonious voice. Miss Ainsley needs to stage-
manage herself with the aid of a skilled dress-
maker and a critical physical-culturist. She will
open her concert season in Auckland, and in the
sweet by-and-bye will lift her voice in Sydney.
The interview of Miss Rose Scott, Mrs. Hugh
Dixson, Mrs. Jenkin, Miss Atkinson and Miss
Fry with Minister for Public Instruction Hogue
looks in the reports like an Anthony Hope
dialogue. Nobody finishes a sentence; always
somebody interrupts. Not one purr of Miss Scott’s
but was broken in upon by a Hoguean ultimatum :
“That has already received my attention,” or
“ That has already been determined upon.” Yes,
there was one purr : “ You must have let us come
just to hear us praise you.” For all the deputa-
tion had come to ask had already been set down
for granting by the Minister. What the dep.
wanted was an amendment of the Truancy Act.
What about giving Lady Northcote Gibbons’
“Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire” (in
eight volumes) as a farewell bit o’ lumber ?
Mayoress Hughes presided at a meeting called by
her last week to decide upon a suitable remem-
brancer to her Ex. from the.women of Sydney.
No decision was arrived at, and the Mayoress
didn’t hear me make my eight-volume suggestion.
The 150 women present will do a big think
between now and next Eriday about the
raising of the subscription and the spending
thereof. The fearfully prosperous-looking Mrs.
Hugh Dixson made some useful suggestions.
Her suggestions are always useful, but they were
lost because as soon as she stood up the listeners
became gazers only at her furs. No one made the
perfectly sane suggestion that all those who have
been in the habit of tearing a bit of food with
their Excellencies should send their cheques for
five guineas each to the Mayoress, and so settle
half of the problem. If the Mayoress would like
a list I’ll draw one up.
As the matter now stands Mrs. Hughes is Presi-
dent, and has Mrs. Barton, Mrs. Wade, Mrs.
Suttor, Mrs, J. M. Gordon, and Mrs. Ashton
acting with her.
Lady Northcote, attended by Early Christian
Martyr Fyers and Captain Stephens, returned to
Sydney on Thursday, and on Friday night she
and Miss Rawson, attended by the faithful and
adhesive Wilson, took a dose of Culture from
Professor Henry Jones at the University. The
Professor continued his serial (or python) story
on Philosophy and Modern Life. He wrestled
with the Deep Down and the Far Qff, or Self in
Relation to Society, and a lady graduate friend of
mine kept murmuring, “O, how acute, how
acute ! ” as if the Prof, were neuralgia.
The Robertson-Bettington wedding took a
Crowd of smart Sydneysiders to Parramatta last
week. The squatter bridegroom’s holding is
Toganmajn, in N.S.W., and the bride is Beatrix,
youngest daughter of Bettington, of Oatlands,
one of the haute noblesse of that very hot (in
summer) flat district of past grandeur, Parra-
matta. She was decked in Empire dress
of white satin, rose point, silver cords,
—the usual bridal garniture, only more so and on
the more artistic and costly scale. Pretty Miss
Esme Sly was one of the bridesmaids, and Mr.
Oliver Osborne was letter-perfect in his r6le of
best man. A motor-car, a present from the bride-
groom’s mother, bore the joyous pair away on
their honeycomb. When you get a wedding
present of a motor-car you can afford to dispense
with the singing of the hymn, “ O Perfect Love.”
Miss Elsie MacDonald, daughter of the new
Under-Secretary for Lands, is preparing to climb
the orange-tree and fetch down the harmless
necessary blossom. Her future partner is one of
the squatter Macks of Narandera.
Another nice girl, who has been teaching music
at Paling’s, is graduating into matrimony about
the end of this month. She is Miss Lindeman,
daughter of Captain Lindeman, of Woollahra.
She marries a sprig of the Pelham Clinton family.
It was one of that totem who was for a while hus-
band of May Yohe. the music-hall ladv.
Yet a third brace are qualifying for the as-
long-as-ye-both-shall-live blessing—Mr.. Montagu
Francis Morgan, third son of Mr. George Mor-
gan, of Neutral Bay, and Miss Cecilia Isabella
Scott, second daughter of Mr. Barry Scott of
“ Stirling,” Mosman.
In the monthly dress competition that is run by
the St. James’s Gazette and Evening Standard
Mrs. W. B. Dailey has taken first prize for the
design for an Ascot gown. It is described as “a
charming toilette in ivory-white crepe-de-chine
meteor and ninon embroidered in marguerites,
with leaves of palest green. The design is a
particularly graceful one, the Greek influence
being shown in the case of the tunic skirt and
plainly cut corsage.” The yoke is of finely tucked
net cut out over a little vest of lace, while folds of
the same edged with gold are crossed over the
bust just above the opening of the corsage. It all
sounds “charming” enough, and so does the
paper’s practice of having the gown “carried out ”
by Ernest and presented to the designer and
prize-winner. Ascot is the best-frocked of English
outdoor frisks, so Mrs. Dailey, encircled by her
own creation, ought to take the eye “amongst
those present.”
The latest tone in toast or tan —the “ pale ale ”
shade that took the Viennese such time and
money to develop—was worn here for the first
time in Sydney by Mrs. Fred Shipman at the
reception given her at the Australia by Mrs. J. C.
Williamson and Mrs. C. Deane. The non-
temperance shade was in materials of silk net
over silk. A white hat, with white feathers and
some gold, “went with ” the frock harmoniously.
It was a huge and merry party.
Mrs. W. H. Weston, a Point Piperess with red
gold hair and Clara Butt height (not width), leaves
Sydney presently for a visit to foreign parts. Her
doctor hubby remains here earning the well-
buttered crust. Before and after crust-earning
hours he cultivates the loveliest pansies and carna-
tions. Their pretty villa, “Coowong,”is to be
inhabited by the American (Dr.) Maxwells, who
have been tethered in Armidale. He is the sugar
expert, and she is reputed to be fearfully smart —
the last word in fashion.
Other near departers from this State are Mrs.
and Miss J. H. Want. The Cornishes (Mrs. and
Miss), who went hence a while ago, are having a
chromatic time with the Wilders. The Wilder
lady’s motor and jewelled snake and frocks are
still recollected by Sydneysiders.
Nicholson and Co. have been showing a window
full of beautifully printed and illustrated musical
compositions, by Mrs. Beresford Brodie. Dance
music would seem to be Mrs. Brodie’s specialty.
Her Beresford Waltz has been played at recent
Government House balls, and helps to fill the
intervals at the theatres. The Royal and the
Langley Waltzes are also her work, and provoca-
tive’ of a desire to solve perpetual motion. The
composer is a daughter of Archdeacon Langley,
but, fortunately, her musical bent didn’t run to
depressing hymn compositions.
Mark Hambourg’s baby girl Sonia is by way of
proving a good advertising asset. She is like an
owl in an ivy bush, the very image of Pa—but she
may grow out of it. Sonia bossed the Manuka,
and was beloved for her cuteness and sweetness ;
and the Musician with the Mane is her slave, and
alludes to her respectfully as “ Miss Hambourg.”
Sonia’s Ma, who was a Miss Muir Mackenzie, is
very tall, very thin, and has the valuable posses-
sions of good teeth and eyes. Mark’s musical
season is going to be a financial failure—sure.
But anyone in the instrumental musical groove
would “frost” at this juncture. Sydney is
yearning to hear the boom of big guns, not the
melody of big chords crashed out by even such
master hands as Hambourg’s.
The Art Society’s annual ball (plain and fancy
dress) will occur at Paddington Town Hall on
July 30th. Tickets may be procured from Mr.
A. J. Daplyn, Vickery’s Chambers.
“ Mario ” writes from Brisbane :
There’s one thing that worries the R.C. fraternity in
this city. The vice-regal dinners are invariably held on a
Friday. This would be bearable enough were the wily
flounder or the skipsome skate present in sufficient force,
but often there is not a fin to be seen. And it is a hor-
rible temptation when the devout Romaness has helped
herself (for politeneSs-sake ; it does look so bad to keep
on refusing things !) to have to leave the wing of the
duckling on her plate for her soul’s sake, and exist on a
mere pea or so.
There’s a story going the rounds this week, which I’m
sure isn’t true, of a high horse-marine dignitary, who fell
a victim to’flu’just as his best friend arrived from the
South for a week or sO—“ to escape the Southern frosts
you know.” A feeble scrawl to the effect that he was
laid low on the field of battle was her only greeting. But
she was a woman of action, and next day the masthead
soldier on duty at the gangway of the barracks was con-
fronted with a be-uti-ful nurse, in spotless garb. She
had come to take charge of the invalid. The patient
nearly had a fit when she arrived, but, all the same, he is
recovering rapidly, and will soon be charging in a hollow
square on his vessel’s deck once more.
The rush for the imported Bury Ann leads to weird
complications. A week or two ago a country dame, pos-'
sessed of 'more lucre than perception, sallied forth to
engage a “chummie,” at the Kangaroo Point dep6t,
where they keep the rosy English maid until she accepts a
place. Mrs. Money Bags has a way with her, so she
managed to bustle through the crowd and got in about
fifth. Seeing a likely-looking person near the end
of the room, she rustled up that way, and having planted
herself in front of her intended victim, eyed, her up and
down, from her heels to her head and then from her top
hairpin to her boots. Then she inquired, “ Are you en-
gaged?” The addressed one lighted up her eye with agleam
of amusement. “ Er —no—I’m married,” she remarked
languidly moving away. And it took the flurried dame
about half a second to realise that the female to whom
she had almost offered a kitchen job had been in the
inner circle of Society when she herself was struggling
with the dishes in the kitchen on pa’s selection. Why
anyone should worry over such a trivial mistake, I don’t
know, but I hear the Money Bag's family has taken to its
bed and is receiving medical attendance.
The wonderful Children’s Hospital held its annual
meeting in a quiet manner last Wednesday afternoon,
Her Ex. occupied the chair and said amiable things
about progress being sure if slow. It was a peaceful day,
and the lions of the Committee lay down with the lambs,
so'to speak ; but the fact leaked out, nevertheless, that
unless something or somebodj’ gets a hustle on, another
diphtheria outbreak is expected in the germ-pervaded
wooden structure.
It’s about time that the ladies of the W. C. T. Union
had their attention called to that little paragraph in the
Scriptures which advises all and sundry to respect
truth. The advertisements they put in the papers
touching the attractiveness of their meetings and
the brilliance of their orators are a disgrace
to any Christian body. They unblushingly affirm
that their specially-imported lectures?, Miss Fitzjob, is
another Demosthenes in skirts or a Cicero in corsets.
Well, you go to hear her. Your first view of the meeting
is a blow, for it consists of about 30 untidy women in
drab clothes. But Miss Demosthenes ! The last one I
heard had a motherly figure, a mouth likedhe delta of the
Nile, and a voice that suggested the raven which
had a cold and said “Deverbore.” Her British
language matched her voice. She talked of the
“perlice,” “the seatings of the Suprime Court,”
“ What we do in the Dough-minion,” and the “lovely
young lady who sung us a solo.” She told two hoary
chestnuts, and declared them to have been epi-
sodes that came under her personal notice, and in
conclusion she naively remarked, “ Of course your light
mayn’t be as brilliant as mine.” How the meeting ended
I don’t know, for just here I went out with thoughts too
awful to be repeated.
There was a jolly little dance on board the Ortona, at
Pinkinbar on Friday night. It was quite informal —just
about 20 girleens in their prettiest frocks, and as many
blue-coated officers and mere shoremen. Miss Edith
Mason had the arrangements in hand.
A smileful story which is going the rounds this week
has to do with a recent private and under-manned dance.
The despairing hostess, having got all the available males
going, retired to the drawing-room for a brief respite. There
she was confronted with no less than 19 unappropriated
blessings. The sight of so much untwirled chiffon made
her light-headed, and to collect her wavering senses she
talked wildly of the flowers which adorned the room.
“ And what do you think of my wall flowers ? ” she asked
of an acid dame in the corner, indicating a bowl upon a
table near by. “ I think they are looking as bright as
can be expected, seeing there’s no one to dance with
them,” was the blighting reply.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

The Asiatic Menace
During the next six months The Lone Hand
will give serious attention to the Asiatic menace.
The reports of its special commissioner, Louis
Esson, after a close study of conditions in China
and Japan, will be published, and with them
other articles by resident Europeans in Asia.
It is work of great national value, and appro-
priately enough, will begin with the August
number, the Special American Fleet Number,
which will be a souvenir of the historic event
that brings a new White Power into the Pacific.
We want Australians to read these Asiatic
Menace articles, every one of them. Are you on
the subscribers’ list ? If not, see to it at once. It
will cost you 12s. post free, direct from the office
or from your nearest news-agent.
