MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 20, 1908.
Mr Dear Yarrie, —
The V. A.S. had its official opening last Tuesday
afternoon. It was a crowded chaotic episode
in two spasms. President Mather’s mild opening
speech comprised one. spasm—of enforced, unwill-
ing silence. The mad endeavor of everyone to see
somebody, else, and so prevent all from seeing a
single picture, made up the second. An artists’
affair is always amusing to me. Igoto it as Ido
to the Zoo or a reformatory, expecting to have
my excited apprehension disappointed—which
duly happens on each occasion. In the last in-
stance, all the pigment-flingers were on view, and
nary aone wore a bow- tie; in fact, several woref rock-
coats and demeanors that even surpassed the mis-
shapen respectability of those garments. Bernard
Hall paraded his inscrutable frontispiece around,
pursued by anxious eyes. His students, past and
present, followed him to their various works, and
waited ox-like for a vanity-killing blow. But
mighty Mr. Hall was merciful, and never said
anything more cutting than “umph.” Blatnire
Young centred the most pleasant circle, and said
smart things. And John Ford Paterson and
Fred McCubbin collogued with Gaelic en-
thusiasm. The actor Wickham reared his bulk
beside ruddy Cornelius McNally. Mrs. Buchanan
and Mrs. Jas. Gibb struck the high-water line in
the way of frocks—Mrs. B. in a picturesque
arrangement of blue velvet, with a monstrous
mauve-feathered hat; Mrs. Gibb in a fine cloth
tailored gown of creaseless fit, and a rose-trimmed
lid. Miss le Soeur was a lovely pastel arrange-
ment of pale prettiness and grey furs, with a
violet-flowered toque. And Mrs. McCubbin was
a radiant, good-humored filling for a green Empire
shape. Mrs. E. P. Fox wore a surprising black
velvet princess garment with dead white flaps, and
a white pussy-cat cape which belied her nature.
Ruby Lindsay assaulted her reputation as a studio
belle with a hat which looked like a soup-plateful
of cherries ; and pretty Miss Prendergast and her
mother, Mrs. George Michael, arrayed themselves
respectively in simple blue and ornate brown.
Dora Sommers, in a quiet tweed, and a dame in a
courageous French hat of bright yellows com-
pleted the score of people whom one looked at
hard. Web Gilbert was on view, and, of course,
Mrs. Art-patroness Laurie Abrahams,- in black
chiffon, and accompanied by a cluster of sisters.
Nowadays no artistic function has a better raison
d'etre than the presence of the intelligent picture-
purchasing family of Abrahams.
An Australienne in London writes to say that
she’d just finished buying bargains in Bond-street
when she stumbled across two Victorian beauties.
One was Hal Cohen, the large, blonde, melodious
tenor of gorgeous memory, whom we once cherished
here. T’other was Elsie Moore, now Mrs. Million-
aire Starr.
Hal was sublimely tailored, and slimmer and
handsomer than of old. Elsie Moore was a
bundle of chinchilla and ermine furs, which alighted
from an electric landau. She was merely
a margin on either side of a stream of diamonds,
and her hair was a shade too red, and her cheeks
also, to conform to the best English ideals of
beauty. She was supercilious rather, and couldn’t
remember “Florodora.” But she was full of in-
formation about her hubby’s family’s devotion to
aerintberdle of daughter-in-law, and she ended
:>y taking along the one who writes to me while
die bought five hats at prices that ranged up to
iwenty-eiglit guineas. Why, oh! why was
your Johanna not made into a comic opera
chorister in her past youth !
Unie Russell, the golden-haired girl who played
“seconds” to Maud Jeffries during her two years’
flutter with the Julius Knight company of yore,
writes to Melbourne friends in enthusiastic
praise of London and its medical talent. Four
months ago Miss Unie and her mother set out to
the Cold Country on pleasure bent. A week after
their introduction to the Great Smoke the youth-
ful-looking mater was stricken down by ’citis,
plus complications. Pleasure flew out of the
window as the doctor came in at the door. In fact,
several doctors were concerned with the interesting
case, a surgeon named Ware achieving a wonderful
operation—since when the patient has been con-
valescing nicely. The Russells had been fortunate
in finding their old friend, Mrs. G. B. Lewis.
Mrs. G. B. it was who prepared Unie for the
stage, and when this alarming trouble befell the
two strangers in a strange London she took her
ex-pupil to live with her at Maida Yale, where
she and her son have a flat. Which reminds one
that Mrs. G. B. has had her troubles since her
return to England. Three months ago, her
brother, Willie Edouin, died, and in losing him
she lost his influence towards obtaining an
engagement.
What will become of “Chloe,” that famous
painting of the nude, owned by the late Surgeon
Fitzgerald? A year ago a squatter offered
Fitz £lOOO for it. It is one of the most exquisite
nude studies painted in modern times. It was
loaned to Melbourne National Gallery some years
ago, but created such public comment from the
people with the Seven Deadly Virtues that it was
hustled back, blushing, to the shelter of the
owner’s Lonsdale-street house. It is Lefevre’s
“ Chloe,” and it was first exhibited in the Paris
Salon in 1875.
Bertie Zelrnan has begun his series of Quartet
chamber concerts in Glen’s Hall. The Quartet
consists of Hattenbach at the ’cello, and Zelman,
North, and Dawson, with violins and viola. If
one counts Hattenbach a naturalised Australian,
then the Quartet can claim pure Australian origin,
and its concerts are the finest instrumental music
in small doses laid on in Melbourne.
J.C.W.’s tall and beautiful conductor-man,
Howard Carr, has taken possession of his comic-
opera baton once more. The lengthy musician
has had a pain in his weskit, or some other subtle
sickness upon him, since he last waved a stick
here, and has been nursing it round on the other
side of Australia. It’s to be hoped that, with the
deposition of the tremendous Slap., a lighter
interpretation of the thrilling “Widow” waltz
will arise and please. My special expert informs
me that here one wouldn't; recognise the waltz as
the same bit of sensuousness that made London
breathe so loudly. I almost believe him.
Apropos to the Higginsoii, some more amusing
yarns circulate. Meeting a blithe comedian,
during a morning saunter, he asked, “ Have you
seen how (a certain unmentionable papah) is
cutting up young Bottomley about his vanity and
so on? Rather funny—what?” Ten minutes
later the rival warbler was asking the same ques-
tion of the same humorist. “Did you see
(certain paper) on Higginson ? Isn’t it cruel?”
Again, the “ Widow’s ” houses have decreased
in numbers. “ Mai people are not heah ! ”
explains Merry Andrew. “Mai people are
giving balls, and have many social duties
for the taime being. They will be back to watch
me again on Saturday. They are very faithful—
what ?” I suppose that any man’s common-sense
would be deranged if, after fourteen years of mostly
provincial mumming in the Supercilious hemi-
sphere, he were suddenly dumped into the centre
of a city of silly woman, and so fussed over.
Rupert Clarke returned badly damaged on
Wednesday last, after pursuing money and mos-
quitoes and other superfluities through Papooah
(is that the way Mr. Chapman says it should be
pronounced?). With him came his henchman,
A. S. Bloomfield, the man whose acquaintance-list
is everlastingly being curtailed owing to his
friends’ bankruptcies. Our only hereditary aris-
tocrat managed in his absence to smash up his
tendon Achilles. (Which is something to do with
the foot, and nothing to do with his collection of
Greek literature ; the latter is still intact, even to
its uncut leaves.) And he is limping round at
present upon a rare piece of furrin wood. The
hereditary aristocrat brought back a cargo of
gorgeous birds of Paradise, and skulls, and other
decorative details. Also he has brought with him
a live chowdoglososaurus—an almost extinct
animal of wonderful beauty, which he’s going to
use as a chaperone in his bachelor flat.
Miss Maisie le Soeur, the Rosetti-like damosel
who rears her slender throat out of Meynell and
Gunn’s melodrama trappings, is a cousin of Mrs.
Lily Langtry, that Helen of the Channel Islands
who iced King Neddie’s spine in the long, long
ago. Folk who admired the elder lady claim that
the Australian girl’s flower-like beauty has a
family likeness to enhance it.
That extraordinary effort of an American
imagination (aggravated possibly by drink and
cheese), “The Fatal Wedding,” lured a large
audience to the Royal on Saturday night, even
though it was its fourth revival. The show had a
new interest, on account of the Australianism of
its cast. Lilian Myers, who used to talk with a
miraculous accent in the Brune co., and pretty
Beatrice Holloway, besides the Williams infant
and a score of other brolgas, have the pick of the
parts these days. Miss Mvers plays the adven-
turess in a succession of beautiful gowns, which
are rather spoiled by her figure—the figure is
generous to the point of extravagance. Her even-
ing frocks were, one a poetic thing in black
crepe-de-chine, t’other a skin-tight sheath of gold
tissue veiled in jewelled tulle. A walking frock
of white ninon, with wide bands of flower-embroi-
dered net, was another charming arrangement.
The little Holloway, as long as the plot let her,
was expensively arrayed in mauve chiffons and
silver details.
In the audience, Mrs. Charlie Lempriere’s
golden head waved a huge aigrette above her
black and white net frock, and sundry operatic
individuals paraded their best bibs and tuckers
with much buoyancy. And, of course, the
übiquitous Boffard sat in a conspicuous seat,
where the whole house could wonder at him and
admire.
The Northcote dame dispensed her favors with
a lavish hand before she departed. The Writers’
Club got a fine collection of pictures bestowed
upon it; and the Austral Salon got another dozen.
All Miladi’s pet hen-coops got some souvenir, but
now some are sulky because the other hen-coop
got as much as they did, and there are bitter feel-
ings in chookdom.
The farewell singsong tendered by Melbourne
business girls to Dame Northcote sounded less
impressive than might have been. Various choirs
gave tongue vigorously under the guidance of a
conductor, but the serenaded lady looked down
from her balcony on a sea of silence, dotted by
islets of vociferous song, so numerous were the
damsels who came to sing but remained to stare.
Besides girls there were mere men in evidence,
and certain ancient and unlooked-for crones
prowled round the Government House porches,
and peered up at Mrs. Northcote on her balcony
as if they expected vice-regal gratitude to material-
ise in a shower of flannel petticoats and the
popular brass-bound ulsters.
Melbourne’s parting view of the Northcote
Lady will be remembered as a feast of color. An
aggressive turquoise-blue feather nodded to the
multitude, in unison with the vice-regal head. A
bouquet of wattle yellow quivered in the smiling
Dame’s hand, and the cushion that supported her
through the triumphal ride to Spencer-street
station furnished a royal purple background to the
picture.
Mrs. Robert Harper is god-mothering a pecu-
liar scheme. Lizzie Newbold, a rather mature
wearer of a long plait and short petticoats—-a girl
with a fine soprano voice—is being floated into a
company under the auspices of that lady and
others. The 1200 shares are at the normal guinea,
amounting when fully subscribed to £1260. This
sum is to be applied to the cultivation of the
Newbold voice. I don’t know the exact arrange-
ments about paying dividends, but I have been
told that the co. will get one year of the finished
Lizzie’s services. The young woman, meanwhile,
is to be insured, so that, if she should take fire
and be burned to the ground, the co. will not be
at a total loss. But it is hoped that, at the worst.
Miss Newbold will be extinguished before any
serious damage is done.
These nights our Vienna Cafe is doing its best
to look like a smart supper-room. There’s quite a
recrudescence of the late-at-night conviviality.
This is all owing to the two opera companies.
The brightest show-ladies have each a following
of men, and often six men sup one lady by
turns. When Smith is the favored host of a
favorite beauty, six mere Browns and Robinsons
combine forces and sit girlless at an opposite
table to ogle her. Therefore our cafes are
crowded. On Saturday night the adorers and the
adored were in full force. The Sinclair Grace
with elongated Miss Cottee were on view,
with their respective hair crops bound into
quaint parodies of classic Greek coiffures. Little
Gwen Boyce, looking schoolgirly in a simple blue
silk garment, was close by with an attentive
Jewish youth. John Gunn and Manager Wen-
man presided over another table ; while little
Willie Cromwell and his dainty missus sat further
along amidst the Philistines. That was as much
of “ Miss Hook ” as one could see from a corner.
Then there was Florrie Young, gorgeous in green
and black chiffons, with a set of superb diamond-
set emeralds, shedding a radiance on her
vivacity. With her were more mummers,
and Reginald Roberts. Behind her Carrie
Moore, at an orchid-decked spread, sat
with a furry-looking worshipper. Our Carrie is
getting into bad habits as far as her appearance
goes. She had her hair screwed into a sorry little
knob, which disappeared behind a blue bow,
bigger by half than her heaving young bosom : and
she wore a fatigued-looking frock of the same
color, which she wisely concealed under a rosy
burnoose. Claude Bantock and the mildly aesthetic
Mr. Sherras were some more.
The late Colonel Templeton’s gentle little
widow (who was also his niece-in-law) enjoys for
life the income derived from the bulk of her dead
husband’s estate. The .departed warrior’s first
wife, having no children of her own, had always
been very fond of her nieces, the Taylor girls, and
when she died the bereaved widower clung to them
as part of his old home. After a while, one of
them became the second Mrs. Templeton.
Another is Mrs. iEneas Gunn, authoress of the
“Little Black Princess.”
The Hawthorn Lacrosse Club announces its
annual ball for September 4. Its secretary adds
that the affair is on -the official list of Fleet fes-
tivities, and that the supper is going to beat the
band, and upwards of 100 officers from Ohio and
elsewhere are going to help eat it.
Mrs. Janet Clarke still wavers ’twixt a recovery
and a continued illness. Her favorite Dr. Maud-
sley is in attendance now, as well as solemn Dr.
Stawell. Dr. M. is the sort of family physician
who prescribes a course of what amounts to
Christian Science to his patients, bidding them
make up their minds that they’re going to get
better now that he’s in attendance. The dowager’s
next rally is expected to be the corner round
which she’ll catch health again.
The State concerts which are to tame Uncle
Sam’s big nephews are to be conducted by Freddie
Beard and the weighty Geo. Peake. These days
Mr. Marshall-Hall might as well be in Europe
for all that any but his own coterie hear of him.
Dr. Bob Strong is said to be in bad health.
Anybody who bears up under Supreme Court
trials always strikes me as being a clod. A break-
down after a divorce suit is as proper a social
observance as a dinner-call. Poor Dr. Bob has
white hair and a pale, lined face these days.
A young Melbourne debutante waxes enthusi-
astic over first impressions of the London season :
The grandest of all things was the Royal Opera
[enclosed was a majo-magnificent programme of the show
printed in scarlet and gold on parchment]. The theatre
was hung and ceiling-ed and lined.with roses—roses that
began with white-pink buds, and smelled upward through
the scale of pink rose until they burst into red rose in
alt. The four tiers of boxes wore garlands of roses. The
Royal box boasted orchids—orchids at about a fortune a
whiff.
And hereabouts the writer grew excited, and
began to scatter capital letters and exclamations
wildly:—
Oh, the Women ! Oh, the Jewels ! Oh, the Singing ! Oh,
the Duchesses, Peeresses! and Lords and Dukes —
in fact, Oh the everything! Peeping out from
their bowers of roses were English aristocracy.
Mostly all were painted or enamelled, and blazing
with jewels. They all wore their crowns and
coronets, and all the men were in full dresk uniforms. A
maharajah wore a headpiece worth £50,000. The Weedons
were there with the Earl and Countess of Jersey. Now,
how am I to explain ? Well, the women wore just
diamonds and a little—very little—chiffon for bodices. The
singing was wonderful. Tetrazzini thrilled me through
and through. Imagine, she trilled on E in alt! Melba was
adorable and icily perfect. Tetrazzini thrills you and
makes vou feel. I just love and love the King. The
President of the French Republic led the Queen in.
Wouldn’t I just love to have her figure !—it’s willowy and
perfection. She wore a black “spangly” dress, a tiara
of diamonds, a big necklace of diamonds, diamond
bracelets, a huge pink and red carnation in her
bodice, and gloves only to her elbows, with all
the top arm bare. We’d think it country-cousinish.
She does not wear her dresses low like the duchesses.
Dresses are nearly all Empire and Princess Robe. It is
all Directoire style and the time of Robespierre. The
hair is just a nest of curls. Really as long as you can
make it look old-fashioned you are Just It. Ribbon is
threaded through ; bands of silver and gold and color.
People with profiles indulge in a thoroughly Grecian fiair-
dress—bands and all.
The Princess wore dead white, and was mostly hidden
behind diamonds.
It was a pageant to see the King and Co. drive away.
All the Royal' personages drove away. All the Royal car-
riages were out, with the footmen and powdered wigs.
All the dukes had their state carriages out. It was mag-
nificent. I was greatly amused seeing one duchess pass
by in the stately, old-fashioned carriage with the gold
and white liveries, for she was smoking for all she was
worth ! The old-fashioned turnout and the modern
woman inside ! The Queen looks marvellously young,
but is at least an inch thick with enamel; yet she is won-
derful. London is packed.
The 1830-style bonnets are to be worn at Ascot this
week (first week in June)—hats running wild with flowers
and feathers. Nell Gwynne’s hat is just The Thing of the
moment.
Too much motoring and golf have given the English
women a stony, fixed look. I have been out night and
day ever since I have been here—two or three in the
morning is considered early ! The Victorian Court at the
Exhibition is the worst in all the show. The Queen
stopped to look at the Queensland jewels, and she bought
something in the court. A few discolored fruits and a few
sundries form the Victorian Court. It was the first the
King went into, and there ivas nothing to see. N.S.W.’s
show is just fine. It takes four hours and a half to walk
around the Exhibition, without stopping to look at any-
thing.
From “Rosna ” in Adelaide: —
Our song maiden, Ethel Hantke, gave a farewell burst
of melody at the Town Hall on the 16th, before hastening
back to fulfil engagements in London about August. The
audience was a good one, and much of the peerage left
its hearthstone to hear the lady’s charming bird-notes.
Our chief fiddling boy, Eugene Aldermann, lent a hand,
also clever Violet Parkinson came from Melbourne by
Bent’s train to help at the piano. The singer-girl wore a
simple gown of soft white, and much gorgeous vegetation
and ribbon were handed up to her.
The latest engagement will eventually deprive Adelaide
of one of its clever elocutionary and amateur mumming
maidens. Miss Mabel Haslam will be wed, somewhere
about next March, to Mr. Home, who dwells in the red
and green isle of Ceylon. The damsel returned not many
months since from a European jaunt taken in company
with a girl chum, and the engagement is one of the un-
premeditated results of the trip. Mr. Home pronounces
his name Hume, which confusion of sounds is a sign that
he can trace his ancestry back to the dark ages when
Alfred the Great defeated George Washington on the
bloodstained field of Chickabiddy Lick. When a man
spells his name S-m-i-t-h and pronounces it Johnson it
is solid evidence that he is of high degree and comes
from the Browns of Jerusalem,, who were contempora-
neous with Pontius Pilate.
Ladv Le Hunte gave an At Home at the Residence last
week 'in honor of Mrs. Hallida.v of the Mothers’ Union,
and all the local dukes were invited to hear how to bring up
offspring the way it should spring. The ballroom was
crowded with a frilly throng. The Chief Justice Wav
dame, the Bishopess and others lent their support on the
dais. The visitor gave an interesting discourse on the
way of the wily infant, and the influence of Home, and
sundry other thrilling topics. Then a vote of thanks Was
handed to her on a plate, and the guests flocked into the
dining-room to eat and tell each other how true it all was.
But it isn’t likely one of them will remember to take the
advice thrust at them, and little Willie Will scramble along
on general principles as heretofore.
The Vice-Chancellor of Oxford ’Varsity, in a letter to ah
ex-Bishop of S.A., has been givihg a nice little unsolicited
testimonial of that brilliant Adelaide youth, Roy Lister
Robinson. He says :—“ A fellow named Roy Robinson is
one of the most remarkable all-rohhd men we have ever
had. He runs, puts the weight, is Captain of the lacrosse
team, has taken a first in geology and a Burdett-Coutts
scholarship, and is now coming out as a ’Varsity
Cricketer.” The letter was read at the St. Peter’S old
scholars’ dinher t’other night, and the association swelled
Visibly.
Some well-known voyaging South Australians, the
Misses Kay, have been writing enthusiastic descriptiohs
of their doings in Rome, where they intend to tarry awhile
and study art. They had an audience with his Holiness,
and. strict Unitarians though they be, their reams of
rhetoric about the wonderful palace, the gardens, the
quaint ceremonial, and, above all, the wonderful charm of
the Spiritual Monarch himself, would make the Yellow
Pup howl in the back-yard for the rest of its life. The
frocking rule for an audience with the Pope, it seems,
requires you to wear a black evening gown and black lace
mantilla. Boots are advisable, but not compulsory.
Adelaide mankind is busy brushing the cobwebs off its
top hat for the Governor-General’s levde at the Residence,
which will happen on the 27th, at the end of his Murray
trip. The same day will see the City of Unley fall on the
neck of our Premier, and welcome him home.
Mr. Barr Smith has given £2300 to the Trades
Hall, which will finally clear the building from debt.
This is the largest gift ever received for the hall,
thousrh sundry well-known South Australians have given
smaller sums on various occasions, among them Edwin
Smith, Richard Baker, the late J. H. Angas, Alexander
Hay, and G. 0. Hawker.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
On Tuesday next, the 28th, the ball in aid
of the Hospice for the Dying, at Darlinghurst,
-will take place in Sydney Town Hall. In order
to make the affair attractive to the many who have
given up dancing, and thereby help to fill the great
hall to the doors, Mrs. J. C. Williamson will stage-
manage a series of tableaux and living pictures,
which will be a revelation of beauty and art. A
number of the most handsome of Sydney
young women have volunteered their services
towards depicting some of the pictures in
the Sydney Art Gallery. It will be the
first entertainment of this nature ever offered to
a Sydney audience, and as it may be a long while
before the said audience gets another, it should
roll up numerously. The musical programme
will be rich and varied, and rendered by an
excellent orchestra. Tickets are on sale at
Nicholson’s, price 25.. booking 6d. extra. The
Hospice for the Dying is one of the most meritori-
ous establishments in N. S. Wales, and it is
urgently in want of funds, as the accommodation
available is, quite unequal to the demand.
Premier Bent’s latest idea is to build a
hospice (which is the knightly way of mentioning
an ordinary pub) on Mount Buffalo for the accom-
modation of tourists to that outlook. Thomas has
been considering the Westralian State hotel at
Gwalia, likewise the profits thereof, and has a
notion that an ornate building in the baronial
style of architecture, with a publican’s license,
could be built to bed and board about 100 tourists,
with pleasure to all and profit to the State, at a
cost of about £lO,OOO. Which suggests an idea.
Bent certainly ought to appoint himself landlord
of this pub. He could easily find somebody else
to go round making futile promises and boosting
up land values to no purpose.

Interstate Mail
Steamship Lines

HE traveller who
takes one of the
steamers of the
Five Companies,
and speeds away
to North Queens-
land at this season of the
year., can always be sure of a jolly
time. He steps on board either at
Sydney or Melbourne, and henceforth be-
comes a decidedly envied person. He can have a
deck cabin, fitted with every convenience, and
peculiarly suited to the needs of the northward-
bound tourist. Ample deck-space provides him
with room for exercise. Magnificent, invigorating
air gives him a keen appetite ; and he is always
ready for each of the many adjournments to the
provision department, which generally keeps him
in a state of tender expectancy after each adjourn-
ment. As an appetiser alone, the northward tour
is to be recommended.
Of course, the merit of the cuisine and of the
accommodation generally in the steamers of the
Five Companies is not the sole purpose of this
article. It was merely mentioned somewhat inci-
dentally to get the reader into a good humor.
Now, the bulk of the travellers who go to North
Queensland might be said to be genuine tourists
who want to pack into every day of their stay as
many sight-seeing excursions as their time will
allow. And, as there are hundreds of sights and
beauty spots that are worth visiting, it becomes a
matter of exceeding difficulty to advise upon a pro-
gramme that must be necessarily limited. Start-
ing from Melbourne, the steamer calls at Sydney,
Brisbane, Keppel Bay (Rockhampton), Flattop
Island, for Maekay, Bowen, Townsville and Cairns,
long enough to permit excursions into the
neighborhood. Thus, before the trip has,
say, reached finality in the inspection of
the natural wonders in and around
Cairns, the tourist has acquired a
heap of impressions of other splen-
dors on his way up. For in-
stance, the steamer remains a
couple of days at Brisbane,
which gives him an opportu-
nity of looking around the
prettily-situated city. The
electric tram service, which
covers the whole eity with a net-
work of rails, will take him to
any part of the environs, affording enjoyable rides ;
while the train will put him into touch with the
pleasant seaside towns of Sandgate, Southport,
etc., or with inland towns such as Ipswich—which
areall within short distances. Again, without mak-
ing use of the facilities provided by tram and train,
the traveller can easily spend an interesting hour or
two in the city proper—and here let it be men-
tioned that' he should not omit to visit the
Botanical Gardens, especially attractive for their
fine collection of tropical flora. Altogether, the
traveller who spends the couple of days ashore is
in no danger of succumbing to ennui. Though
the city cannot approach either Sydney or Mel-
bourne in size, it is still interesting to the stranger.
P
Bound North,- the traveller again settles down
to the regimen of seven or eight meals a day,
and during the intervals of recuperation, so to
speak, gathers up the illuminating tales of
those passengers who have journeyed to the North
before. In the saloon everybody is all expectation,
and the person who has previously made
the tour, becomes the centre of an inquiring
circle. If the person is at all talkative, he imme-
diately ceases to be the bore he was. His fellow
passengers overlook his fault in the charm of
getting their imaginations tickled by his tales.
And if he is at all observant, and has the ability
of a raconteur, he is certainly worth listening to.
He tells of the beauty of the Hinchinbrook, open-
ing up to the tourist some of the finest coast
scenery in the world. Then he descants upon
Cairns and its remarkable railway, that climbs up
the Cairns Range, and finally lands the traveller
in the immediate vicinity of the Barron Falls.
Anon, he talks of the sugarcane country; of
the timber forests; and of the immense Barrier
itself, which the steamer is fast approaching. Anon,
he discourses of the miles of shining beaches ; of the
air; of the fruits that one only sees in the shop
windows of Melbourne town ; of the acres and
acres of canefields, indeed. Thus it can be reason-
ably conceived that such a person will have a
mightily-interested audience.
And what a world is this that he talks about !
For a thousand miles an almost motionless sea that
carries the steamer on an even keel from day to
day, and, in all this thousand miles an ever-vary-
ing coast-line, in places equal in picturesque effect
to anything extant. Moreover, the travellerhas
this part of the tour punctuated, loosely speaking,
by little jaunts ashore. A day from Brisbane,
and he arrives at Sea Hill, Keppel Bay,
where a steam tender meets the vessel to
convey passengers up the Fitzrov River to
Rockhampton. The next day the vessel anchors
off Flattop Island (Mackay), and proceeds thence
through the extremely picturesque Whitsunday
Passage to Bowen, which is reached the same
evening. Here he again disembarks to collect
more material for future reminiscences. A further
day at sea and he is set down at Townsville,
where, after a survey of the town, he picks up the
“Kuranda,” to proceed to Cairns via Hinchin-
brook Channel. Hinchinbrook Channel is not
very long, but, mile for mile, it can compare in
color with anything extant. And along this
route are scattered such attractive spots as Lucinda
Point, Cardwell, and Mourilyan Harbor. At
Geraldton, a little further on, one gets a view
of the Johnstone River and of the banana and
cane plantations. Here the industrious Mongolian
may be seen in practically his native state. He
may be said to entirely control the cultivation of
the banana in the district—for on few of these
plantations hereabouts is there seen a European.
And thus being a person of some importance in
these parts, anyhow, Ah Sin has imported one or
two of his ancient implements of locomotion. He
may be noticed on the Johnstone flitting about in
a sampan—and the observant traveller will note
the contrast between Orient and Occident —
between antiquity and modernity, in fact—when
the oil-launch of the white man appears on the
scene.
(53?
Indeed, everywhere from Geraldton up to
Thursday Island, John and many of his Asiatic
neighbors make the thoughtful visitor con-
templative. In the crowd of pearl-shell
and beche-de-mer fishers at Thursday Island
in the populations of the coast towns right
down to Cooktown and Cairns, may be seen the
Malay, the Japanese and the Chinaman. They
add a certain amount of picturesqueness to the
scenery, if you look at it that way.
But, otherwise, they are
only a small part of the
North. It is the
Atherton Scrub; the
mountains around
Cairns; the exquisi-
tely-colored Barrier
Reef, and a million
other beau t i f u
things that consti-
tute the attraction
of the magic North.


GOOD”
DIGESTION
MAKES GOOD HEALTH.
The surest way to have good health is to keep your digestion healthy
and active. All the nourishment of your body must pass through your
stomach, and if "that is not in a fit state to digest it properly, it stands
to reason that your health must suffer. Undigested food will not yield
its nourishment. On the contrary it sours in your stomach and clogs
your system with impurities. Mother Seigel’s Syrup puts your stomach
right, and keeps it right. Here is an instance. Mr. Harry Dunn, 187,
Goulburn Street, Sydney, writing on March 4th, 1908, says:—“l suffered
from violent pains in my stomach, frequent sick headaches, and bilious
attacks every few days. I took all sorts of medicines without the least
benefit until at last I was persuaded to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup. The
result was complete cure, and I have been well ever since.”
MOTHER
SEIGELS
SYRUP

Business, Robbery, etc.
Some of the mining methods on the West Coast
of Tasmania are fearsome. At one of the two leading-
mines in one district the general manager is surrounded
by his sons and daughters, their wives and husbands and
sons’ sons.. The scientific knowledge of the bunch would
wear no brain out. Every little while the local paper
mentions an “ unfortunate accident.” Lately the com-
bined wisdom of the staff designed a new shaft which was
to be sunk to a great depth. (Already on this property
there are about a dozen shafts. Each starts to go down to
500 ft. straight away, but the same old 50ft. sees a level
opened out.) Unfortunately they omitted toput in a single
diagonal stay, so now the poppet legs sport guy ropes to
prevent them being pulled over bodily. Until recently the
ore from the main body reached the mill via a tram
w'hich delivered the ore at the foot of an old, abandoned
set of poppet legs (with the usual guy ropes), when it was
tipped on flat sheets and shovelled up into a skip, which
delivered it from the surface to above the level of the
bins, about 30ft. in height. It was then run along a
trestlework rotten with age, which delivered it into a bin
abov , e the mill. The truck went through one day. The
trucker averted another unfortunate accident by
seizing a beam, and remained suspended in mid-air tiil
rescued. After that it was decided that the ore should
be delivered to the mill by means of an inclined tramway
from the ground level to above the height of the bins. A
start was made, and uprights were erected (still no dia-
gonals); the cross-bearers laid on top ; and it was well in
progress of erection when, unfortunately, it blew down !
However, the job was finished and the mine delivered
the bj ns< (There were no tie bolts, and the structure just
°P ened * * *
“ Val Strange” : Re “Hub’s” par. (B. 28/5/’08).
I also am a White Australian, and I sympathise with Mrs.
Hub in her knock-out experience whilst trying to
encourage the White Australian ideal. If the lady has
not a i rea( jy burst with grief, I would ask her to postpone
the explosion until I console her with a tale of the woe
that happened to me. I was sojourning in a Northern
River Ma State town. A white wksherlady of the district
had the honor of laving my linen ; and, although her
seell,ed
that she appeared pretty stiff herself, and had
no “ hub ” to help her with a goodly mob of youngsters.
Q ne sa d day a crippled wreck of a man called and asked
me jf j WO uld send my washing to his missus, who was
the sole support of himself and a houseful. I said I was
sorrj', but Mrs. Bluebags, likewise an indigent citizen,
did my washing. “ She does it?” he snarled. “ Yer
think she does it, like a lot more of yous ; but the loafin’
old takedown sends it all to the blightin’ Japs, and when
it conies back, she makes out her own bills and flops a
penny or so on’every article, and then sends ’em round to
yous by one of her pore, little, bare-footed nippers. She’s
doin’ orl right, she is.” And behold, on investigation, I
found that it was so.

“Victae”: Rumor came and whispered in the
earg Qf tWQ M L detec tives recently, whereupon quoth
No. . « Arise, j e t; us g 0 hence. In yon bleak land of
prohibition is a knave who selleth spirits at a guinea a
bottle, calling it cold tea, forsooth. Let us capture
him.” So they travelled with haste until they came unto
the place, and they encamped that night in the company of
th< ; evildoer. When it was light they awoke, and one cried
out, as if in exceeding bitter pain, calling for brandy. Then
chanced along their host. “ Brandy? ” quoth he. “ Yes,
nrlord.” He brought the potion, and the stricken man
drank copiously. “ Good sir, ,ve have saved my life,” said
he, smacking his lips. “ What’s the damage ? ” “I am but
an honest, poor man,” responded his host loftily, yet
firmly. “ But the law may not be broken. Put back
your gold, sirrah. I sell no brandy here.” Then one
winked. “ Mayhap,” he said carelessly. “But we go upon
f long journey, and rumor tells of a noble drink named
‘cold tea.’ I prithee give us some. ‘Ay that will I and
merrily, answered the other. “How much-? quoth No. 1,
a s he looked lovingly at the bottle. “ Two guineas, good
master. Extra special brew of cold tea on this morning.”
And he, too, winked. Then, with much joy in their
hearts, the two minions departed. When they came
unto one invested with much authority they brandished
the bottle, exclaiming loudly—“ Look ye ! And make ye
oub a warrant for the arrest of a knave who
sold ns fchls calling it cold tea, and charging us
extortionately therefor. And with haste and much re-
joicing the script was made ready. “ Uncork,” said the
one in authority. “The day is hot. Let us sample this
beverage of the'wrong-do.er.” And the two smiled largely,
and proffered the drink to him. The one in authority
took a goodly mouthful. . . . And away in the King
Country one sinner who had emptied the teapot chuckled
the chuckle of the ungodly as he beheld two sovereigns
that nestled softly in his palm.
COASTING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Tis no fool’s game on the bridge to-night, for the
Southerly blows like H—l,
And the drenching sprays fly left and right—What’s up
with that fo’c’s’le bell ?
A mere hour seems like a full-kept watch. Good Lord !
there’s a sea to ship !
What’s that!. ... Burst open the for’ard hatch ?. .. Well,
the skipper ivould let her rip !
Now rouse the watch and batten her down (I’m as cold
as a perished shag).
I wish I could settle in Sydney town with the kids and
my old girl Mag.
It’s cheerless work is this coasting life, and I’m full to
the neck to-night,
For I see the smiles of my kids and wife in the loom of
the South Head light 1
The light looms up with a lazy gleam ; but it’s millions
of miles away.
And I keep my watch in a kind of dream, half blind with
the stinging spray !
Millions of miles—so it seems to me as I watch this darned
old tub
Rise up and descend remorselessly with a crash on the
world’s great hub !
She shakes and shivers, and strains and groans, and the
racing screw flies round
Till the souls in the haunts of Davy Jones burst out into
mirth profound.
Two knots she’s done by the patent log—and the waves
they increase in height,
And the rain comes thick as a Melbourne fog, and shuts
in the South Head light 1
The black squad stops in its lair below, and it works with
a stubborn will,
And the “second” watches the heave and throw as he
throttles the “coffee-mill.”
The chief walks round with his face askew and prates of
the dearth of coal,
And his curses rise in a vapor blue from the depths of his
inmost soul.
The cook looks out with a frenzied eye, and acts like a roan
gone daft
As his pots and pans go sailing by en route for the well-
deck aft.
And I—l think as my watch goes on of home that is clean
and bright,
And a wife and kids whom I dote upon, not far from the
South Head light.
We moor to the wharf next day at eight, and then when
my graft is o’er
I rush away, with a heart elate, to the ones whom I love
on shore.
I give no thought to the previous day, nor care for to-
morrow’s blow,-
While the kiddies talk in their childish way to their dad,
whom they scarcely know.
An hour at home and it’s time to part—ah! the time it
is none too slow ;
So I kiss them all with a heavy heart, then back to my
ship I go.
We lose the lights of old Sydney town and steam through
the moonless night,
And the thoughts in my heart go sinking down, and dip
with the South Head light.
N.S.W.
ALBATROSS.


QUALITY in a piano depends
upon many things, of which the first
and most important are the Scale and the
Sounding-board.
The SCALE of a piano is the scientific
plan of its sound-producing parts, the length,
thickness, and radiation of the strings, and
the striking-line of the hammers. The calcu-
lation of the scale is by far the most difficult
part of piano-manufacture, and can only be
accomplished after many years’ experience.
Most of the makers of foreign pianos simply
buy their frames from foundry . people
who are usually quite ignorant of the
mathematical requirements, and devoid of all
scientific knowledge. One piano “ manu-
facturer, in writing a “ Handbook of Piano-
Manufacture,” recommends that a frame
should be taken out of some well-known
piano, and the casting be made from that,
thus saving the trouble of calculating a scale!
But this cannot be done successfully, for
there is an exact allowance to be made for
shrinkage in casting. Ihe only correct and
scientific way is to first calculate the scale,
making allowance for two shrinkages, then
cast a pattern, and from that cast the frame
to be used. This is the method followed in
the production of the BEALE AUSTRA-
LIAN PIANOS.
The SOUNDING-BOARD is the
second important feature. No ordinary care
is required in its manufacture ; the highest
skill and perfect knowledge only will assure
satisfactory results. Ihe timber must be
carefully selected, of the best quality, and the
grain must be graded from fine in the treble
to coarse in the bass. The strips of timber,
never more than five inches in width, must
also be tapered m thickness from treble to
bass. Ihe amount of camber, or con-
vexity (like a violin belly), of the complete
sounding-board must be arrived at to
a hair s-breadth, so as to assure the
best quality of sound when the strings are
vibrated.
In the Beale Australian Pianos all these
details are carried out with the utmost care
by specialists of many years’ training, in their
turn guided by management whose know-
ledge of the whole result to be obtained—
and the method of obtaining it—is unsur-
passed in any part of the world. The
materials are the best that money can buy.
Our great aim is always “ How much better
;
can we make it ?” And to this end we
spare no pains, giving our every thought to
the perfecting of an Australian product that
is already far ahead of its old-world com-
petitors. That this is so you can verify for
yourself by comparison. Call and inspect
our pianos, and then examine the goods of
our competitors. Our faith in our product
is shown by the fact that we guarantee every
Beale Australian Piano for Twenty-Five
Years, whereas no importer will guarantee his
goods for more than Twelve.

AN AUSTRALIAN INDUSTRY.
For the past five years Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills have been manufactured in Australia, and
we believe they are the only purgative pills on the
market that are made in this country by the pro-
prietors. Most other pills are made by manu-
facturing chemists and sold in bulk to the specula-
tive pill companies to be boxed and labelled. We
take no chance of this kind with Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills, and we know that the formula
does not vary one-hundredth part of a grain. We
also know that the ingredients are up to the
standard, both in strength and quality. The
crude material is imported direct, being ground by
our own machines in our American laboratory,
where the greatest care is observed in the selection
of the roots and gums from which this crude
material is made. We have both in Sydney and
Wellington (New Zealand) up-to-date plants for
the manufacture of these pills, and should any of
our friends be interested we will be only too
pleased to show them the process of manufacture.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Evidently the Anglo-Welsh team has struck
form, evolved a system, or done something very untoward
when the Taranaki Tigers could make no impression
against it. Taranaki won by a goal to nil, hut that goal
should not have been allowed, and will even yet probably
be wiped out by the Union. The ball had gone
out close to the English line, and a Taranaki player was
lying prone, badly damaged on the Head. The English
forwards crowded to his assistance, expecting the whistle
.to blow. But it didn’t, and to the Maoriland foot-
baller in his baresark fury, a few deaders don’t matter.
They lined up, and as it was Taranaki’s throw in, the ball
was placed in the arms of Dives, who hopped across, and
the goal was kicked while the English forwards were still
attending to the injured Maorilander. The Taranaki cow-
spankers are mighty:’bailers, and the fact that the in-
vaders stood up to them in the manner they did will go
far to renew interest in the tour.
Most of the English cracks are suffering re-
coveries, and the rough play of the local men has brought
down a storm of abuse and expostulation from the news-
paper correspondents. The Maoriland footballer is as
unscrupulous as he is brii?sant, and his doctrine is “ win
anyhow,” or “ win by fair play if possible, but win all the
time.” And unless that sort of thing is stamped out,
Rugby, as it is now played in the Isles, is doomed. That
hardened ’bailer who writes under the name of “ Off-side
Mac ” is not squeamish, and knows how to take hard
bangs as well as give them ; but even he has pointed an
accusing quill ac his countrymen, and declared the
last match in Wellington was too rough. And it
was, as newspaper correspondents declare that
there was deliberate hacking by the exasperated
Maofilanders. This sort of thing will be excellent
when Maoriland is playing Asiatic invaders for keeps, but
it is.quite out of place in friendly football. The record
of Wallace’s injuries, recently mentioned in this paper,
; sounds like the record of a veteran of about 17 campaigns,
or the injuries of a man who had spent 40 years in frontier
fighting. If the Maoriland Union can’t control the game
better than it is doing it should give up the ghost, abolish
the clubs, and recommend the players to take to lawn
tennis, where a net separates the rivals. If necessary,
the net can be replaced by an iron fence with spiked tops
or a wall crowned with broken glass.
* « * *
The Union Rugby match between Queensland
and N. S. Wales drew a crowd of over 20,000 people to
Sydney Cricket Ground on Saturday last. The ground
was sodden and slippery, and the local backs were indivi-
dualistic and powerless. But the forwards were as nippy
as could be desired, and practically won the game. The
Queensland team was decidedly fast, so much so that the
forwards repeatedly got in among the Cornstalk backs
and played Aged Henry with the blue jerseys. It was the
superiority of the local men in dribbling rushes, and the
lack of combination among the Queensland back division,
that won the match for N. S. Wales. The game vvas a
dreary struggle in which men clawed the air to hold
themselves up. N.S.W. did all the scoring during the
first-half, and midway had put up a record of 14 to
nil. After the resumption, Queensland converted two
tries, and played' much better right through. Then
N.S.W. added another try, and made the score 17 to 10.
There it stuck till the whistle blew.
* * * *
A much finer game to watch was the struggle
between the Rugby League’s Australian team and the
Maoris. The latter, as expected, made it a wild and
whirling business during the first half, and did things on
a considerable scale. They played with their well-known
abandon and recklessness, Pouwhiuwhiu soaring clean
over the head of an Australian back who tackled him
while he was in full flight for the goal. Within the first
five minutes the Maoris had swept the struggling Austra-
lians right back, and broken through them, and jumped
over them, and scored a try, and then kicked a field
goal from half way. With the score five to ni
Messenger commenced to kick down the field, but
he had no .hope. The ball came back like lightning, and
the Maoris came with it, laying out and running over the
Australian full back as they did so. But for Hedley’s fall
the blacks would have gone clean over the line. After
the resumption the blue team got to the half-way line and
was driven back. Another passing charge was made, and
brought down. Then Messenger led his whole back
division down the field, sweeping on right to the line,
where the Maoris, with a whoop, flung the whole crowd
right out of bounds. In the struggle that ensued on the
line the Maoris again got the ball and carried it to the Aus-
tralian line. There Hedley was again laid out, and over his
prostrate form the Maoris converted the try, making their
score 10 to nil. Hedley retired, and when his substitute
arrived the Australians flung the whole team on to the
Mxoris, and from a free kick Messenger sent the ball
across the face of the goal. The Walers dashed in and
scored, and, of course, Messenger converted. The Maoris
were beginning to fizzle out, and the whites came again.
Messenger showed the Maoris one of his beautiful star
runs; he reached the line and fell into the arms of the
Maori full back just as he passed to O’Malley,
who dived across. Messenger again converted,
and the scores were 10 all just as half-time
went. After resumption Messenger commenced to
bombard the Maori goal, and, following up one of his own
shots, got across and converted. Then the Maoris
reached the Australian line, and a series of scrums
ended in Australia forcing. Play swept away to the
Maori line, where the Black team forced twice. Time was
running out, and the Maoris strained every nerve to
equalise things, but they were a wasted lot of browns by
this time, and the old energy was not in them. The game
ended in a win for Australia by 20 to 10, after one of the
fastest and most exciting struggles ever seen in Sydney.
The brown men fulfilled sundry Bulletin prophecies to
the letter. They raged furiously in the first half, and
then died away. The Maori is a maddened seidlitz-
powder. First‘he fizzes, and then he goes flat. Being
improvident, like all his kind, he never economises his
fizzing power, but lets it all off in a single act.
As to Vic. League football, as Carlton has but
one game to the good over Essendon, and a single
additional defeat may mean the loss of the “right of
challenge,” a huge crowd trammed and walked out to the
ground beyond the cemetery on Saturday to see if Col-
lingwood could equal Essendon’s feat of the week before.
The magpies started well, and, playing methodically
along the windward side of the paddock and at a rare
pace held their own till nearly half-time, when a chance
kick from the scrimmage gave the blues a three-pomt
lead. So far, the play had been open, clean, and very
swift ; but, on resuming operations, the Woodsmen
seemed to be victims to over-anxiety, and time after time
mulled chances that would have kept Carlton back at its
most dangerous time—the third quarter. The systematic
game was played as usual, but its effect was nullified
by a disheartening inability to hold the ball when passed,
or to pick it up cleanly and send it on. So they added
nary a point that quarter, while Carlton put on
30 After that the episode became a mere rout, the Old
Blues finallv winning by 77 points to 37. ’Wood needs
a man with'a powerful boot to clear its goal, and a lot of
practice in handling the ball. A gorgeous crowd trans-
pired at South Melbourne, in the expectation of seeing
Skinner’s Hirelings give Melbourne a “doing,’ and thus
wipe out the unaccountable defeat inflicted earlier in the
season. There was no doubt about South’s superiority,
in the barrackers’ minds ; and it was on its own ground
and couldn’t go wrong. So the crowd yelled cheer-
fully, and urged deeds of bloodshed. But the first quarter
saw the Red Legs with three goals, South 2 goals 3 behinds.
At half-time the tally was Melbourne 6 goals 2 behinds.
South 3 goals 5 behinds, and the barrackers flocked to the
booth and imbibed deeply. Lemon-time saw the pets
still nine points to the bad —and Melbourne had the wind for
the finish. The supporters “ blime-d ” freely and argued
lengthily as to how things could be thus, and why, and
what for. When the bell rang, leaving Melbourne victors
by 11 goals 8 behinds to South’s tally of 5 goals 10 behinds,
there was no room for any excuse about luck or rotten um-
piring. South had played its darnedest and was beaten,
and badly at that. Richmond was at home to Essendon
and by making the most of its chances, kept the lead till
the last quarter—that quarter that mostly belongs to the
well-trained, seasoned team. Then the “same old,”
knowing that even one defeat meant a lot to them, bogged
in, and drove the ball down the ground even as Jehu
drove his mud cart, scoring 4 goals 6 behinds, while Rich-
mond added one goal—scores 68 to 45. St. Kilda was
burning to avenge its previous defeat by ’Varsity. The
cheek of these newly promoted juniors, in daring to
“out” an old-established club like Saints merited condign
punishment, and an example was to be made. So the godly
men wired in and steadily piled up points till at lemon-
time they led by 43 to 29. Just then ’Varsity’s captain
was badly damaged in the ruck and was carried off
the field to be nursed, and it looked any odds on the
Saints winning. But the Students set their teeth, and in
a whirling, clashing 20 minutes put oh 27 points, while
the paralysed Sinners added none. It was a revelation,
and if the. Students could but bog in that way always
even the mighty Carlton would have to play second
fiddle. Geelong encountered ’Roy, and the usual disaster
occurred —by 56 points to ’Roy’s 74. Geelong has now a
record of 12 successive defeats, and this paper opines that
something ought to be donel There is such a thing as
being too monotonous.
* * * «
The most pleasing feature among Association
Clubs is the continued uprising of Port Melbourne.
After seven notable wallopings that club drew its
game against the top-notch ’Scray, which happening
seems to have persuaded it that it could, if it would,
beat all-comers. Anyhow, it has won every game since
then, ’Cote being Saturday’s victim by 95 to 19 ;
wherefore this writer desires to pass Port the glad hand as
a repentant sinner or a reformed rake or a riser on
stepping stones of its dead self or something, and to
assure it that the door is not yet shut, and that if it will
joyously and enthusiastically jump on its opponents the
next four Saturdays, it may yet arrive at the final. The
Association premiers—Williamstown —commence to be an
obstruction to the scheme of things by reason of their
habit of getting thrashed unaccountably and unex-
pectedly. This habit upsets all scientific calculations.
As a consequence of its Saturday’s defeat by P’ran —32 to
59 —’Town is now 2£ games, or 10 competition points, the
worse of ’Scray, and only two games ahead of Brunswick.
Essendon, having regained the services of the redoubtable
Mick Madden, plucked up courage to fracture its run of
defeats, and put a head on Preston by 88 points to 24.
’Scray collided with Brunswick, and after a period of
banging and bumping, stressed its superiority by a score
of 58 to 39. Brighton encountered North, and permitted
it to trouble the scorer to the extent of 13 points, itself
scoring 63. North is hereby recommended to get a band,
as Port did. It might change the luck, and it couldn’t
make things any they are.
Seems probable that professional toeballers will
be among Maoriland’s exports this year. Several members
of the recently-returned-from-England pro. team have
been offered tempting salaries to go back and play for
Northern Union clubs. One of the forwards will captain
the Wigan Club, a very famous N.U. organisation. The
agreement is that 'be is to be supplied with a steady
billet at £2 12s. per week, while for his services on the
field he is to be rewarded to the tune of £4OO per annum,
with 10s. added for every win and ss. for every loss. The
young man who can scientifically boot an inflated bladder
about on a grass plot is in great demand in Fogland.
* * * *
West Adelaide (S. A.) is another example of the
wonderful recovery business. From being last for years
on the premiership list (it didn’t win a game in 1906) the
team suddenly heaved uo and won three out of four.
Critics said it 'was a mere flash in the pan—a sort of dying
kick. But it wasn’t. This season the club has carried
all before it, mowing down even the redoubtable Nor-
wood —until then the champion club of Australia —in
the wild rush for the premiership.
* * * *
The Anglo-Welsh team struck trouble once
more when it bumped Auckland, going down by nothing
to 11 points. Still, considering that the team was de-
prived of the services of its best six men, who are still
recovering from the previous matches, the showing can
be considered good. At the start of the game the visitors
were somewhat superior, and kept Auckland on the
defensive, but by the end of the spell, which closed with
three to nil, theAucklandsrs had got their second wind,
and were beginning to press the British very hard. In
the second spell the Auckland men crossed the line twice,
while the British had never a hope. The third Test will
take place in the Queen City next Saturday. Meanwhile
the injured men have been sent to Rotorua, in the hope
that the bracing atmosphere and hot water will speed
the recovery. The casualty list among the British is an
eloquent commentary on the quality of Maoriland Rugby.
* » » *
The Queensland League Rugby team made a
somewhat better showing against N.S.W. on Saturday.
The explanation was that the League cracks were to meet
the Maoris, and it was a second-rate team that downed
the Queenslanders by 12 points to three.
The race for the Australian sculling champion-
ship duly eventuated on Saturday on the Hunter River,
over the Raymond Terrace course. The honor was
decorated with a purse of £lOO a-side, and the coin and
laurels were a gift to big Benjamin Thoroughgood. By
one means and another Fogwell’s barrackers had blown
up around him a pretty bubble reputation, but before
three-quarters of a mile had been traversed Thorough-
good bad driven the bow of his boat right through the
bubble, and Fogwell began to resolve into mere
slush with great rapidity. From the start Thorough-
good made the pace, and Fogwell kept sprinting
to overtake him, onlv to be left again imme-
diately. At the half-mile Fogwell, barring a miracle,
was an obviously beaten man. Shortly afterwards the
boats, which had both been in neutral water for some
time, came so close together that the sculls clashed, and
both men raised their hands for a foul. Then Thorough-
good swung out and shot away, leaving Fogwell as
though standing still. At three-quarters of a mile things
resolved themselves into a procession of two, Thorough-
good pulling easily and Fogwell making frantic
efforts to swarm over the intervening water.
But although he did lessen the distance some-
what he had never a hope, and Thoroughgood
paddled home an easy winner by a distance that was
variously estimated at from six to 10 lengths. Seldom
was an aspirant for championship honors more easily
wiped out. The debacle was a severe set-back for the
Fogwell party, which had been cheerfully laying six to
four on its man almost to the start of the race. The
time was 18min. 20 3-ssec. It was fast, but the record
is somewhat discounted by the fact that the scullers
pulled with the tide.
r * * * *
Some Fogwell supporters made a considerable
stir over the clashing of the sculls, but it was as bad for
Thoroughgood as it was for Fogwell. The men were
equally'’to blame, as the boats were both in neutral
fluid. Thoroughgood seems to have been thrown out
of his course more than the other man, as he drew out
and rowed right round Fogwell till he passed him. As
they both appealed, the race should probably have been
stopped and started afresh,' in which case Fogwell would
probably have got a worse licking than he did, and the
one he got was bad enough in all conscience. Still, the
foul may serve as an excuse to get Fogwell another race,
on the ground that the impact threw him out of his
stride, or off his mental balance, or dislocated his driving
crank, and things like that. But unless he can improve
vastly in the interval he is strongly advised not to put
any of his own cash into the speculation. He is almost
as big a disappointment as Charles Towns.
The Grand Prix Motor Race (Dieppe, France)
provided something of aboil-over this year. Last year
the brilliant driver, Felice Nazarro, on a Fiat, took the
championship to Italy, and in consequence Italian cycle
exports went up by leaps and bounds. With a view to
recapturing the lost ribbon, France this year entered 24
cars the Italians only six, the Germans nine, the English
six and the Yankees one. France was popular favorite,
and a great tussle was expected between the French and
Italians. But none of the favorites came up to expecta-
tions and Lautenschlager drove the German Mercedes
round the distance of 478 miles in 6hr. 55min. 43sec., and
beat all others rather easily. This was over eight
minutes slower than last year’s time, when Felice
Nazarro won. Lautenschlager wa9 quite an unknown
man and no one dreamed that he had a championship
about him. The race was marred by some bad smashes.
The Panliard car left the track owing to the bursting of a
tvre and killed driver Oissac and his mechanician ; and
Harrison, one of the English drivers, was taken to hos-
pital as the result of his car overturning into a ditch.
The winning Mercedes was a four-cylinder 120-h.p. chain-
driven ear, with four speeds and Simms-Bosh high ten-
sion ignition. This is Germany’s first win since Jenatzy
did the deed in 1903. Oissac was one of the oldest drivers,
and was admittedly the cleverest and most successful
at the business in Europe. Altogether, motor racing
seems to be about as deadly as steeplechasing. Lauten-
schlager’s performance is enhanced by the fact that in
the draw for places he drew the 85th position, and,
therefore, when making speed, had to thread his way
through and past 34 other cars on a road that was a dense
dust-cloud. Under the conditions his 68 miles an hour is
considered a better performance than Nazarro s 70J the
previous year. w
Owing to recent rains in the interior of Cen-
tralia and the use of the Stepney spare rims on the
driving wheels, South Australian transcontinental motor-
ists, Dutton and Aunger, are making excellent progress.
Alice Springs has been left behind, and if the same speed
is maintained right through, Port Darwin will be'struck
at the end of the fifth week out.

The detailed reports of that big match for £5OO
between Nazarro on a Fiat and Newton on a Napier car, at
Brooklands, last month, when the Napier broke down, show
that Reuter’s cabled account was a rank injustice to the
Emdish car. At the start Newton jumped away, and at
three miles was leading by a quarter of a mile and tra-
velling at the rate of 111 J miles an hour. At this juncture
a hot bearing seized and drove the connecting lod right
through the crank chamber, compelling Newton to haul
off Nazarro flew on, and averaged 94J miles an hour for
the 27| miles. In the third lap he shimmered over 2f
miles at the rate of 120 miles an hour. The Napier car
lost the 90-h.p. event, but in the two other matches for
£5OO against the Metallurgique, the English representa-
tive wiped the floor with the foreigners.
Japan has been bitten by the cycling bug : Last
year 34,500 machines, worth £130,000, and £95,200 worth
of parts were imported ,to the land of wistaria and
chrysanthemums. #
Ellegaard, the Danish cycling wonder, is said to
be riding better than ever, although he is at an age, both
in years and in length of cycle riding, when a man usually
retires from the track. He is now the father of his
circuit. _ _
* * * *
Apparently the cycle is about to enter on a new
phase The “ pedal-glider ” has already been proved a
success, and will soon be on the market. The pedal-
glider is a foot-driven aeroplane wherein the rider sits in
a bicycle saddle. He drives the propeller with the ordi-
nary bicycle driving wheel and gear. The cycle is a bit
of a curse now, but it will be worse when the blessed
things take to sailing through the air.

With the exception of A. T. Clark, all the com-
petitors for the Vic. amateur billiard championship (now
rao-ino- at Alcock’s room in Melb.) have figured in previous
contests. At time of writing, ex-champion Arthur Tricks
looks most likely to live through the tournament, in
which case he will have to try conclusions with Jacoby,
who beat him in the final last year. Tricks has made the
break thus far, and although he has shown nothing
better than his old form, and is not quite in the same
class as Belfield, the Sydney amateur, he can be trusted
to put up a plucky fight for his State if he wins the local
blue riband once again.

There will be a mighty gathering of men who
love horseflesh at Christchurch (M.L.) in August, for on
the 12th of that month the horses of the late G. G. Stead
will pass under the hammer, and the break-up of the
Round Table was nothing compared to the scatter of the
finest stable in Australasia.

J. J. Miller’s sporting pamphlet, just to hand,
is the usual shilling’s worth of information on sporting
matters in general, and the spring horse handicaps in
particular. The performances of all the entrants for the
Metropolitan, and Caulfield and Melbourne Cups are
alphabetically set forth, and the lists of race winners and
athletic records are brought up to date with sundry latest
improvements in the arrangement thereof.

R. G. Casey, chairman of the V.R.C., toasting a
departing Governor the other day, hoped that his Ex.
would use his influence to have King Edward send a horse
to Victoria to win the Melbourne Cup. We should like
to see his Majesty represented here by a horse or two,
said Mr. Casey. Why not? Hasn’t Majesty been re-
presented here by an ass or two in our time ?
* * * *
Champion bruiser Tommy Burns, whose real
name is Noah Brusso, is evidently a man of resource.
Not only has he made more money at the game than any
other bruiser who went before him, including even the
marvellous Philadelphian, Jack O’Brien, but, like
that meteoric pugilist, he has produced a book.
It is a well-written volume, in which Burns dis-
plays neither swelled head nor bogus modesty. He is,
if he wrote his own book, a well-balanced individual of
pleasing personality. He knows something about fighting,
havinn- fought his way to the world’s championship, and he
has a yarn"to tell. He tells it in an easy manner, and in
simple, direct language, driving his arguments home with
sentences of Maoaula.v-like brevity and force. He calls
his book “ Scientific Boxing and Self Defence, but the de-
fence he teaches is of the Napoleonic type, which con-
sists of being the attacking party all the time, while
keeping the other fellow under the delusion that he is
the aggressor. And anyone who has had a few rough and
tumbles of his own will bear testimony to the wisdom
of that gospel. “ Glove fighting,” he remaiks,
“ has passed out of its early stages of brute
force and indomitable endurance into the era of brain
work and science. Brains are of more importance than
fists to-day.” “ It is the quick, short jab on the proper
spot which is felt, even though it isn’t often seen.” “ Trick
ball-punching is the bane of the boxer... especially if you
are young at the game ” He drives home the neces-
sity of speed—rapidity of hitting, rapidity of evasion, the
delivering of short, quick blows that anticipate the
longer and heavier ones of an opponent of the
older school. He discourses on the short, quick
tap on the shoulder that destroys the aim and para-
lyses the force of the other fellow’s swing. But it is
when writing of Squires that Burns becomes of most
interest to Australians. Before Squires left Australia we
knew he was a rusher of the worst sort who had been
demoralised by meeting men who allowed themselves to
be rushed, and we “ feared for him,” as the Scotch say,
when he met a man who wouldn’t be rushed. It is in his
instructions “ How to Meet a Rusher” that Squires floats
to the top as a shocking example, and Burns explains
that although rushers may look awkward, they are
usually, barring accidents, dead easy. Here is Burns
account of the historic debacle, . , .
“ A striking instance of this (easy disposal of rushers)
may be quoted.of my meeting with the Australian cham-
pion, Bill Squires.
“ We had only just faced each other, when he came at
me like a bull at a gate. In a bound he was on me, but
my right got there first on the angle of his jaw.
“ Down he dropped as though he had been poleaxed,
and, for just a few seconds, I fancied that the whole busi-
ness was over. ,
“ But in four seconds he was up again, had leapt clear
across the ring, and had given me a beauty right in the
mouth. It was one of the worst and hardest blows 1
ever received, and for a second or so I was dazed and
hardly knew where I was.
“ But I kept my wits about me, clinched and leant up
against him, while I recovered my senses. I wasn t far
from being ‘ out,’ but had no anxiety that he should get
acquainted with the fact. So I just cuddled up to him
and asked him w r hat he was about. I said I wanted to
know whether he couldn’t fight or wouldn’t?
“The bluff came off, and I’m sure that Squires
didn’t even guess that he had hit me, while all the
reporters stated that I never received a blow at all right
through the affair. . ,
“He struggled to push me away, and I just leant
against him and let him shove, for my senses were fast
coming back to me.
“ We broke in the centre of the ring, and, as he came
on at me, just as before, I popped my right over again
and dropped him for the second time. He was up again
in a second, and feeling rather groggy, evidently, but as
full of fight as ever. So I just put in as many as I could
and as fast as I could make them follow each other. I
didn’t want to give him an opportunity to recover and
give me another like the one I had already received. He
dropped his arms. Over came another right to his jaw,
and he was down and out in 2min. Bsec., one of the
shortest, if not the shortest, championship battle on
record.”
Judging by the accounts just to hand of the
scrap between Burns and Squires at Paris, Bill has suc-
ceeded in getting out of his rushing habit. Burns
book contained the first hint that the Australian
once had victory within his grasp at Colma, and the in-
formation knocks out the tale that the Australian had
lost his punch. He had not mislaid the wallop—the
trouble was he couldn’t get it home. Burns admits that
he was nearly outed by the solitary blow Squires did get
in. At Paris Squires managed to more than hold bis own
till his strength gave out, which bears out what Burns
says in his book about making his opponent do the fight-
ing. Squires exhausted himself making play, and the
fact that he lasted as long as he did suggests that he
did not make play bv rushing as aforetime. Letters re-
ceived by the Mclntosh tell that Squires had improved
out of all knowledge, and never offered a-chance till his
strength gave out. If Squires continues to advance at
the same rate, it is possible that Tommy Burns will bury
his championship in an Australian grave. But whether
he does or not, his book proves him to be a man of parts
—or else his literary' “ ghost ” is.
* *. * *
The want of a stage-manager and announcer
accounted for an unexpected flatness on the opening
night (which was Saturday last) of the Melbourne amateur
boxing and wrestling competitions at the Masonic Hall.
As a rule the stoushing bouts provided livelier sport than
the wrastles on the mat, but in the matter of humorous
display the young pugs werfe outdone by the one pair of
singlestickers. The thwack of a wooden implement on
the protected person of one who looks like Ned Kelly is
an essentially droll incident, and this department of
athletics deserves more encouragement than it gets. The
competitions at the Masonic will be concluded next Mon-
day night—the fifth of the series.
*** * . .
The perpetual drawing of wrestling matches
had already been very discouraging to the “ gate,” and
the third match between Peter Ban non and Buttan
Singh, which lately transpired at Melb. Oyclorama,
ended in a way that is liable to make future “ gates ”
worth 2s. 6d. or thereabouts. For the first half-hour the
display was vigorous and scientific; then, in 39min.
35sec., Bannon got a leg hold and a half-Nelson, and
steadily forced the brown ancient down. The match had
been declared to be “to a finish,” so, on resuming, one
naturally expected Bannon to try for a second fall and
settle the argument; but, instead, he calmly 7 sat or
spread himself around, and let Buttan do all the work.
Either Peter was playing for another draw, or he felt con-
fident of his ability to throw the Indian once more. Any-
how, after a quarter of an hour’s twisting, Buttan got a
waist-hold and arm-lock that speedily brought Bannon’s
shoulders to the mat. Time, 18min. 47sec. Once more
the two came on, and, after a brief spell on top, Bannon
again sought the floor. He was earnestly adjured to
get to work,” but seemed satisfied with wriggling and
spinning out of holds. He seemed to be showing the
crowd how much he is a master of the game ; then, sud-
denly, after 21 minutes had passed, Buttan slipped his leg
round the other’s, spun him over, and held him. There
were a few hand-claps, but before either could reach the
dressing-room loud hoots broke from all round the house.
The situation was emphasised by the fact that, after
Bannon had secured the first fall , 3 to 1 was laid on
Buttan. Suspicions of this size will probably kill pro-
fessional wrestling in Melbourne for a while.
* *
In England the hog in the juggernaut begins to
strike a new brand of trouble. For instance, “Lord Vernon,
of Poynton Towers, Cheshire, was at Leicester on Saturday
fined' £2O and costs, and his license was suspended for 12
months, for furiously driving a motor car through the
village of Oadby.” My lord had got into the habit of
being convicted, so the magistrates did the next best
thing to locking him up—ordered him to abstain from
handling his weapon for 12 months. Just how it will
workout isn’t too certain ; but, on the face of things, it
looks like a hint that Australia might do worse than take.
There would be particular joy in handing out to a fat
importer or a Stinking-Fish Australian a brand of punish-
ment made in “ the dear old country.”

the penalties of medical, electrical or surgical treatments when coping with
any disease of the PAR, which is the most delicately and wonderfully
constructed organ of the entire human anatomy.
SCIENTIFIC EXPERT’S OPINION.

medical, electrical, or even surgical system of coping with ear diseases is a scientific

For the Heaveb-knows-how-manyfch time
the Australian Foreign-trade press is trying to
assure itself that the United States is tired of Pro-
tection, and that the Low-Tariff party is going to
triumph at the coming Presidential election. This
cheering over the dead horse grows pathetic. For
about 30 years the same papers saw the great
Foreign-trade reaction coming at every Victorian
election'. At two Federal elections they have
seen it approaching, without any possible doubt.
They have noticed it arriving to a dead certainty
in Maoriland, Queensland, and S.A. They have
observed .the obvious revolt of Germany against
Protection ;•■ ditto France; ditto Austria; ditto
almost every other place. And the only thing
that really comes is the British revolt against
Foreign-trade, and the steady decline of the im-
porting gospel in N.S.W., and the bursting of the
Cobden myth everywhere.
In view of the vicious remarks made so
often by the Sydney dailies regarding Victorian
protected industries, it is interesting to find that
the N.S.W. workman is not fooled by them. As
witness the recent testimonial sent by the Sydney
wire-netting workers to Jabez Coon, member for
Batman (Vic.), for his efforts to get higher Pro-
tection for wire-netting—a big Sydney industry :
We wish to express our thanks for the interest you have
shown in the N.S.W. wire-netting industry, and the
endeavors you have made to get Parliament to protect
the Australian product against the competition of that
made with cheap foreign labor.
Coon fought hard for a decent duty, but the
N.S.W. Foreign-trade votes —Cook, Kelly, Reid
and Thomson amongst them—were too many for
him. And the Smellygraph has the impudence
to denounce Victorian Protectionists for their in-
difference to N.S.W. interests.!
They simply boiled the billy for the Inter-
State Labor Conference dinner in Brisbane—there
was no stronger drink on the premises. The
Labor agitator used to be represented with a pipe
in his mouth, a leer on his face, and a bottle in his
pocket; but they have had to leave out the bottle
at last. When they picture the Labor Socialist
setting out to break up the ’ome and disrupt the
marriage tie, they have to assume nowadays that
he gets infuriated on ginger-beer or lemon-squash.
“J.P.”: Cleveland, ex-president of the
U.S.A., who has just gone across the border, was
the man who tried to run “the greatest kuntry
on airth” in much the same way as Freetrade
Reid, and others of his ilk, would run Australia.
A few years back I asked an American what he
thought of Cleveland. The Yank spat viciously,
and, in a twang that smote the stars, he observed :
“ I guess, stranger, if thet gol-derned maniac
with his no-tariff loonacy hed uv run fer another
turm th’States ud iv been in charge uv th’ Sheriff,
an’ ther’ wouldn’t ’a’ ben a denied factory left in
th’ kuntry, Blarst him ! ” Cleveland was evi-
dently not a man beloved of the people.
There is trouble over the estate which
John Robb, the magnificent, left behind him ;
and the family has started to peck at one another
in an expensive way in the Victorian courts.
John, in the course of his railway contracting, had
a good share of the big job of punching the inter-
state railway through the hills at the back of
Adelaide. While he was busy at that, his
daughter, reckoned the most beautiful woman o r
the day in S. Aus., married “Charley” Muir-
head, who was the province’s beauty man.
“ Charley ” is a solicitor (his last job of import-
ance was to defend “Joe” Coleman, who was
hanged the other day), and it is against the
ex-beauty man that most of the “other side’s”
shafts will be hurled. For a start, the trustees
tell the Court in an affidavit that “plaintiff’s
husband” (Mrs. Muirhead is the plaintiff) “is
freely using this suit for financial purposes,
alleging that plaintiff is entitled to a sum of
£30,000, being one seventh of an alleged fund of
£2l0 n OOO of which defendants (the trustees) are in
complete ignorance.” There is “law” on both
sides, so the old contractor is likely to have his 1
bones greatly raked and pawed over.

Bent has struck the commendable idea of
introducing Australian marble into Victoria’s new
London offices, and has cabled the contractor an
offer of 60 tons of Toungabbie stone. Proposal is
to polish the stuff locally, and land it at £6 per
ton free in London, where, properly displayed, it
would prove a splendid advertisement for Aus-
tralia’s little-known marble resources.
