The other day Adelaide lost a man who
was one of the world’s boss authorities on some-
thing, even if the som '■thing was only the shade-
house cyclamen. Georg > Laughton was the boss
authority: he divided p his life between skins
and hides and the gract ul bloom, and gave the
bloom all his last few years. In his shade-houses
he had 600 plants, of almost every tint and color ;
but the king of the lot was a tremendously-photo-
graphed ancient of 46 years, claimed to be the
patriarch of all cyclamens—the last time he was
pictured he was bearing 295 blooms.
Like many another municipal body, the
Moorabbin (Vic.) Shire Councils burdened with
the incubus of its own foolishness. Some years
ago it leased to the usual shindykit one of its
main roads along the Port Phillip foreshore
between Sandringham and Mordialloc. The said
shindykit thereupon laid down a single line horse-
tramway—the usual hilarious concern that ought
to be arrested for disorderly conduct. The tram,
however, is still running—or rather ambling—
down in those parts; and the council, practically
right up against Bent’s new and excellent electric
service between Brighton and St. Kilda, can’t help
but make comparisons. Consequently it makes
no secret of its desire to remove its own
ugly animal, but doesn’t know how to do it
pleasantly because it has just recently renewed
the lease. So, for the present, it contents itself
with bitterly reproaching the tram co. for the way
it keeps the road, and has, moreover, coldly in-
formed the mortgagee of its action. It seems
there is a mortgagee, and has been for quite a long
while now.
“ The Big Five,” Ambrose Pratt’s stirring
story of the Northern Territory, nears a conclusion
in The Lone Hand. The chapters dealing with
the fight with the Asiatic colony in the Territory
(July No.) have a fine thrill.*
The M.L. cleric, with the mind that
clamors aloud for f umigation, has been at it again.
Rev. R. McNaughten told the Napier Magis-
trates Court, the other day, that he considered
Gervais’ picture, “La Folie de Titania” (it is a
fanciful painting of fairies, sprites and gnomes,
illustrating a scene from “A Midsummer Night’s
Dream”) was “indecent,” and unfit for the public
eye. Bernard Hall, Melbourne’s ice-cold art
authority, should—according to McNaughten—be
“ashamed of himself for having painted ‘Sleep.’ ”
The Rev. Canon Tuke then stormed the witness-
stand, adjusted his halo, and gave it as his view
that “La Folie de Titania” and other pictures of
its sort were “calculated to play evilly upon the
passions,” and were “suggestively indecent.”
Tuke remarked that “whenever he noticed pic-
tures of the nude in art galleries, and had young
people with him, he got them away as quickly as he
could.” Normal citizens, noting the unpleasant
state of these people’s intellects, begin to wonder
whether they ought to be allowed in art-galleries
at all.
The N.S.W. Parliament re-opened for
business on Tuesday, and the Governor’s speech
contained, among other things, the following
The rights guaranteed to New South Wales under the
Federal Constitution with respect to the capital site are,
THROUGH CIRCUMSTANCES OVER WHICH THIS STATE HAS HAD
NO control, still withheld ; but the hope is entertained
that, in view of the assurances given, a final settlement
of the question will be arrived at during the next ensuing
session of the Commonwealth Parliament.
The Australian Parliament did its part of the
work by selecting a capital site which not only
conformed in every way to the letter of the Con-
stitution, but which was also in the locality
specially recommended as the best by the officer
whom the N.S.W. State Government appointed
to report on possible sites. Then things stuck
fast, because a new N.S.W. Government had come
in, and it repudiated the acts of its predecessor
just as it might have repudiated its predecessor’s
bonds, and the new Government was savagely anti-
Australian, and apparently regarded it as its chief
mission in life to insult the Australian Parliament
on every possible occasion. Seeing that everybody
knows that it is the Oarruthers and Wade Govern-
ments alone which have prevented the establish-
ment of a capital, and seeing that the right to have
the capital in N.S.W. hasn’t been “withheld,” for
it was granted four years ago, it would have been
a lot more decent to have left that outrageous
statement out of the Governor’s speech.
Bent has a bill before the Victorian
Assembly which tries to do good by a roundabout
road. It provides that in future all persons
desiring to commit matrimony must give the
parson three days’ notice of their intention.
Should this regulation be fractured the marriage
will still be legal, but the parson will be liable to
a £2O penalty. Any minister who, in the Chief
Secretary’s opinion, makes a business of cele-
brating marriages instead of carrying out the
“ ordinary duties ” of a minister, may be struck
off the roll altogether ; and the head of each de-
nomination is required to send to the Government
Statist, every January, a complete list of all
ministers of his faith who are in the regular soul-
saving business, so that the Chief Secretary may
the better keep the list free from the professional
marrier. Yet, instead of going to all this trouble,
Bent might have done the thing much uiore
effectively by providing that the legal marriage
must be performed by a State official. After the
State official has done his best or his worst, the
parties might be at liberty to employ any parson
they liked, or no parson at all. In that case, if a
man believed that the only true sect is one that
has only two adherents —himself and the pastor —
he would be at liberty to give that pastor a
job. Under Bent’s scheme the chances are
that that pastor won’t get a license. Yet
the only True Church may be one whose
parson only gets lid. on the plate and that sort
of parson has to do something to earn the rest of a
living, even though that something isn’t connected
with the regular soul-saving business.


Melbourne Anti-Sweating League recently
commented on the scarcity of State land available
for settlement in Victoria, and the stricture
prodded Bent on the raw. Yapped Tommy :
I wish that the man who made the statement, and
others like him, would put forward some suggestion about
settling people on the land. There is too much yapping.
Let the critics of the Government point out how more
land can be made available.
Well, -here goes for a suggestion: Supposing
Tommy tries what a heavy graduated land-tax
will do towards bursting up some of those big
estates that Tommy knows of ; and thus ascer-
tains whether “ more land can be made available ”
that way. It isn’t a new suggestion, still
In the light of Postal Secretary Scott’s
evidence, before the Postal Commission, the
chronic “Federal Postal Muddle ’’ howl becomes
interesting:—
Has the discredited system arisen since Federation ?
It arose before Federation. Since Federation the De-
partment has not been given sufficient money to replace
the obsolete appliances and methods handed over to it
when the Commonwealth was established.
Then the service was in a rotten condition when it was
handed over ?
Yes, I think it was.
The witness said, in answer to questions, that some
complaints against postal administration were un-
doubtedly prompted by hostility towards Federation, a
desire to discredit the Federation by discrediting a
Department which it managed. ;

"Myself and two little children were
covered with a red pimply rash, and it
was so itchy. I had tried every oint-
ment it was possible to get. Until I
tried the Cuticura Ointment, it was the
first relief we got. I was afraid to go
near a doctor for fear of him making
a fuss, as of course I knew it must be
infectious, for I took it first, then one
little one, and then the other, and finally,
my husband. I used about twelve or
thirteen boxes Cuticura Ointment, but
that wasn’t much considering all I had
to do with it. My own arms and be-
tween my fingers, on front of shins and
between my toes, were completely cov-
ered with an ugly, itchy rash, and my
youngest little girl was covered from
crown to sole. I thought she would
have died. She was only a year and
two months at the time. My eldest
wasn’t so bad, but bad enough. Thanks
to the Cuticura Ointment, we are com-
pletely cured, and I shall recommend
the Cuticura Remedies to my friends.
Mrs. D. M. Grieve, Donaghmore Co.,
Tyrone, Feb. 12 and Feb. 19, 1906.”

The Inky Way.
Indeed and indeed the “ Australian Short Story ” of the
average British weekly is a wild farrago of calamitous
rot, and ought to be slain at birth. In a recent issue of
Puck, a new London publication, a “ typical young Aus-
tralian ” is represented paying a call, in the Old Country,
on a Bart. whom he has met “in the colonies.” Illustra-
tions show him a splendid being in white duck riding-
breeks, spotless shirt, shiny black top boots, and immense
spurs, Spanish sombrero shading his brow, and
countless knives, bowies, daggers, pistols, revolvers,
derringers, etc., at his waist, galloping furiously along a
country road with about 180 ft. of stock-whip encircling
his head. Says the author :
“ The horseman, Stock Ryder by name, was fresh from
Australia, and looked somewhat out of place between
green English hedges. He still wore the everyday dress
of a Queensland stockman.”
The Bart, arrives, whereupon :
“ Whip in hand the Australian horseman plunged down
the lane, and the whip-lash went off in a volley of start-
ling cracks in front of a motor which contained young
Sir Edward Loscoe and his chauffeur, who wore goggles.
“ ‘ Bail up, Teddy ! ’
“Judged by the style he stopped the motor, Stock Ryder
might have been Ned Kelly, the bushranger. The young
baronet had just lighted a cigar, and there was a neat rim
of ash upon it. The driver jammed the brakes on and
wrenched at the wheel. The car stopped with a jerk,
and the reckless horseman yelled :
“ ‘ Good old Teddy. I’m collecting cigar ash, and I’ve
got yours.’
“ The whip-lash flashed through the air in a sudden
streak, and a little puff of ash from the cigar-end flew on
to the baronet’s hat, but the cigar itself was not even
shaken in his mouth.”
The Australian feels he would like to make Sir Edward
a present:—
“ At 10 yards distance Stock Ryder drew something from
a saddle-bag. It was a nugget of solid gold big as a
cricket ball.
“ Flinging up his arm, Stock Ryder threw the nugget
across to the young baronet, and, rising in the motor-car,
Sir Edward caught it neatly.
“ ‘ Got it, Teddy ; you always were a good catch, and a
knock-out rider. How a man who’s ridden for the Mel-
bourne Stakes as a gentleman jockey could come down to
a tin-can motor beats me.’”
The chauffeur, maddened by the gold-lust, essays to
steal the nugget thus donated :
“ The gleaming revolver of the mechanician was
levelled straight at the baronet’s head a. Stock Ryder
stood forward with his stockwhip uplifted. The whip,
flashed through the air, and the long leather lash curled
like a serpent around Nat Slack’s wrist, forcing it up, and
at the same time discharging the revolver overhead.
Rvder’shand gave a twist to the lash, and the revolver
flew high up in the air above Nat Slack’s head. The next
instant the lash curled round Slack’s legs, and he was
pitched backwards to the ground, as if he had been
thrown by a wrestler.
“Two other men, accomplices of the chauffeur, broke
through the brushwood, armed with sticks.
“ ‘ Leave them to me, Teddy,’ Stock Ryder cried.
“ He slashed out with his whip, and served them the
same as he had done Nat Slack, roping them together
with the lash and bringing them to earth in a heap.”
j Among the semi-educated of Britain, grotesques of the
Stock Ryder breed are trustfully accepted as “ represent-
ative Australian citizens.”

“ Jean Sibi ”: The Twilight News of my township is an
ino’enious organ. Somewhere buried in its office is a
paragraphical genius, and every day this god has to bury an
atom of information in a w'hole column of pars ; also every
day the purchaser of the rag has to fish for the atom. For
instance, the boss reporter is told off to report the trial
of one Giuseppe Maraschino, A.B. of the barque Britannia,
accused of having stabbed a fellow seaman, Gustav
Pedersen, in the “ stummick.” Both appear in court
supported by their witnesses—a crowd of shellbacks
whose cleanliness, English and veracity might be beyond
reproach, but aren’t. The evidence is, strictly speaking,
commonplace; but is lightened by little sparks of un-
conscious drollery, mainly due to the witnesses’ rocky
conceptions of English. The boss reporter faithfully re-
cords every word, and, later, returns to the office, where
he spreads himself in a verbatim report over three columns.
Throughout that report are scattered sensational head-
lines emphasising the tiniest points in the narrative ; and
that should be the end of the matter in a reasonable news-
paper. But the boss reporter also runs the “Notes”
column and the “ On Dit” column, and he fills them up
in such fashion as this
“In a case for assault, held at the Port Police Court
to-day, one Giuseppe Maraschino was charged with
stabbing his 'shipmate, Gustav Pedersen, in the stomach.
Accused created much amusement by his persistent
declaration of ‘ Bosh-ta, me bosh-ta ! ’ ”
Then, two or three pars further down, something like
this will attract notice
“ ‘Basta! him drinka fooll up-a.’ was the description
of the state of the accused, given by a witness in a case
for assault at the Port Police Court to-day.”
Towards the end this will glitter :
“ The quaint, broken speech of several witnesses in the
Port Police Court to-day created much amusement on the
Bench. For full report see page 3.”
And further:
“An Italian seaman, by name Giuseppe Maraschino,
was sentenced to 17 years and 2 minutes at the Port
Police Court to-day, for scabbing one Gustav Pedersen in
the stomach.
And again : .
“Gustav Pedersen, who was stabbed in the stomach
(vide our report on page 3), is not, as stated previously, a
native of Trondhjem. He hails from Khamschatka.”
And even then the subject of Gustav Pedersen’s stomach
isn’t quite exhausted.
•* * * *
“ Y.” : One of the sights in the Australian newspaper
world is Kunnel Reay and his aide, or major or deputy-
quartermaster-general, constructing a Melbourne Herald
“Leading Note.” Reay selects most of the topics,
and dictates his ideas of what the paper should say
without any danger of rushing headlong into rash
statements. The adjutant makes a memo, of the
Colonel’s views, and when the senior well of wisdom
begins to pump dust, he retires and reduces the stuff to
typewriting. But the real grief is the first proof of the
labored note when the Colonel gets through with it.
Often the foreman printer has to calculate whether it
would be quicker and cheaper to correct the thingorgive
it out as fresh copy. It is quite another note when the
final revise is done—as a general rule. Erudite, dignified,
logical, and models of grammar and composition as Mel-
bourne Herald “Leading Notes ” always are, it is doubt-
ful if they are worth quite all the worry they cost.
THE MONEY BOWL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the legend poets troll
Of One on some first-floor
Who keeps a brimming money-bowli
Beside his office door.
And ev’ry morn the office waif —
A small, old-fashioned boy—
Doth fill it gaily from the safe
With jingling, golden joy.
Then, as the day draws slowly on,
The bards creep up the stair
And feast their hollow eyes upon
The treasure gleaming there.
A tattered sign that swings above
The bowl gives all to know,
“ Take what you need, with my best Jove t
Don’t count; to count is low.”
So, stooping to the calabash,
Each bard drops in a tear,
And takes a handful of the cash
That buys the soothing beer.
They steal like ghosts adovvn the stair,
They creep like spectres up ;
And gradually pubwards bear
The Treasure of the Cup.
Next morn the office waif appears
To sweep with might and main ;
He brushes up the poets’ tears,
And fills the bowl again.
This is the legend poets troll
Of some good editor
Who keeps a brimming money bowl
Beside his office door.

It was dreadfully foolish for the Victorian
agricultural implement manufacturers to revert
to the old scale of wages paid in the anti-tariff
days, which they did as soon as the High
Court’s decision was delivered. Some reduced
the rates without even the usual week’s notice.
Apparently these stupid individuals imagine
that the whole advantage of a Protective
tariff is to accrue to them—the worker is not to
share in it at all. It is folly such as this which
lies at the bottom of the old Laborite demand to
“sink the fiscal issue.” The difference, however,
between the two policies is just this. Without
Protection the worker won’t have even the chance
of getting high wages ; with Protection he has the
chance of getting high wages, though in such cases
as this he will have to struggle for his share.
Without Protection all the struggling in the world
wouldn’t help him, because there isn’t in that case
anything to share.
‘ ‘ Branaraba’s ” musings
Carious how the politics of the Darling- Downs (Q.)
agriculturist have changed of late. Fifteen years ago the
Downs voted Labor to a man. Then they became Mor-
ganite and later on Kidstonite. At the last State elec-
tion, the Wages Board Bill being in sight, they nearly all
went the whole Tory hog, and voted Philp. 'Before* the
election Kidston held all the Darling Downs seats (four),
but at the election he lost three (Warwick, Cunningham’
and Aubigny), and it was only the purely Labor vote that
saved the fourth (Cambooya). As a matter of fact, the
Darling Downs was Philp’s stronghold. The explanation
is, of course, that the Downs dwellers have got down from
the barren ridges they inhabited of yore to the fertile flats
which used to be the exclusive preserve of the big
squatter, and are making their pile. So they want the
status quo maintained. They have got all the Socialism
they want for the moment, so they turn dog and vote the
Anti-Sosh ticket in the good old usual way. Their sup-
port goes to the party which tried to keep them on the
barren mountain-tops while the big land-grabber held
the rich plains and valleys, and the party which helped
them down from the haggard peaks and found them good
farm-lands in the vales gets nothing bdt curses these times.
Australian Mutual Provident Society.
Established 1549.
For Life Assurance on the Mutual Principle.

BRONCHITIS and PNEUMONIA
Cured by
HEARNE’S BRONCHITIS CURE
After other Treatment had Failed.
Kr. W. G. Hearne. Dear Sir,- —From a strict sense
of duty I feel that I should publish the following state-
ment, so that others may know what to do when the
awful fact is evident that a life is in danger of being
lost:—ln September, 1906, my little girl, aged at that
time 3 years, contracted Measles, and in the following
October was attacked by Bronchitis, Pneumonia and
Congestion of the Lungs. She was attended by a
legally qualified doctor of high standing, but his treat-
ment was not successful in arresting the progress of the
illness. On Saturday, the 21st October, 1906, he said
that her life was in danger—that there was very little
hope for her. For eight days and nights she had been
prostrated by Cough, Pain and Fever, and was lying
like a statue, unconscious. At this stagtf I was per-
suaded by a friend to obtain Hearne’s Brohchitis Cure,
with its auxiliary medicine for the Fever and Conges-
tion of the Lungs, as directed in the Catalogue of
Medicines which , accompanies each bottle of the Bron-
chitis Cure. I gave the medicine as directed, and there
was an improvement from the first dose of Hearne’s
Bronchitis Cure. The improvement continued after
each dose of the medicine. In a week she was perfectly
free from the Pneumonia, Congestion, Cough, Pain and
Fever, and was well, except that she was still weak. In
a fortnight she was quite recovered, and is now in
splendid health, and stronger than ever. Any person
asking for information about this grand medicine can
be supplied by me, or by any of my neighbours who have
witnessed its wonderful effects. It absolutely snatched
my child from an early grave.—Yours gratefully,
D. GARDINER,
Police Station, Geelmng East, Feb. 5, 1908.

.I> Prudence M'Kee, of Carr-street, South Geelong, In

follows:
My son, Henry M'Kee, then aged 8 years, had been

him to be suffering from Pneumonia, Pleurisy and a
stoppage of the passing of Urine. Under the doctor’s
treatment the child gradually got worse, and the doctor
pronounced the case hopeless. He told me that the
child could not live. At _ this stage I obtained from
Mr. W. G. Hearne, Chemist, of Geelong, a bottle of

lots of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure,
for which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR,

BRONCHITIS and PLEURISY.
A Bevere Case Cured By Two Bottles of
Hearne's Bronchitis Cure.
After Other Treatment had Failed.
Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong.
Dear Sir, —Some months ago, in Sydney, I suffered
from a severe attack of inflvfenza, and was confined to
my room for about a week, at the end of which time,
feeling somewhat better, I got up and tried to transact
my business as usual. But I got up too soon, for the
very next day I had a relapse, and suTtred tortures from
what the doctor told me was pleurisy and bronchitis.
The pain from the former in my chest and shoulders
was frightful, and for four long weeks I was confined to
my bed under the care of a well-known Sydney doctor,
and all the time his medicine gave me but temporary
relief. The landlady of the hotel (the Cleveland), where
I resided, told me of a medicine—Hearne’s Bronchitis
Cure—from Victoria, which had cured her of a bad at-
tack of bronchitis and pains in the chest, and begged
of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks and gratitude
to you, tell you that, after the second bottle, my cough
had ceased; but what is more astonishing, the pains from
pleurisy entirely left me, and in about a week I was able
to attend to my duties as usual.—Yours faithfully,
~ „ „ „ .. J. BRAHAM.
Melbourne ‘ Punch ’ Office, Melbourne.

The Standing Army.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HAVE you seen the Out-at-Elbows on
parade—
Seen the chronic ragged-seated regulars.
In their uniform of patches, when they rendezvous
in batches,
Cadging beers and smokes and matches at the
Bars ?
At the Bars ! Bars! Bars ! Open Bars !
(Fall in ! )
When they skirmish round for fluid at the Bars !
Though they never send recruiting sergeants out,
They’re an army that grows stronger with the
years.
Though their aspect uninviting may repel, it is
exciting
Just to stand and watch them biting ears for
beers—
Ears for Beers ! Beers ! Beers ! Gratis Beers !
(Eyes front !)
Ears for long and cooling, thirst-allaying Beers !
By the beer-stains on their whiskers are they
known !
By the foozled, super-foozled way they blink !
And their wretched wives go charing while those
reckless souls are marring
Health and prestige, gamely sparring after
drink !
After Drink ! Drink ! Drink ! Lengthy Drink !
( Quick march 1)
After pots that hold full pints of precious drink !
Some were born to bite the pewter; some were
not.
There are derelicts of Fashion in their ranks ;
But they’re all upon a level, drifting on with
every revel,
Nearer, nearer to the Devil! They are Tanks !
Merely Tanks! Tanks ! Tanks ! Human Tanks !
(Breast up !)
“Death or Moisture” is the motto of the Tanks !
MAORILAND.
SINC S. CLARK.
A Debacle,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ON a corner allotment, swept by a
bitter wind, stood the survivor of a forest
of land agents’ boards, and on it flapped
loosely a sign which announced in straggling
characters, “ Great Rally: Testemoneys, To
Night.” Grouped in a small circle round the post
was the great rally. It consisted of half-a-dozen
men, whose fierce eyes mocked the feebleness of
their features and physique, and several objects
which appeared in the torchlight like small, gabled
dove-cots on posts, but which closer inspection
proved to be coal-scuttle-bonneted Amazons of
the “Army.” Some of the men wore robin-
breasted uniforms, some were in mufti, but one
and all bore the metal S of active service, even to
the armor-bearers and camp-followers of the
detachment.
The enemy was mostly resting on its base at
that stronghold of sin, the Royal Hotel—the pub
obliquely opposite. Yet its vedettes were within
range, fully eleven of them, tempted from cover
by a rumor that an erstwhile “pal” who had
“gone over” and become a “comrade’’was to
give the worldly show away.
One by one the members of the little band
related to the wind their tedious, twice-told tales
of the terrible time when they feared not God
neither regarded man—or, for the women’s part,
regarded man too much. And the fervor broke
out at the usual pauses, deepening in intensity
when an extra deep probing brought to light a
sin “ not elsewhere included.” And each suc-
ceeding testifier prefaced his depositions by
remarking with proud sadness, “Ah, fr'en’s, I’ve
bin a grite sinner. I’ve bin even worse ’n youse.”
Then the raw recruit fell clumsily into line.
“Fren’s,”he said, “it takes more courige some-
times ter surrender than ter stand out. I've stood
out often, ’n’ I’ve fell often, slain—slain by sin; but
this time I’ve got up again, ’n’ I’ve got up on ther
other side of ther line.” (Slow music: “Over
the line it’s on’y a step.”) “’N’ I’m goin’ ter
kick orf me truck ’n’ unload me orful parst.
Fren’s” (as his Dutch courage rose), “some of
youse bin bad coves, but my sins ’as bin as scarlet
—scarlet with crimson stripes ’n’ delirium trim-
mins. I was alius bad. I was born bad. Before I
wasone luseter chuck menuss-girl under thechinas
I lay on’er knee, ’n’ say ‘goo’ to’er. ’N’ while me'
mother was comparin’ me with neighbors’ nippers,
I useter shake their dummies ’n’ their rattles.
Soon after I was weaned I stunned me mother
with me feedin’-bottle because she never put no
sugar in me milk. As I grew older I on’y grew
worse; me thoughtless prime was early ripe to
ill. I passed from folly into crime, ’n’ me parents
pleaded ‘no control ’ when I was five. When I
broke away from the schools, on me sixth birth-
day, ’n’ went ’ome, me father and mother cleared
out because they couldn’t, stand me drunken
’abits, ’n’ the way I roughed ’em up. When me
father ’n’ mother forsoke me, the perlice took me
up ; ’least, they did orf and on, but I was too ’ot
to ’old, I was. I was a ches’nut fair orf the ’ob.
When I turned round er corner one d’y, ’n’ come
right onter two pleecemen, they both dropped
dead, ’n’ the verdick was ’arc failure. I slowed
up a bit when I got a job. I woulden’ er took it
on’y it give me the ’andlin’ o’ cash. But em-
bezzlement ’n’ petty larceny got too slow fer me.
‘ Satin finds some mischief still fer idle ’ands ter
do,’ ’n’ I started a bit o’ garrotin’ in me spare
time. Bein’ noo at the gime, a good many o’ me
victims died at first; but I never felt no remorse,
although I useter git ’eadaches thinkin’ out noo
crimes. But now the harrer of conviction ’as sunk
deep into me soul. I realise I bin too frisky.
I’m goin’ to break off me ole bad ’abits, ’n’ instead
o’ the ’and of the lor, I wanter feel on me shoulder
ther steadyin’ ’and ’n’ look around ter catch ther
lovin’ smile er Jesus.”
With a sudden suspicion of something missing,
the speaker paused, but vnot even a groaned
“Amen ” or “ Glory Be ” ch'eeted him to continue.
For a broken, dispirited band had dejectedly dis-
persed into the darkness, and the only sound was
the wind-borne echo of stolid, sullenly-retreating
footsteps upon the sleeping streets.
But down upon his head fluttered the sign, like
the robe of the praying Pharisee descending upon
a worthy successor, or the winding-sheet of
Ananias enfolding a lovely liar who could shame
even a Salvation Army squad into silence.
WUSSLEAN.
A Tertium Quid.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE was once a man who loved his
wife, and lived with her for many years in
such content as comes to those who are
truly mated. But troubles fell upon him—bur-
dens grievous to be borne, yet making little show;
and he began to falter as he went. “Unto him
that hath shall be given,” says the Scripture, and
so, to crown his dismay, he suddenly found him-
self possessed of a devil.
It came to him at the hottest hour of a dead
and breathless summer’s day, when his work was
heaviest and his vitality lowest; no horned and
tailed reminiscence of Pagan Pan, but something
mist-pale, shining with a thin phosphorescence,
grey as a man’s shadow in the water, with eyes
f«ll of inexorable malice, their rims reddened, not
from tears, but from the flame whose flicker woke
them to life.
The Man bore with this visitation as best he
might, assuring himself it was but the juggling of
an overtired brain. But the thing wore on him.
By clay he could neglect it; he grew used to
working with those red-rimmed eyes upon him—
but of evenings, as he sat book in hand, it rose
above the opened page and held his helpless gaze,
and at night it drew him from his bed to commune
with it under the black sky.
His wife saw him thus ill at ease, though he hid
it all he could. She desired exceedingly to ask
him the occasion of his trouble; but, being not
only wife, but also true companion and friend, she
waited in silence and made as though she saw
nothing.
For a long time this went on, till one night she
heard him mutter in his sleep of eyes, eyes, and a
devil, and so guessed him to be haunted by some
fearful and relentless presence.
The Man withdrew into himself more and more.
His great and abiding fear was lest his wife, whom
he loved, should learn his trouble, and so suffer
for him, or come to share it, and so suffer with
him. When the devil became too insistent,
therefore, he would away out of doors, walking
back and forth with hurried step while he fought
for the mastery of his mind, that, like a mad-
dened horse in the grip of some strange terror,
made shuddering, frantic struggles to get loose.
So he walked one evening under the climbing
roses whose fragrance the night dews called forth,
and all the way as he went the devil walked
beside him, near—nearer—in him, perchance : —
God knew which. Then his wife, after calling
from the window, came out to him; but he,
absorbed in that fierce conflict, at handgrips with
that evil power, heard her not till she was close on
him.
“ Back ! keep back ! ” he cried, and the sweat
broke out on his face. “ Never touch me, or you
will see it, too ! ”
Then the wife knew her time had come. She
stepped forward fearless, and clasped hp damp
and shaking hand in hers. She had thought and
thought over this moment, how she would see
what he saw, through his mind, and know it to be
a thing of fancy ; how she would smile in its face,
mock it, and so lead him she loved away from it
for ever.
But now, when she looked into its eyes of
flickering flame—lo, she was afraid. He felt her
tremble.
“You see it? You fear it?” he cried. No
chance had she to lie—her face spoke.
“I see it! I fear it!” she quavered. “Oh,
hold me close, Beloved, lest it come between us.”
Then they walked back and forth together, the
Thing beside them, near them, in them—God
knows which—and he told all he had suffered so
far as words might tell, and what he could not
tell she knew, for at every step her heart within
her failed for fear, and she went with him all his
weary way.
So the days and years passed on over that
haunted pair, and the shadows darkened about
them. Yet through it all, they knew touches of
joy together,, moments of half-forgetting, fore-
shadowings of the peace they looked for. when this
lifelong trouble should be ended. Whereas when
the man watched alone he seemed cut off from all
hope and rest, now they could ease their hearts
by speaking together of their relentless visitant.
When she saw him blench and lift a frozen stare
she would come close, and put her face against
his, declaring she had no fear of that ugly horror ;
it had never hurt them, nor ever could, and so
win him back from that dreadful quietude to
common kindly talk. When he saw her features
distorted with a sudden pang, her hands wrung
together in agony, he would gather her into his
arms and speak comfortably to her till her strained
muscles relaxed and the tears came. Once her
heart failed her for a moment. “ Shall we never
be free ? ” she wailed, as they lay handfasted one
starless night, while from the darkness looked out
on them those red-rimmed eyes lit with flame that
was to them as tears.
“Never,” said he ; and then, groaning, “Why
have I, who love you, brought you to this?”
Then she turned and gripped him fiercely with
her arms.
“Sooner this,” she whispered from dry lips—
“ sooner this than all Heaven without yo'u, ” and
after a while they slept.
There came a time at last when the man was
permitted to lay down the heavy burden of his
life. He had but little to say as he drew near
that desired end ; he lay for the most part still,
with fast shut eyes, for his enemy had no mind to
quit him till the very last. Words were not
needed between him and his wife ; they had shel-
tered- together too long under the terror that
walketh in darkness and wasteth at noonday to
have much left unsaid.
Yet she would sit for hours patiently, and so
hungrily waiting for the languid look, the slow
smile, the faintly breathed word; and ever with
them waited the devil that was their portion
in this world.
At dawn of his last day she rose to put back the
curtain. The wan light that comes before the
day fell across his face, and from the midst of it
hot eyes glowed and flickered. He turned his
sunken gaze upon her ; then, with a sudden
startling quickness, glanced to left, to right, before
him.
“Gone!” he panted, for his breath began to
fail. “ Free at last! ” he sobbed, and stretched his
arms towards her. Before her lips reached his
lie was past all knowing.
She knelt beside him, and watched his worn
face settle to an utter quiet; then she lifted a tear-
less look on the mist-thin Presence beside her.
“You think to daunt me now?” she smiled,
“ It was for him, for him I feared,” and laid her
face to his.
When she raised it she was alone. The earliest
glow of sunrise flooded the silent room, emptied
now alike of man and devil, and to the mind of
the woman came a sudden sense of desolation most
strange, most piteous.
A. WISHAW.

Of the Delight in Bad Plays.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DRAMAS that are just bad are every-
night occurrences. The perfectly bad
drama is comparatively rare, but when
found it is worth all the trouble the discovery
may entail, and connoisseurs carry the news to
each other with enthusiasm not exceeded by
Egyptologists at the finding of a new tomb or an
unheard-of Pharaoh. A perfectly bad drama is a
classic in its way, and it is to be part of the office
of my projected Society for the Cultivation of Bad
Plays t.o collect and preserve such dramas, with
the idea of establishing a unique library.
Another object of the Society will be to rescue
from oblivion those authors whose bad plays are,
in the opinion of the committee of experts, found
to be possessed of all the essentials of artistic
badness, and reward them with a benevolent
infamy.
The defects necessary in a really bad play are
very numerous, and the collecting and compress-
ing of them all into four or five acts, and the fix-
ing of appropriate places for them, implies in the
author at least that capacity" which is manifested
by a genius for taking pains. In fact, the rules
for the making of perfectly bad plays are so hard
and fast, the materials so rigorously prescribed,
that the mere dilettante student might jump to
the conclusion that it is within the power of any
bad playwright to make a play that will be artisti-
cally and intrinsically bad just when and yvhere he
pleases. But this is not so ; otherwise, plays de-
serving to rank as classic for their badness would
be quite common. Your shockingly bad dramatist
has his moments of inverse inspiration, when his
genius assumes a sort of ecstasy of imbecility. It
is at such moments that the exquisitely bad play
is produced.
No caricature of man and his customs, no bur-
lesque of civilisation that I know of can approach
the exquisitely bad play for humor and vivid
satire. You get these qualities in a less degree in
the merely bad play, but in the bad play that
attains to classical depravity they are present in
perfection. What better setting for satire or
travesty could you have than the great theatre
flooded with its dim, religious light, the audience
filling every part, grave, rigid, accepting the farce
with the ingenuous confidence of babes; the
orchestra, never doubting, sedulously blending
appropriate music with the emotions rioting on
the boards; and then the actors, in many respects
closely resembling men and women, in many others
so deliciously impossible, posturing and babbling,
with a solemn respect for themselves and for the
inspired balderdash of the author—that is the
quintessence of comedy!
Consider the hero, the ideal, so exquisite in
person, so lofty in purpose ! He is the noblest
work of God. With what is great and good in
the human race the author thinks to endow him,
and, as ne plus ultra , the audience accepts him.
With all these perfections thick upon him, he
plunges into follies that are the distinguishing
characteristics of the village idiot. But let him
demonstrate convincingly in test after test that
his mental qualities would earn him the
contempt of a community of African pig-
mies, let him be inane, weak, a blather-
skite, a coward, a currish husband, and a bad
father, not one jot of his imagined magnificence
falls from him. His intelligence, his heroism, his
courage, his amiability cling to him still. He is
the trusted hero to the end, when, in an amazing
flash of insight, he suddenly discovers divers
simple facts that have been grinding into his skull
through five acts, and exclaiming, “Ha, by
heavens, I see it all! ” he forgives the heroine the
wrongs he has inflicted upon her, and retires to
enjoy unmerited happiness and prosperity in the
bosom of a large family wholly his own.
The villain is even more grotesquely imagined
and portrayed. He is the sublimity of farce.
Ostensibly he is the very Devil, becomingly dark,
utterly unscrupulous, fiendishly cunning, capable
of any crime that will serve his ends. Yet he is
actually a paltry poltroon whose criminal instincts
would not win respect for a dog-stealer. Although
he is accepted as a daring ruffian, ready to break
houses and murder his own parents for the meanest
motives, the slim heroine has only to strike a stern
attitude, point a rigid finger, and cry : “ Stand
back, Gerald Armitage ! you shall not harm my
child!” and he cowers three paces down stage,
and skulks out of the limelight a crushed and
beaten man.
The pale heroine is perhaps the most bewitch-
ing feature in the perfectly bad play. Her stupidity
is without fleck or flaw. The unerring instinct
with which she does the wrong thing clamors
for the intervention of the Master-in-Lunacy.
But the audience respects and loves her. It is
blind to her crass foolishness, and oblivious to the
fact that she ignores the simple and easy ways out
of her most trying situations with a fatuity that
would be irritating in a blind puppy. She trusts
herself implicitly to the villain, when the smallest
grain of common-sense would satisfy her that she
would be safer in company with a crocodile. She
is idiotically dumb at moments when a word
would put everything right, and foolishly voluble
of explanations when explanations are no longer
called for. In real life, if not sent to an asylum,
she would be tied to the bed leg to keep her from
playing with the matches and biting the baby ; but
she is the heroine, and that fact consecrates all
her acts and scenes. The heroine can do no wrong.
The elderly parent usually dies early. He
adapts himself to being murdered in Act I. in a
most accommodating way, but while he survives
he lives at grotesque variance with his reputation
as an astute man of business. With two men to
choose from —a youth of flawless integrity and
an unspeakable villain —he invariably selects the
villain as his friend and confidant, while his mis-
trust of the hero, whose visible and glaring honesty
is almost a disease, and whom he loves with an
affecting tenderness, can only be excused as a
quaint form of monomania.
In this particular the comic servant is diamet-
rically opposed to the heavy parent. He knows
an honest man by instinct, and can identify a
villain as readily as a terrier sniffs out a rat.
Fortunately, however, he makes no particular use
of his gifts, otherwise the play could not possibly
go on. The liberties permitted to Court fools in
other times are extended to comic servants in bad
drama, and increased and multiplied. In point of
fact, the comic servant is the only really in-
fluential person in the play. The good characters
all defer to him ; the bad characters all fear him.
He it is who, when weary of trifling with human
lives, brings matters to a climax by confounding
the last device of the villain, and inducing the
assistant ruffian to exclaim : “Then I will tell all
I know !” and the sallow adventuress in a shabby
black cloak to confess everything that has been
plain to everybody for three acts past.
In the bad play the Law is always an ass, com-
mon-sense belongs to the fools, heroes are knaves,
and wisdom is sheer idiocy—in fact, topsy-
turvydom riots with an abandon that Gilbert did
not dare to approach in his travesties. Consider
its delicious “business.” When the villain,
having the heroine drugged, is about to destroy
her with a steam-hammer or a rolling-mill, he
prowls the stage, peering right and left (anything
to waste time), and hisses, “ Ha, ha ! the coast is
clear!’ while the comic servant, or the super-
numerary bad man, is staring him straight in the
face, so openly that the intruder can only be
ignored through a mental or optical derangement.
Again, when the wicked one, having the lady in
some inaccessible place, laughs a mirthless laugh
and sneers, his “ R’s ” in fine frenzy rolling : “ So,
my cold, proud beauty, you cannot escape me
now!” we know perfectly 7 well that that is the hero’s
cue to step through the brick wall, deal the air a
shocking blow that sends the villain sprawling,
and drape the heroine over his arm while the cur-
tain falls and the orchestra works up the general
excitement. There are scores of these familiar
situations, all screamingly funny to the student in
his right mind.
The delightful farcical quality of the truly bad
drama is attested by the fact that the play ceases
immediately the characters become commonly
rational. To finish his play, the author cracks his
finger and thumb, like the professional mesmerist,
cries “ Now you’re all right! ” and the whole fan-
tastical business is at an end. The men and
women wake up, blinking; they become sane
again ; they will no longer drink kerosene, eat
raw carrots, and carry themselves like asses. We
connoisseurs have enjoyed a feast of unreason,
but it is over. With fairly sensible men and
women to handle, the author is nonplussed. He
pairs his couples precipitately 7, and drops the cur-
tain on his fantastical world. It is a mad world,
my masters, but its madness makes for inex-
tinguishable mirth if you have eyes to see, and
ears to hear. May its creator live long to hold
his knotted mirror up to nature, and edify with
his reflections the distinguished members of the
Society for the Cultivation of Bad Plays !
SILAS SNELL.
Exceeding Small.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(~y WING round the motor cars to where
. \ The tall Australia stands —
V-/ The female bronzes light you there
To take the ladies’ hands—
And from your silver sovereign case
Slip out the careless gold —
The gay world, for a little space,
Is yours to have and hold.
Our home, it is a bonny place,
The hills and bush are near,
And picnic parties camp along
The water frontage here.
Our guardian is a kindly man,
To comfort and forgive—
For three of us are doomed to die,
And one is doomed to live.
There’s one of us—our hope and joy
Wlio’d thirty years of strife ;
He thinks he is a ‘‘naughty boy,”
And plays the part to life.
There’s one who cannot read nor write,
Yet studied ancient bards—
He seeks his fortune all day long
From endless rows of cards.
So “grind ” to win your high degrees
While runs the new world round,
And win the Chair / You’ll not be sunk
In wisdom more profound
Than some of us. We’re from all schools —
One’s an 31.J0. Alas 1 —
We bachelors, but not of Arts,
And widowers —of Dried Grass.
We’ve fought the merry fight where gaps
In firing lines grow wide
(And one of us is doomed to live
To tell how others died),
And one of us is doomed to grow
In gruesome terror old —
One, breathing, died two years ago,
And so the tale is told.
So prattle through your four o’clocks
And Thursday afternoons,
And never read the thing that shocks,
But toy with silver spoons,
And show your arms, so fair and white,
And coo and smile to please :
But I could write of things to-night
Woidd give you little ease.
We four can jest at little things,
We worry not at all;
We’re mortals whom the mills of God
Have ground exceeding small.
There’s one who’d, with his latest breath,
Keep black Depression out,
And he is doomed to die the death
We dare not write about.
There’s one who never lifts his eyes
And ne’er a word has said—
Our living dead lies still in bed
Till he is changed and fed.
There’s one on whom the Fear is set —
The jail presentiment;
Undrugged, he’s never rested yet,
And—he is innocent.
So lie abed, ye gentlemen,
And rest, ye ladies too.
While servants trained in voice and step
Bring breakfast in to you.
Then seek the Stock Exchange or Course,
The ball, or grand hotel —
There’s space for many and many a bed
In wards where we folk dwell.
The trained attendants glide about
To tend the “ paying guests ” ;
They drug the night with sleeping draughts,
We pass the day with jests—
Or brood or rave—or mouth and grin—
Or think and ply the pen—
For some of us are First, and some
Third Generation men.
Some pray all day who never prayed
And rise to no command
(Ah ! this, my children, is a song
That doctors understand).
We are the scapegoats of the world,
The wrongs the world shall rue;
We’re Freethinkers and Atheists
Who found the Bible true.
So speed your flying cars avid build
Your mansions strong and tall —
Be sure the mills of God will find
Avid grind exceeding small.
Though Man or Nation or the World
3lay dream and rave and doubt —
Ah God! the words are true: Be sure
Thy sins shall find thee out.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
